Definition of a Proposition and Its Parts
Defines the parts of a proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject, predicate, and relation in any Arabic proposition from a fiqh or kalam text.

Core Idea
A proposition is not just a subject and predicate; the relation is the glue that makes it truth-evaluable.

In this passage, the author gives the precise definition of a proposition (qadiyya) as the combination of two terms together with an affirmative or negative relation. This is foundational because all logical reasoning works with propositions, not isolated terms. Understanding the parts will help you analyse arguments in usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject (mawdu‘)
Find the term that is being talked about. In Arabic nominal sentences, it is the mubtada’; in verbal sentences, it is the fa‘il.
The subject may be explicit or implied; in logic it is always a single term.
2
Identify the predicate (mahmul)
Find the term that is affirmed or denied of the subject. In nominal sentences it is the khabar; in verbal sentences it is the verb.
The predicate can be a noun phrase or a verb phrase.
3
Identify the relation (nisba)
Determine whether the proposition is affirmative or negative. The relation is often indicated by the sentence structure and inflection, not necessarily a separate word.
If no negation particle is present, the relation is affirmative by default.
4
Test the definition
Take any complete sentence and check if you can separate the three components. If you can, it is a proposition; if not, it is not a logical proposition.
For example, 'Snow is frozen water' has subject (snow), predicate (frozen water), and affirmative relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲)
الموضوع والمحمول والنسبة بينهما
كل مفردين تجري بينهما نسبة موجبة أو سالبة فأحدهما موضوع والآخر محمول، ومجموعهما مع النسبة بينهما قضية.
والموضوع في تراكيب الجمل العربية يأتي مبتدأ أو نحوه إذا كانت الجملة جملة اسمية، ويأتي فاعلاً أو نحوه إذا كانت الجملة جملة فعلية، وأما المحمول فهو الركن الثاني من ركني الإسناد، وهو الخبر أو ما يقوم مقامه في الجملة الاسمية، والفعل أو ما يقوم مقامه في الجملة الفعلية، وأما النسبة بين الموضوع والمحمول فتدل عليها حركة الإعراب وهيئة تركيب الجملة، وقد يستعمل عند المناطقة للدلالة على النسبة ضمير الفصل وهو رابط غير زماني، أو فعل الكون وهو رابط زماني، كقولنا:
الثلج هو ماء متجمد، والإنسان كان نطفة من مني يمنى.
والأصل في الجملة أنها موجبة ولو لم يوجد فيها كلمة تدل على الإيجاب، فإذا استعمل فيها ما يدل على الإيجاب فهو تأكيد للمعنى الإيجابي الذي تحمله، فإذا أريد فيها معنى السلب فلا بد من استعمال أداة من أدوات السلب (أي النفي) للدلالة عليه، مثل: لا، ليس، غير، ما، لن، ونحوها.
أمثلة:
أ - الثلج ماء متجمد، هذا الكلام (قضية) وهي جملة اسمية، الموضوع فيها هو (الثلج)، وهو مبتدأ، والمحمول فيها (ماء متجمد) وهو خبر، والنسبة
(2)
The Subject, Predicate, and the Relation between Them
Any two single terms between which an affirmative or negative relation holds: one is the subject and the other is the predicate, and their combination together with the relation between them is a proposition.
The subject in Arabic sentence structures comes as a mubtada' or similar if the sentence is nominal, and comes as a fa'il or similar if the sentence is verbal. The predicate is the second pillar of the two pillars of predication; it is the khabar or what takes its place in the nominal sentence, and the verb or what takes its place in the verbal sentence. As for the relation between the subject and predicate, it is indicated by the inflectional vowel (i'rab) and the structure of the sentence. Logicians sometimes use the pronoun of separation (damir al-fasl), which is a non-temporal connector, or the verb of existence (fi'l al-kawn), which is a temporal connector, as in our saying:
'Snow is frozen water' and 'Man was a drop of seminal fluid.'
The default in a sentence is that it is affirmative, even if there is no word indicating affirmation. If something indicating affirmation is used, it is an emphasis of the affirmative meaning it carries. If a negative meaning is intended, then a tool of negation must be used to indicate it, such as: la, laysa, ghayr, ma, lan, and the like.
Examples:
A) 'Snow is frozen water'—this statement is a proposition. It is a nominal sentence. Its subject is 'snow', which is a mubtada', and its predicate is 'frozen water', which is a khabar, and the relation...
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage defines the three essential parts of a proposition (qadiyya). The author begins by stating that any two single terms (mufradān) connected by an affirmative or negative relation constitute a proposition: one term is the subject (mawḍū‘), the other the predicate (maḥmūl), and together with the relation they form the proposition. Then the author applies this to Arabic sentence structure: in a nominal sentence, the subject corresponds to the mubtada’ (topic) and the predicate to the khabar (comment); in a verbal sentence, the subject corresponds to the fā‘il (agent) and the predicate to the verb. The relation is expressed by the inflections (i‘rāb) and sentence structure, and logicians may use special connectors: the pronoun of separation (ḍamīr al-faṣl) like ‘huwa’ which is timeless, or the verb of existence (fi‘l al-kawn) like ‘kāna’ which carries a temporal sense. Examples: ‘al-thalju huwa mā’un mutajammidun’ and ‘al-insānu kāna nuṭfatan.’ The passage then explains that the default of a sentence is affirmative; if no explicit affirmative word is used, the sentence is still affirmative. Negation requires explicit negative particles. The example given is broken off but illustrates a simple affirmative proposition.

Key Terms
موضوع
محمول
نسبة
قضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the default of a sentence regarding affirmation or negation?
A) The default is that it is negative.
B) The default is that it is affirmative, even without an explicit affirmative word.
C) The default is that it is neither, until a word clarifies it.
D) The default is determined by the mood of the verb.

Key Takeaway
Every proposition consists of a subject, a predicate, and a relation; the relation is inherently affirmative unless negated explicitly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the Quranic statement: 'Allah is the Creator of everything' (Allahu khaliqu kulli shay'). Identify the subject, predicate, and relation. Explain how the relation is indicated.
?
Find a proposition in a fiqh manual (e.g., 'Prayer is obligatory') and analyse its three components.

Markers of the Relation: Damir al-Fasl and Fi‘l al-Kawn
Explains special connectors for the relation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the non-temporal connector (damir al-fasl) and the temporal connector (fi‘l al-kawn) in Arabic logical propositions.

Core Idea
The relation in a proposition can be timeless or temporal, and Arabic logic marks this with specific words.

The passage explains that logicians sometimes use special words to explicitly indicate the relation between subject and predicate. The pronoun of separation (damir al-fasl) like 'huwa' is timeless, while the verb of existence (fi‘l al-kawn) like 'kana' carries a temporal sense, as in 'Man was a drop' (al-insanu kana nutfatan). This distinction matters for understanding how propositions relate to time in kalam arguments.

Guided Path
1
Recognise damir al-fasl
Look for a pronoun like 'huwa' placed between subject and predicate. It emphasises the relation and does not indicate time.
Example: 'Zayd huwa qa'im' – the huwa is not performing a grammatical function but signalling the logical copula.
2
Recognise fi‘l al-kawn
Look for forms of 'kana' or similar existence verbs. These indicate that the predicate holds of the subject at a particular time.
Example: 'al-insanu kana nutfatan' – kana gives a past tense relation.
3
Compare the two connectors
Note the temporal implications: damir al-fasl is timeless, fi‘l al-kawn is temporal. This affects the truth conditions of the proposition in theological contexts.
In kalam, attributing a temporal connector to Allah may be problematic; thus damir al-fasl is preferred.
4
Identify the default relation
Even when no explicit connector is used, the relation is still present via i'rab. The connectors merely emphasise or specify time.
The passage says the default of a sentence is affirmative; explicit markers are for emphasis or time.

Source Text

والموضوع في تراكيب الجمل العربية يأتي مبتدأ أو نحوه إذا كانت الجملة جملة اسمية، ويأتي فاعلاً أو نحوه إذا كانت الجملة جملة فعلية، وأما المحمول فهو الركن الثاني من ركني الإسناد، وهو الخبر أو ما يقوم مقامه في الجملة الاسمية، والفعل أو ما يقوم مقامه في الجملة الفعلية، وأما النسبة بين الموضوع والمحمول فتدل عليها حركة الإعراب وهيئة تركيب الجملة، وقد يستعمل عند المناطقة للدلالة على النسبة ضمير الفصل وهو رابط غير زماني، أو فعل الكون وهو رابط زماني، كقولنا:

أمثلة:

(2)
The Subject, Predicate, and the Relation between Them
Any two single terms between which an affirmative or negative relation holds: one is the subject and the other is the predicate, and their combination together with the relation between them is a proposition.
The subject in Arabic sentence structures comes as a mubtada' or similar if the sentence is nominal, and comes as a fa'il or similar if the sentence is verbal. The predicate is the second pillar of the two pillars of predication; it is the khabar or what takes its place in the nominal sentence, and the verb or what takes its place in the verbal sentence. As for the relation between the subject and predicate, it is indicated by the inflectional vowel (i'rab) and the structure of the sentence. Logicians sometimes use the pronoun of separation (damir al-fasl), which is a non-temporal connector, or the verb of existence (fi'l al-kawn), which is a temporal connector, as in our saying:
'Snow is frozen water' and 'Man was a drop of seminal fluid.'
The default in a sentence is that it is affirmative, even if there is no word indicating affirmation. If something indicating affirmation is used, it is an emphasis of the affirmative meaning it carries. If a negative meaning is intended, then a tool of negation must be used to indicate it, such as: la, laysa, ghayr, ma, lan, and the like.
Examples:
A) 'Snow is frozen water'—this statement is a proposition. It is a nominal sentence. Its subject is 'snow', which is a mubtada', and its predicate is 'frozen water', which is a khabar, and the relation...
Source pages p. 24

This passage defines the three essential parts of a proposition (qadiyya). The author begins by stating that any two single terms (mufradān) connected by an affirmative or negative relation constitute a proposition: one term is the subject (mawḍū‘), the other the predicate (maḥmūl), and together with the relation they form the proposition. Examples: ‘al-thalju huwa mā’un mutajammidun’ and ‘al-insānu kāna nuṭfatan.’ The passage then explains that the default of a sentence is affirmative; if no explicit affirmative word is used, the sentence is still affirmative. Negation requires explicit negative particles. The example given is broken off but illustrates a simple affirmative proposition.

Checkpoint
What is the difference between damir al-fasl and fi‘l al-kawn according to the passage?
A) Damir al-fasl is a noun, fi‘l al-kawn is a verb.
B) Damir al-fasl is non-temporal, fi‘l al-kawn is temporal.
C) Damir al-fasl is used in verbal sentences, fi‘l al-kawn in nominal.
D) There is no difference; both are synonymous.

Key Takeaway
The logical relation can be explicitly marked by a timeless pronoun (damir al-fasl) or a temporal verb (fi‘l al-kawn), but the default relation is always present via inflection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Propositions

Classifying Propositions by Structure and Relation
Author explains four types of propositions and how to parse them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any Arabic declarative sentence into one of the four types of propositions (nominal/verbal, positive/negative) and identify the subject, predicate, and relation indicator.

Core Idea
Every proposition is built from two terms and a relation; the type depends on sentence form and affirmation.

The author begins by completing the description of a positive nominal proposition, then introduces three more types. Understanding this classification is essential for evaluating truth conditions in kalam and fiqh reasoning. In this lesson, we will systematically parse each type and learn to identify its components.

Guided Path
1
Identify sentence type (nominal vs verbal)
Look at the first word: if it is a noun (e.g., 'al-thalj' - the snow), it is nominal; if a verb (e.g., 'ashraqat' - rose), it is verbal.
Nominal sentences start with mubtada; verbal with verb.
2
Determine the relation (positive or negative)
Check for affirmative markers: in nominal, the raf' (nominative) on the predicate indicates positive; in verbal, the raf' on the fa'il indicates positive. Negation tools like 'laysa' or 'lam' indicate negative.
3
Locate the subject (mawdu') and predicate (mahmul)
In nominal sentences: subject = mubtada, predicate = khabar. In verbal sentences: subject = fa'il (doer), predicate = verb. The relation (nisba) is the connection between them.
The author provides examples: in 'al-thalju laysa hārran' subject = al-thalj, predicate = hārran.
4
Identify the tool indicating the relation
For positive nominal: raf' on the khabar. For negative nominal: 'laysa'. For positive verbal: raf' on the fa'il. For negative verbal: 'lam' before the verb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإيجابية بينهما قد دلت عليها حركة الإعراب وهي الرفع في الخبر، وهيئة تركيب الجملة .
ب - الثلج ليس حاراً ، (قضية) وهي جملة اسمية أيضاً، كسابقتها، إلا أن النسبة فيها سالبة، والأداة التي دلت على السلب هي كلمة : (ليس).
جـ ـ أشرقت الشمس، (قضية) وهي جملة فعلية، الموضوع فيها (الشمس)، وهو في الجملة فاعل، والمحمول فيها (أشرق) وهو فعل، والنسبة الإيجابية بينهما قد دلت عليها حركة الإعراب وهي الرفع في الفاعل، وهيئة تركيب الجملة .
د ـ لم تشرق الشمس، (قضية) وهي جملة فعلية، الموضوع فيها : (الشمس) وهو في الجملة فاعل، والمحمول فيها (تشرق) وهو فعل، إلا أن النسبة فيها سالبة، والأداة التي دلت على السلب هي كلمة (لم).
وقد يصلح كل من المفردين لأن يكون موضوع القضية أو محمولها، وعندئذ يرجع تعيين الموضوع والمحمول في تركيب القضية إلى الحالة الذهنية التي تستدعي أن يكون أحدهما بعينه هو الموضوع، وأن يكون الآخر هو المحمول .
ففي المفردين التاليين : (محمد) و (خاتم المرسلين) يصلح كل منهما لأن يكون موضوعاً ولأن يكون محمولاً وذلك بوجه عام، ولكن تعيين الموضوع والمحمول منهما يتبع ما يقتضيه الحال، فإذا كان الحال يقتضي إثبات أن محمداً متصف بأنه خاتم المرسلين، فينبغي جعل الأول منهما موضوعاً - أي مبتدأ في الجملة - وجعل الثاني محمولاً - أي خبراً في الجملة - فنقول : محمد خاتم المرسلين، وإذا كان الحال يقتضي إثبات أن خاتم المرسلين هو محمد لا غيره، فينبغي جعل (خاتم المرسلين) موضوعاً أي : مبتدأ، وجعل (محمد) محمولاً أي : خبراً، فنقول : خاتم المرسلين محمد .
والقضية في هذا الفن تساوي الجملة المفيدة فائدةً تامة عند علماء العربية، وأقلّ ما تتألف منه مفردان: مبتدأ وخبر، أو فعل وفاعل، أو ما يقوم مقام كل منهما.
جدول إدراكات تصورية وتصديقية للتطبيق:
الموجبة
قضية في جملة اسمية: الموضوع النسبة المحمول
السالبة
الموجبة
قضية في جملة فعلية: المحمول النسبة الموضوع
السالبة
خذ من المفردات التالية ما تكوّن به قضية موجبة أو سالبة، على أن تعين الموضوع والمحمول وتبين النسبة الموجبة أو السالبة فيها، مع بيان ما دلّ على الإيجاب أو السلب، وبيان المفردات التصورية فيها، والنسبة التصديقية.
جبل - طائر - كبير - تراب - نائم - غزال - أحمر - صاهل - ورد - إنسان - عميق - ناطق - فرس - بئر - عمود - كثير - طويل - ماء - يسيل - يطير - يتحرك - جاء - يتكلم - امتدّ.
The positive relation between them is indicated by the movement of declension (i'rab), which is the raf' in the predicate, and the structure of the sentence composition.
b - 'The snow is not hot' (a proposition). It is also a nominal sentence, like its predecessor, except that the relation in it is negative, and the tool that indicated the negation is the word 'laysa'.
c - 'The sun rose' (a proposition). It is a verbal sentence. The subject in it is 'the sun', which in the sentence is the doer (fa'il). The predicate in it is 'rose', which is a verb. The positive relation between them is indicated by the movement of declension, which is the raf' in the doer, and the structure of the sentence composition.
d - 'The sun did not rise' (a proposition). It is a verbal sentence. The subject in it is 'the sun', which in the sentence is the doer. The predicate in it is 'rise', which is a verb. However, the relation in it is negative, and the tool that indicated the negation is the word 'lam'.
Each of the two terms can be suitable to be the subject or the predicate of the proposition. In that case, the determination of the subject and predicate in the composition of the proposition reverts to the mental state that requires that one specific one be the subject and the other be the predicate.
Thus in the following two terms: (Muhammad) and (Seal of the Prophets), each can be subject or predicate in general, but the determination of which is subject and which is predicate follows what the situation requires. If the situation requires proving that Muhammad is described as the Seal of the Prophets, then the first should be made subject (i.e., mubtada in the sentence) and the second made predicate (i.e., khabar), so we say: 'Muhammad is the Seal of the Prophets.' If the situation requires proving that the Seal of the Prophets is Muhammad and no other, then 'Seal of the Prophets' should be made subject (mubtada) and 'Muhammad' predicate (khabar), so we say: 'The Seal of the Prophets is Muhammad.'
A proposition (qadiyya) in this art is equivalent to a complete meaningful sentence (jumlah mufida) according to Arabic linguists, and the minimum it consists of is two terms: a subject (mubtada) and a predicate (khabar), or a verb and a doer, or whatever stands in for each of them.
Table of conceptual and assenting perceptions for application:

Negative

Proposition in a verbal sentence: Predicate - Relation - Subject

Take from the following terms to form a positive or negative proposition, specifying the subject and predicate, indicating the positive or negative relation in it, stating what indicated affirmation or negation, stating the conceptual terms in it, and the assenting relation.
Mountain - bird - big - soil - sleeping - gazelle - red - neighing - rose - human - deep - rational speaking (natiq) - horse - well - pillar - much - tall - water - flows - flies - moves - came - speaks - extended.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage classifies propositions (qadaya) into four types based on sentence structure (nominal/verbal) and relation (positive/negative). It begins by completing the description of the first type (positive nominal), then lists three more: a negative nominal (b), a positive verbal (c), and a negative verbal (d). For each, the author identifies the subject (موضوع), predicate (محمول), and the tool indicating the relation. The crucial point is that the same two terms can switch roles as subject and predicate depending on the speaker's intent: e.g., 'Muhammad is the Seal of the Prophets' vs. 'The Seal of the Prophets is Muhammad.' The passage then defines a proposition (qadiyya) as a complete sentence in Arabic grammar, minimally composed of two elements. Finally, a table and an exercise are provided for students to practice identifying subject, predicate, and relation in various word pairs. All examples must use Islamic or Arabic contexts; the given words include terms like 'natiq' (rational) and 'Muhammad'.

Key Takeaway
Every proposition is either nominal or verbal, and either positive or negative; the type determines how subject, predicate, and relation are identified.

Propositions

8 min
Subject-Predicate Flexibility Based on Intent
Author explains flexibility of subject and predicate assignment based on speaker's intent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the subject and predicate of a proposition based on the speaker's intent, using the example of Muhammad as Seal of the Prophets.

Core Idea
Subject and predicate are not fixed; they depend on what you want to prove about the two terms.

The author demonstrates that the same pair of terms can yield two different propositions depending on the intended meaning. This flexibility is crucial in theological and legal arguments where emphasis matters. In this lesson, we will learn how to assign subject and predicate based on the mental state (al-hala al-dhihniyya) and the required proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms under consideration
The passage uses 'Muhammad' and 'Seal of the Prophets'. In general, any two terms can be paired. Write them down as Term A and Term B.
2
Determine the intended assertion
Ask: what are we trying to prove? (a) that Term A is described by Term B, or (b) that Term B is described by Term A? The answer determines which term is subject (the described) and which is predicate (the describers).
The author says: 'If the situation requires proving that Muhammad is described as the Seal of the Prophets...'
3
Assign subject and predicate accordingly
Subject = the term that is being described; predicate = the term that describes the subject. In the first case, subject = Muhammad, predicate = Seal of the Prophets; in the second case, subject = Seal of the Prophets, predicate = Muhammad.
4
Formulate the proposition in Arabic
Place the subject as mubtada (or fa'il) and the predicate as khabar (or verb). For nominal sentences, this gives two formulations: 'Muhammadun khatamu al-mursaleen' and 'Khatamu al-mursaleena Muhammadun'.
The grammatical cases must match: subject in nominative, predicate in nominative in nominal sentences.

ب - الثلج ليس حاراً ، (قضية) وهي جملة اسمية أيضاً، كسابقتها، إلا أن النسبة فيها سالبة، والأداة التي دلت على السلب هي كلمة : (ليس).
جـ ـ أشرقت الشمس، (قضية) وهي جملة فعلية، الموضوع فيها (الشمس)، وهو في الجملة فاعل، والمحمول فيها (أشرق) وهو فعل، والنسبة الإيجابية بينهما قد دلت عليها حركة الإعراب وهي الرفع في الفاعل، وهيئة تركيب الجملة .
د ـ لم تشرق الشمس، (قضية) وهي جملة فعلية، الموضوع فيها : (الشمس) وهو في الجملة فاعل، والمحمول فيها (تشرق) وهو فعل، إلا أن النسبة فيها سالبة، والأداة التي دلت على السلب هي كلمة (لم).
وقد يصلح كل من المفردين لأن يكون موضوع القضية أو محمولها، وعندئذ يرجع تعيين الموضوع والمحمول في تركيب القضية إلى الحالة الذهنية التي تستدعي أن يكون أحدهما بعينه هو الموضوع، وأن يكون الآخر هو المحمول .
ففي المفردين التاليين : (محمد) و (خاتم المرسلين) يصلح كل منهما لأن يكون موضوعاً ولأن يكون محمولاً وذلك بوجه عام، ولكن تعيين الموضوع والمحمول منهما يتبع ما يقتضيه الحال، فإذا كان الحال يقتضي إثبات أن محمداً متصف بأنه خاتم المرسلين، فينبغي جعل الأول منهما موضوعاً - أي مبتدأ في الجملة - وجعل الثاني محمولاً - أي خبراً في الجملة - فنقول : محمد خاتم المرسلين، وإذا كان الحال يقتضي إثبات أن خاتم المرسلين هو محمد لا غيره، فينبغي جعل (خاتم المرسلين) موضوعاً أي : مبتدأ، وجعل (محمد) محمولاً أي : خبراً، فنقول : خاتم المرسلين محمد .
والقضية في هذا الفن تساوي الجملة المفيدة فائدةً تامة عند علماء العربية، وأقلّ ما تتألف منه مفردان: مبتدأ وخبر، أو فعل وفاعل، أو ما يقوم مقام كل منهما.

جبل - طائر - كبير - تراب - نائم - غزال - أحمر - صاهل - ورد - إنسان - عميق - ناطق - فرس - بئر - عمود - كثير - طويل - ماء - يسيل - يطير - يتحرك - جاء - يتكلم - امتدّ.
The positive relation between them is indicated by the movement of declension (i'rab), which is the raf' in the predicate, and the structure of the sentence composition.
b - 'The snow is not hot' (a proposition). It is also a nominal sentence, like its predecessor, except that the relation in it is negative, and the tool that indicated the negation is the word 'laysa'.
c - 'The sun rose' (a proposition). It is a verbal sentence. The subject in it is 'the sun', which in the sentence is the doer (fa'il). The predicate in it is 'rose', which is a verb. The positive relation between them is indicated by the movement of declension, which is the raf' in the doer, and the structure of the sentence composition.
d - 'The sun did not rise' (a proposition). It is a verbal sentence. The subject in it is 'the sun', which in the sentence is the doer. The predicate in it is 'rise', which is a verb. However, the relation in it is negative, and the tool that indicated the negation is the word 'lam'.
Each of the two terms can be suitable to be the subject or the predicate of the proposition. In that case, the determination of the subject and predicate in the composition of the proposition reverts to the mental state that requires that one specific one be the subject and the other be the predicate.
Thus in the following two terms: (Muhammad) and (Seal of the Prophets), each can be subject or predicate in general, but the determination of which is subject and which is predicate follows what the situation requires. If the situation requires proving that Muhammad is described as the Seal of the Prophets, then the first should be made subject (i.e., mubtada in the sentence) and the second made predicate (i.e., khabar), so we say: 'Muhammad is the Seal of the Prophets.' If the situation requires proving that the Seal of the Prophets is Muhammad and no other, then 'Seal of the Prophets' should be made subject (mubtada) and 'Muhammad' predicate (khabar), so we say: 'The Seal of the Prophets is Muhammad.'
A proposition (qadiyya) in this art is equivalent to a complete meaningful sentence (jumlah mufida) according to Arabic linguists, and the minimum it consists of is two terms: a subject (mubtada) and a predicate (khabar), or a verb and a doer, or whatever stands in for each of them.

Commentary
This passage classifies propositions (qadaya) into four types based on sentence structure (nominal/verbal) and relation (positive/negative). It begins by completing the description of the first type (positive nominal), then lists three more: a negative nominal (b), a positive verbal (c), and a negative verbal (d). For each, the author identifies the subject (موضوع), predicate (محمول), and the tool indicating the relation. The crucial point is that the same two terms can switch roles as subject and predicate depending on the speaker's intent: e.g., 'Muhammad is the Seal of the Prophets' vs. 'The Seal of the Prophets is Muhammad.' The passage then defines a proposition (qadiyya) as a complete sentence in Arabic grammar, minimally composed of two elements. Finally, a table and an exercise are provided for students to practice identifying subject, predicate, and relation in various word pairs. All examples must use Islamic or Arabic contexts; the given words include terms like 'natiq' (rational) and 'Muhammad'.

Parsing Propositions: Nominal and Verbal Forms
Presentation of a table and exercise for forming propositions from singular terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject, predicate, and relation in both nominal and verbal propositions, and form affirmative/negative propositions from given singular terms.

Core Idea
A proposition is composed of subject, predicate, and relation—but the order changes between nominal and verbal sentences.

Before you can evaluate the truth of any statement in Islamic sciences, you must be able to identify its parts. This exercise from our textbook trains you to dissect a proposition (qadiyya) into its core components, whether it is a nominal or verbal sentence. It also teaches you to distinguish the simple concepts (tasawwur) from the act of assenting (tasdiq) that joins them. Let's walk through the table and apply it.

Guided Path
1
Understand the table structure
The table shows two rows: nominal sentence and verbal sentence. For each, affirmative and negative are listed. In nominal: Subject - Relation - Predicate. In verbal: Predicate - Relation - Subject.
Memorize these orders; they are the skeleton of any proposition.
2
Identify the parts in a given proposition
Take a proposition like 'al-jabalu kabirun' (the mountain is big). Identify subject (al-jabalu), predicate (kabirun), relation (affirmative). Note the order matches nominal.
In Arabic, the relation is often implicit in the inflection of the predicate.
3
Construct propositions from the list
Use the given singular terms (e.g., jabl, kabir) to form a proposition. Choose one as subject, one as predicate, and decide on affirmative or negative.
Try both nominal and verbal structures.
4
Find the indicator of affirmation/negation
In Arabic, affirmative propositions may have no explicit marker (just the predicate with a damma), while negative often uses 'la' or 'laysa'. In the exercise, specify what indicates the relation.
For example, in 'al-jabalu kabirun', the absence of negation indicates affirmation; in 'laysa al-jabalu sagheeran', 'laysa' indicates negation.
5
Separate tasawwur from tasdiq
The singular terms are tasawwuri concepts. The proposition is a completed tasdiq. Identify which words are concepts and which is the judgment (the relation).
This distinction is foundational for later discussions on knowledge.

Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage provides a pedagogical table and exercise for understanding the basic parts of a proposition (qadiyya). The table distinguishes two types of sentences: nominal (عبارة اسمية) and verbal (عبارة فعلية). In a nominal sentence, the order is subject (موضوع) - relation (نسبة) - predicate (محمول). In a verbal sentence, the order is predicate (محصول/verb) - relation (نسبة) - subject (موضوع). The relation can be affirmative (موجبة) or negative (سالبة). The exercise then asks the student to take the given list of singular terms (all are tasawwuri concepts) and form a proposition by pairing them, identifying the subject, predicate, and relation. The student must also determine what word or particle indicates affirmation or negation (e.g., absence of negation for affirmative, presence of a negation word for negative). This trains the student to distinguish between conceptual singulars and the act of assenting (tasdiq) that joins them. All examples are from everyday experience suitable for beginners.

Key Terms
قضية (qadiyya): A proposition: a sentence that can be true or false, composed of a subject and predicate with a relation. [Note: No significant shift; stable across the tradition.]
موضوع (mawdu'): The subject of a proposition; what the proposition is about. [Note: No significant shift.]
محمول (mahmul): The predicate of a proposition; what is affirmed or denied of the subject. [Note: No significant shift.]
نسبة (nisba): The relation (affirmative or negative) linking subject and predicate. [Note: No significant shift.]
تصور (tasawwur): A singular mental concept; the conceptualization of an object without judgment. [Note: In later epistemology, tasawwur is often paired with tasdiq; this text uses it as the building block of propositions.]
تصديق (tasdiq): Assent; the judgment that a proposition is true or false. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Subject - Predicate - Relation
D) Relation - Subject - Predicate

Key Takeaway
A proposition has three essential parts: subject, predicate, and relation; the order depends on whether the sentence is nominal or verbal.

The Three Modes of the Necessary Concomitant (Lāzim)
Author presents the first way of dividing lāzim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any necessary concomitant into one of three modes: mental only, external only, or both.

Core Idea
The necessary concomitant can exist in the mind, in reality, or in both.

The logicians recognized that not every necessary relation exists in the same way. Some relations are purely conceptual, while others hold in the external world, and some in both. This classification helps us understand the nature of causal and logical dependencies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
From the first way of division, list the three categories: (1) in both mind and external, (2) in mind only, (3) in external only.
2
Understand the examples
For each category, the author gives an example: need of originated for originator (both), mental image of sight from blindness (mind only), blackness of crow (external only).
These examples come from kalām and everyday experience.
3
Apply to new cases
Test a new example: 'Heat from fire' – in which mode does this concomitant belong? Consider both mental and external aspects.
Think about whether the relation exists in the mind, in reality, or both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمناطقة في تقسيم اللازم طريقان : الطريق الأول : أن اللازم ينقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام ١ - لازم في الذهن والخارج معاً، كلزوم حاجة الحادث إلى محدث . ٢ - لازم في الذهن فقط، كلزوم تصوّر البصر لدى تصوّر العمى . ٣ - لازم في الخارج فقط، كلزوم لون السواد لطائر الغراب . الطريق الثاني : أن اللازم ينقسم إلى قسمين : ١ - لازم بين : وهو ما يلزم فيه من تصوّر المتلازمين تصوّر اللزوم بينهما، دون حاجة إلى دليل، وهذا البين ينقسم إلى ذهني، وهو ما يلزم فيه من تصوّر الملزوم تصوّر اللازم . وغير ذهني وهو ما لا يلزم فيه ذلك، كمغايرة الإنسان للفرس . ٢ - لازم غير بين : وهو ما لا يلزم فيه من تصوّر المتلازمين تصوّر اللزوم بينهما إلا بدليل .
Logicians have two ways in dividing the necessary concomitant: The first way: The necessary concomitant is divided into three sections: 1. Necessary concomitant in the mind and externally together, like the concomitance of the need of an originated thing for an originator. 2. Necessary concomitant in the mind only, like the concomitance of the mental image of sight when imagining blindness. 3. Necessary concomitant externally only, like the concomitance of the color black for the crow. The second way: The necessary concomitant is divided into two sections: 1. Evident necessary concomitant: that which, from conceptualizing the two concomitants, the conceptualization of the concomitance between them follows, without need for proof. And this evident is divided into mental, which is that from conceptualizing the dependent, the conceptualization of the concomitant follows, and non-mental, which is that from which does not follow that, like the difference of human from horse. 2. Non-evident necessary concomitant: that which does not necessitate, from conceptualizing the two concomitants, the conceptualization of the concomitance between them except by proof.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage presents two classifications of the necessary concomitant (lāzim) used by logicians. The first classification is based on the mode of existence: (1) necessary concomitant in both mind and external reality, exemplified by the need of an originated being for a creator—a key kalām principle; (2) necessary concomitant in the mind only, such as the mental image of sight following from the mental image of blindness; (3) necessary concomitant in external reality only, such as blackness being a necessary concomitant of the crow. The second classification is based on evidential clarity: the evident (bayyin) necessary concomitant, which is known without proof, divides further into mental (where conceptualizing the dependent immediately brings the concomitant to mind) and non-mental (like the difference between human and horse, which is evident yet not mentally immediate). The non-evident (ghayr bayyin) requires a proof to establish the concomitance. These classifications are foundational for understanding causal relationships and logical implications in Islamic philosophy and theology.

Key Terms
لازم
لازم بين
لازم غير بين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Mind only
B) External only
C) Both mind and external
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The logicians' first division of the necessary concomitant is based on its mode of existence: mental, external, or both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Evident and Non-Evident Concomitant (Lāzim Bayyin and Ghayr Bayyin)
Author presents the second way of dividing lāzim into evident and non-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an evident necessary concomitant, which is known without proof, and a non-evident one, which requires evidence.

Core Idea
Some logical relations are immediately clear, others need proof.

Not all necessary relations are equally obvious. Some are so clear that as soon as you think of the two things, you see the connection. Others require a demonstration. This distinction guides the logician in when to demand a proof and when to accept a relation as self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of evident (bayyin)
According to the author, a lāzim bayyin is such that from conceptualizing the two concomitants, the conceptualization of the concomitance follows without need for proof.
2
Recognize the subcategories of bayyin
Evident is further divided into mental (where conceptualizing the dependent directly brings the concomitant to mind) and non-mental (where the relation is evident but not mentally immediate, like human-horse difference).
The text gives 'difference of human from horse' as an example of non-mental bayyin.
3
Identify non-evident (ghayr bayyin)
A lāzim ghayr bayyin requires a proof to establish the concomitance; it is not immediately clear from conceptualizing the terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Logicians have two ways in dividing the necessary concomitant: The first way: The necessary concomitant is divided into three sections: 1. Necessary concomitant in the mind and externally together, like the concomitance of the need of an originated thing for an originator. 2. Necessary concomitant in the mind only, like the concomitance of the mental image of sight when imagining blindness. 3. Non-evident necessary concomitant: that which does not necessitate, from conceptualizing the two concomitants, the conceptualization of the concomitance between them except by proof.

Commentary

The first classification is based on the mode of existence: (1) necessary concomitant in both mind and external reality, exemplified by the need of an originated being for a creator—a key kalām principle; (2) necessary concomitant in the mind only, such as the mental image of sight following from the mental image of blindness; (3) necessary concomitant in external reality only, such as blackness being a necessary concomitant of the crow. The non-evident (ghayr bayyin) requires a proof to establish the concomitance. Propositions
beginner
5 min
Definition of Proposition and Exclusion of Performatives
Author defines proposition (qadiyya) as a declarative sentence and excludes performative sentences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a declarative proposition (qadiyya) and a performative sentence (insha'iyya) and identify which sentences are truth-apt in logical analysis.

Core Idea
In logic, only declarative sentences are propositions; performatives have no truth-value.

This passage establishes the foundational subject matter of logic: propositions. The author clearly demarcates what counts as a proposition — only sentences that can be true or false. Everyday utterances like commands, contracts, and divorces are set aside. Understanding this boundary is crucial before studying proofs and arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining property of a proposition
Look for the phrase 'الجملة التامة الخبرية' and notice that it is called a proposition in this art. The key feature is that it can be true or false.
The author emphasizes that performatives are not susceptible to truth or falsity.
2
Recognize examples of performative sentences
The author lists commands ('آتني غدائي'), prohibitions ('لا تأخذ مالي'), contracts ('بعتك هذا الثوب بدينار'), and divorces ('اذهبي فأنت طالق') as examples of insha'iyyat.
These sentences do not assert a fact and thus have no truth-value.
3
Explain why performatives are excluded
Performatives do not 'bear truth or falsity at all' (لا تحتمل الصدق أو الكذب أصلاً). Therefore, logic does not investigate them because there is no need for arguments to prove truth or falsity.
The purpose of logic is to evaluate truth and falsity of propositions.
4
Apply the definition to new Islamic examples
Take sentences from Islamic sources and decide if they are propositions or performatives. For example, 'Allah is one' is a proposition; 'Pray as you have seen me praying' is a performative command.
Distinguishing them is essential for logical analysis in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۰)
القَضَايا وأقسَامُها
تعريف القضية:
الجملة التامة الخبرية تسمى في هذا الفن قضية، أما الجمل الإنشائية كالجمل القائمة على الأمر أو النهي أو إنشاء العقود أو حلّها ونحو ذلك فإن المناطقة لا يبحثون فيها، لأنها لا تحتمل الصدق أو الكذب أصلاً، فلا تحتاج إلى أدلة وحجج تثبت صدقها أو تثبت كذبها، فمن قال: آتني غدائي، ومن قال: لا تأخذ مالي، ومن قال لمتعاقد معه على بيع: بعتك هذا الثوب بدينار، فقال الشاري: قبلت شراءه، ومن قال لزوجته: اذهبي فأنت طالق، كلُّ هؤلاء لا يقال لأي واحد منهم: صدقت في قولك أو كذبت، لأن الجمل التي قالوها هي من قبيل الجمل الإنشائية التي لا تحتمل الصدق أو الكذب أصلاً، والمناطقة إنما يهمُّهم بحث الجمل التي تتضمن أحكاماً موجبة أو سالبة، وهذه الأحكام عرضة لأن تكون صادقة أو لأن تكون كاذبة، فتحتاج إلى حجج تثبت صدقها إذا كانت في الواقع صادقة، وذلك لمعرفة حقيقتها، أو لإثبات صدقها لخصم لا يسلم به، أو تحتاج إلى حجج تثبت كذبها إذا كانت في الواقع كاذبة، وذلك لمعرفة حقيقتها، أو لإثبات كذبها لخصم لا يسلم به.
وقد علمنا سابقاً أن مبحث الحجج والأدلة هو المقصد الثاني من المقصدين الأساسيين في هذا الفن.
وسميت الجملة المشتملة على حكم موجب أو سالبٍ قضيةً، لأن
(10)
Propositions and Their Divisions
Definition of Proposition:
The complete declarative sentence is called, in this art, a proposition. As for performative sentences, such as sentences based on command, prohibition, creating contracts, annulling them, and the like, logicians do not investigate them, because they are not susceptible to truth or falsity at all, so they do not need proofs and arguments to establish their truth or falsity. So whoever says: 'Bring me my lunch,' and whoever says: 'Do not take my wealth,' and whoever says to a person with whom he is contracting a sale: 'I sell you this garment for a dinar,' and the buyer says: 'I accept its purchase,' and whoever says to his wife: 'Go, you are divorced' – none of these can be said to any one of them: 'You told the truth' or 'You lied,' because the sentences they uttered are of the kind of performative sentences which are not susceptible to truth or falsity at all. Logicians are only concerned with investigating sentences that contain affirmative or negative judgments, and these judgments are liable to be true or false. They need arguments that establish their truth if they are in reality true, either to know their reality or to prove their truth to an opponent who does not concede it; or they need arguments that establish their falsity if they are in reality false, either to know their reality or to prove their falsity to an opponent who does not concede it.
And we have previously learned that the investigation of proofs and arguments is the second of the two main objectives of this art.
And the sentence containing an affirmative or negative judgment is called a proposition because...
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage defines the proposition (qadiyya) and clarifies its scope within logic. The author states that in this science, a 'complete declarative sentence' (al-jumla al-tamma al-khabariyya) is called a proposition. Performative sentences (insha'iyya) — those expressing commands, prohibitions, contracts, divorces, etc. — are excluded because they cannot be true or false. The author gives vivid examples: 'Bring me my lunch' (command), 'Do not take my wealth' (prohibition), a sale contract dialogue, and a divorce pronouncement. None of these admit truth-value attribution. Logic only deals with sentences that affirm or deny something, i.e., judgments that are truth-apt. The need for arguments (hujaj, adilla) arises only for such sentences, either to prove their truth or falsity. The author reminds that the study of proofs is the second main objective of logic. The final sentence, cut off, begins to explain the etymology: the sentence containing a positive or negative judgment is called a proposition (qadiyya) because...

Key Terms
قضية (qadiyya): A proposition is defined as a complete declarative sentence that is either true or false; it is the subject of logical analysis.
جملة تامة خبرية (jumla tamma khabariyya): A complete declarative sentence that conveys information and can be evaluated as true or false.
جمل إنشائية (jumal insha'iyya): Performative sentences that do not assert a fact and thus have no truth-value; they include commands, prohibitions, contracts, etc.
حكم موجب أو سالب (hukm mūjib aw sālib): A judgment that is either affirmative (positive) or negative; the kind of judgment that can be true or false.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a proposition (qadiyya) according to the text?
A) 'Bring me my lunch.'
B) 'Do not take my wealth.'
C) 'The world is created.'
D) 'You are divorced.'

Key Takeaway
A proposition in logic is a complete declarative sentence that can be true or false; performative sentences are excluded because they lack truth-value.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
From the following Islamic sentences, identify which are propositions (qadiyya) and which are performatives: (a) 'Allah is one.' (b) 'Do not worship idols.' (c) 'I sell you this book for five dirhams.' (d) 'The prayer is obligatory.' Explain your reasoning based on the definition in the passage.
?
Take any Islamic text (e.g., a hadith or a fiqh ruling) and find one proposition and one performative sentence. Write them down and justify your classification.

Propositions
beginner
8 min
Definition of Proposition and Its Quantity Divisions
Author defines proposition and classifies propositions by quantity into personal, universal, and partial (incomplete).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a proposition (qadiyya) and classify it by quantity as personal or universal based on the subject's coverage.

Core Idea
Only declarative sentences that can be true or false are propositions.

In logic, not every sentence is a proposition. Commands, questions, and performatives lack truth value. This lesson teaches you to distinguish a proposition from other sentences and then to classify it by the number of individuals its judgment covers—a key step before analyzing arguments.

Guided Path
1
Separate declarative from performative sentences
Read the passage and identify why sentences like 'Bring me lunch' are not propositions. Focus on the criterion of truth or falsity.
Performative sentences (إنشاء) cannot be true or false.
2
Understand quantity (al-kamm) and quality (al-kayf)
Quantity is the number of individuals the judgment covers; quality is whether the relation is affirmative or negative. Every proposition has both.
These are called 'accidents' of the proposition.
3
Classify by quantity: personal vs universal
If the subject is a specific individual, it is personal (shakhsiyya). If the subject is a universal term quantified by a universal quantifier (e.g., 'every'), it is universal (kulliyya). Notice the author says universal is equivalent to personal in force.
The passage also begins to introduce partial (juz'iyya) but does not complete it.

(۱۰)
القَضَايا وأقسَامُها
تعريف القضية :
الجملة التامة الخبرية تسمى في هذا الفن قضية، أما الجمل الإنشائية كالجمل القائمة على الأمر أو النهي أو إنشاء العقود أو حلّها ونحو ذلك فإن المناطقة لا يبحثون فيها، لأنها لا تحتمل الصدق أو الكذب أصلاً، فلا تحتاج إلى أدلة وحجج تثبت صدقها أو تثبت كذبها، فمن قال: آتني غدائي، ومن قال: لا تأخذ مالي، ومن قال لمتعاقد معه على بيع : بعتك هذا الثوب بدينار، فقال الشاري : قبلت شراءه، ومن قال لزوجته : اذهبي فأنت طالق، كلُّ هؤلاء لا يقال لأي واحد منهم : صدقت في قولك أو كذبت، لأن الجمل التي قالوها هي من قبيل الجمل الإنشائية التي لا تحتمل الصدق أو الكذب أصلاً، والمناطقة إنما يهمُّهم بحث الجمل التي تتضمن أحكاماً موجبة أو سالبة، وهذه الأحكام عرضة لأن تكون صادقة أو لأن تكون كاذبة، فتحتاج إلى حجج تثبت صدقها إذا كانت في الواقع صادقة، وذلك لمعرفة حقيقتها، أو لإثبات صدقها لخصم لا يسلم به، أو تحتاج إلى حجج تثبت كذبها إذا كانت في الواقع كاذبة، وذلك لمعرفة حقيقتها، أو لإثبات كذبها لخصم لا يسلم به .
وقد علمنا سابقاً أن مبحث الحجج والأدلة هو المقصد الثاني من المقصدين الأساسيين في هذا الفن .
وسميت الجملة المشتملة على حكم موجب أو سالبٍ قضيةً، لأن القضية مشتقة من القضاء، والقضاء هو الحكم، وظاهر أن كل جملة خبرية لا بدَّ أن تتضمن حكماً موجباً أو سالباً.
الكمُّ والكيف في القضايا:
يقصد بالكم في القضية مقدار الأفراد التي ينطبق عليها الحكم الذي تشتمل عليه.
ويقصد بالكيف في القضية حال النسبة فيها هل هي موجبة؟ أو سالبة؟ أي: هل هي مثبتة أو منفية؟.
وكل قضية لا بد أن يتعلق الحكم فيها بمقدار ما من الأفراد، واحداً فما فوق، معيناً أو غير معين، ولا بد أيضاً أن تكون النسبة فيها موجبة أو سالبة.
إذن فمن عوارض القضايا الكم والكيف.
أقسام القضايا باعتبار الكم:
ثم إذا نظرنا إلى جانب الكم في القضايا وجدنا أن المحكوم عليه فيها إما أن يكون معيناً مخصوصاً غير قابل للشركة، وفي هذه الحالة تسمى القضية: (شخصية) وتسمى: (مخصوصة) وإما أن يكون كلياً غير معين، أي: قابلاً للشركة المفتوحة دون تحديد، وعندئذٍ فله احتمالات ثلاثة:
الأول: أن يكون مسوَّراً بسورٍ كلي، أي: مقترناً بما يدلُّ على أن الحكم فيها يشمل جميع أفراد الكلي، وفي هذه الحالة تسمى القضية (كُلِّيَّة).
والكُلِّيَّة لدى التحقيق هي، في قوة المخصوصة، أو نقول: إن المخصوصة هي في قوة الكلية، وذلك لأن انطباق الحكم على كل الأفراد في القضية الكلية بقوة تكرير الحكم ونسبته إلى كل فرد بخصوصه، وذلك بعدد الأفراد مهما كثرت.
الثاني: أن يكون مسوَّراً بسورٍ غير كُلِّي، أي: مقترناً بما يدلُّ على أن الحكم يشمل بعض أفراد الكُلِّي فقط دون تعيين لأفراد هذا البعض، وربما
(10) Propositions and Their Divisions
Definition of Proposition:
The complete declarative sentence is called in this art a proposition. As for imperative sentences, such as sentences based on command, prohibition, conclusion of contracts, dissolution thereof, and the like, the logicians do not investigate them, because they do not admit of truth or falsity at all, so they do not need proofs and arguments to establish their truth or establish their falsehood. So whoever says: 'Bring me my lunch,' and whoever says: 'Do not take my property,' and whoever says to someone contracting with him for a sale: 'I have sold you this garment for a dinar,' and the buyer says: 'I accept its purchase,' and whoever says to his wife: 'Go, you are divorced' — none of these is said to any of them: 'You have spoken truthfully or falsely,' because the sentences they uttered are of the kind of imperative sentences that do not admit of truth or falsity at all. The logicians are only concerned with investigating sentences that contain affirmative or negative judgments, and these judgments are susceptible to being true or being false, so they need proofs to establish their truth if they are in reality true, in order to know their reality, or to establish their truth for an opponent who does not concede it; or they need proofs to establish their falsehood if they are in reality false, in order to know their reality, or to establish their falsehood for an opponent who does not concede it.
We already know previously that the topic of proofs and evidences is the second of the two fundamental objectives in this art.
And the sentence containing an affirmative or negative judgment is called a proposition, because 'proposition' is derived from 'judgment', and judgment is the ruling; it is evident that every declarative sentence must contain an affirmative or negative judgment.
Quantity and Quality in Propositions:
By quantity in a proposition is meant the number of individuals to which the judgment it contains applies.
By quality in a proposition is meant the state of the relation therein: is it affirmative or negative? That is, is it positive or negated?
Every proposition must have its judgment relate to some number of individuals, one or more, determined or undetermined, and also the relation must be affirmative or negative.
Thus, the accidents of propositions are quantity and quality.
Divisions of Propositions with Respect to Quantity:
Then if we consider the aspect of quantity in propositions, we find that the subject of judgment therein is either a particular determinate individual not susceptible to sharing—in which case the proposition is called 'personal' and also 'particular'—or it is a universal indeterminate, i.e., susceptible to open sharing without restriction, in which case it has three possibilities:
First: That it is quantified by a universal quantifier, i.e., accompanied by what indicates that the judgment includes all individuals of the universal—in which case the proposition is called 'universal'.
And the universal, upon investigation, is equivalent to the particular, or we say that the particular is equivalent to the universal, because the application of the judgment to all individuals in the universal proposition is by virtue of repeating the judgment and attributing it to each individual specifically, according to the number of individuals no matter how many.
Second: That it is quantified by a non-universal quantifier, i.e., accompanied by what indicates that the judgment includes only some individuals of the universal without specification of those individuals—and perhaps
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage defines the proposition (qadiyya) and then explains its two essential attributes: quantity (kam) and quality (kayf), followed by a classification of propositions by quantity. The author begins by distinguishing between declarative sentences (khabariyya) and imperative/performative sentences (insha'iyya). Only declarative sentences are considered propositions because they alone admit of truth and falsity. Examples from daily life—commands, prohibitions, contracts, divorces—illustrate statements that cannot be evaluated as true or false. The logician's concern is with judgments that are either affirmative or negative and can be proven true or false. The term 'qadiyya' is derived from 'qada' (judgment). Next, quantity (kam) is defined as the number of individuals the judgment covers, and quality (kayf) is whether the judgment is affirmative or negative. Every proposition necessarily has both a quantity (some number of individuals) and a quality (affirmative or negative). Thus, quantity and quality are accidents of propositions. The classification by quantity: if the subject is a specific individual not shared, it is a 'personal' (shakhsiyya) or 'particular' (makhsusa) proposition. If the subject is universal, it falls into three types: (1) universal (kulliyya) with a universal quantifier covering all individuals—interestingly, the author notes that universal and personal propositions are equivalent in force because universal judgment virtually repeats the judgment for each individual; (2) non-universal, covering some individuals without specification—this is the partial (juz'iyya) proposition, but the passage cuts off here. The explanation makes clear that quantity and quality are foundational for analyzing propositions in logic.

Key Takeaway
A proposition is a declarative sentence that can be true or false; its quantity (personal, universal, or partial) determines how many individuals the judgment covers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which of the following sentences are propositions according to the definition: 1) 'Prayer is obligatory.' 2) 'Do not lie.' 3) 'Allah is one.' Then classify each proposition by quantity: personal, universal, or partial (if applicable).
?
Write a universal proposition in Arabic about a fiqh ruling and then convert it into a personal proposition by specifying a single individual (e.g., 'Zayd is fasting'). Explain why the author says universal and personal are equivalent in force.

Propositions
beginner
10 min
Classifying Propositions by Quantity
Author defines propositional quantity and quality, then classifies propositions by quantity into four types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the quantity of any proposition (personal, universal, particular, indefinite) and explain the equivalence between personal and universal, and between indefinite and particular.

Core Idea
The quantity of a proposition tells you exactly how many individuals the judgment covers.

In this lesson, we will learn how to determine the quantity of a proposition by examining its subject and quantifier. The author of our text explains that every proposition has a subject that either is a specific individual or a general term, and if general, it may have a universal quantifier, a particular quantifier, or none. We will also see the logical equivalences that simplify later reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of the proposition
Find the thing being judged (maḥkūm ʿalayh). Is it a specific named individual (like Zayd) or a general term (like 'human')?
2
Check for quantifiers
If the subject is a general term, look for words like 'every', 'all', 'no' (universal), or 'some', 'many', 'a few' (particular). If none, the proposition is indefinite (muhmala).
3
Apply the four types
If the subject is a determinate individual → personal (shakhṣiyya). If universal quantifier → universal (kulliyya). If particular quantifier → particular (juz'iyya). If no quantifier → indefinite (muhmala).
4
Understand the equivalences
Personal and universal are equivalent in logical force because a universal is a conjunction of personal judgments. Indefinite and particular are equivalent because an unquantified general subject naturally indicates some individuals.
The equivalences are important for later steps in logical inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القضية مشتقة من القضاء، والقضاء هو الحكم، وظاهر أن كل جملة خبرية لا بدَّ أن تتضمن حكماً موجباً أو سالباً.
الكمُّ والكيف في القضايا:
يقصد بالكم في القضية مقدار الأفراد التي ينطبق عليها الحكم الذي تشتمل عليه.
ويقصد بالكيف في القضية حال النسبة فيها هل هي موجبة؟ أو سالبة؟ أي: هل هي مثبتة أو منفية؟.
وكل قضية لا بد أن يتعلق الحكم فيها بمقدار ما من الأفراد، واحداً فما فوق، معيناً أو غير معين، ولا بد أيضاً أن تكون النسبة فيها موجبة أو سالبة.
إذن فمن عوارض القضايا الكم والكيف.
أقسام القضايا باعتبار الكم:
ثم إذا نظرنا إلى جانب الكم في القضايا وجدنا أن المحكوم عليه فيها إما أن يكون معيناً مخصوصاً غير قابل للشركة، وفي هذه الحالة تسمى القضية: (شخصية) وتسمى: (مخصوصة) وإما أن يكون كلياً غير معين، أي: قابلاً للشركة المفتوحة دون تحديد، وعندئذٍ فله احتمالات ثلاثة:
الأول: أن يكون مسوَّراً بسورٍ كلي، أي: مقترناً بما يدلُّ على أن الحكم فيها يشمل جميع أفراد الكلي، وفي هذه الحالة تسمى القضية (كُلِّيَّة).
والكُلِّيَّة لدى التحقيق هي، في قوة المخصوصة، أو نقول: إن المخصوصة هي في قوة الكلية، وذلك لأن انطباق الحكم على كل الأفراد في القضية الكلية بقوة تكرير الحكم ونسبته إلى كل فرد بخصوصه، وذلك بعدد الأفراد مهما كثرت.
الثاني: أن يكون مسوَّراً بسورٍ غير كُلِّي، أي: مقترناً بما يدلُّ على أن الحكم يشمل بعض أفراد الكُلِّي فقط دون تعيين لأفراد هذا البعض، وربما يكون البعض هو الأكثر في عدد الأفراد من البعض الذي لا يشمله الحكم، وحينما يكون المحكوم عليه مسوراً بسورٍ غير كُلِّي تسمى القضية (جزئية).
الثالث: أن يكون غير مسوَّرٍ بأي سور، أي: غير مقترن بما يدلُّ على شمول الحكم لكل الأفراد أو لبعض الأفراد من غير تعيين، وفي هذه الحالة تسمى القضية (مهملة).
والقضية المهملة لدى التحقيق هي في قوة القضية الجزئية وهي المسوَّرة بسور غير كُلِّي، وذلك لأن المهملة التي ليس فيها ما يدلُّ على الإحاطة الجزئية تدلُّ بطبيعتها على ذلك، فذكر السور الجزئي لا يزيد في معنى الإحاطة الجزئية شيئاً، وإن كان قد يزيد في نوع الدلالة، إذا كان فيه معنى الأقل أو الأكثر، أو كان فيه تحديد نسبة البعض بالنصف أو الثلث أو الربع أو نحو ذلك من غير تعيين أشخاص الأفراد، أما لفظ بعض وما أشبهه فليس فيه مزيد دلالة، لذلك فالمهملة تلازم الجزئية لأنها في قوتها.
فالقضايا إذن أربع:
١ - الشخصية (= المخصوصة).
٢ - الكُلِّيَّة.
وهما متماثلتان في القوة.
٣ - الجزئية.
٤ - المهملة.
وهما متماثلتان في القوة.
أقسام القضايا باعتبار الكيف:
وإذا نظرنا إلى جانب الكيف في القضايا وجدنا أن النَّسب فيها إما أن تكون موجبة، وذلك إذا كانت هذه النسب خالية عن أدوات السلب (النفي).
وإما أن تكون سالبة، وذلك إذا كانت هذه النَّسب منفية بتسليط أداة من أدوات السلب.
The proposition is derived from al-qadā' (judgment), and al-qadā' is the ruling. It is apparent that every declarative sentence must contain a positive or negative ruling. Quantity and quality in propositions: By quantity in a proposition is meant the extent of individuals to which the ruling contained in it applies. By quality in a proposition is meant the state of the relation in it: is it affirmative or negative? i.e., is it asserted or negated? Every proposition must have its ruling related to some amount of individuals, one or more, determinate or indeterminate, and also the relation in it must be affirmative or negative. Therefore, quantity and quality are among the accidents of propositions. Divisions of propositions with respect to quantity: Then if we look at the aspect of quantity in propositions, we find that the subject of judgment in them is either determinate, specific, not admitting of sharing, and in this case the proposition is called 'personal' (shakhṣiyya) and is also called 'specific' (mukhṣūṣa); or it is universal and indeterminate, i.e., admitting of open sharing without specification, and then it has three possibilities: First: that it is quantified by a universal quantifier, i.e., accompanied by what indicates that the ruling in it includes all individuals of the universal, and in this case the proposition is called 'universal' (kulliyya). And the universal, upon investigation, is equivalent in force to the specific, or we say: the specific is equivalent in force to the universal, because the application of the ruling to all individuals in the universal proposition is by way of repeating the ruling and attributing it to each individual in particular, according to the number of individuals however many they may be. Second: that it is quantified by a non-universal quantifier, i.e., accompanied by what indicates that the ruling includes only some individuals of the universal without specifying the individuals of this portion, and perhaps that portion is the majority in number of individuals relative to the portion not covered by the ruling, and when the subject is quantified by a non-universal quantifier the proposition is called 'particular' (juz'iyya). Third: that it is not quantified by any quantifier, i.e., not accompanied by what indicates the inclusion of the ruling to all individuals or to some individuals without specification, and in this case the proposition is called 'indefinite' (muhmala). And the indefinite proposition, upon investigation, is equivalent in force to the particular proposition quantified with a non-universal quantifier, because the indefinite, which does not contain an indication of partial coverage, naturally indicates that, so mentioning the particular quantifier adds nothing to the meaning of partial coverage, although it may add to the type of indication if it conveys the meaning of 'few' or 'many', or if it specifies the proportion of the portion as half, third, quarter, or the like without specifying the individuals. As for the word 'some' (ba‘ḍ) and similar, there is no additional indication in it. Therefore, the indefinite entails the particular because it is equivalent in force to it. So propositions are thus four: 1. Personal (= specific). 2. Universal. Both are equivalent in force. 3. Particular. 4. Indefinite. Both are equivalent in force. Divisions of propositions with respect to quality: And if we look at the aspect of quality in propositions, we find that the relations in them are either affirmative, when these relations are free from tools of negation (negation), or negative, when these relations are negated by applying a tool of negation.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a classification of propositions (qadāyā) by their two essential accidental attributes: quantity (kamm) and quality (kayf). The author begins by defining the term 'proposition' as derived from 'judgment', emphasizing that every declarative sentence inherently contains a positive or negative ruling. Then quantity and quality are introduced as the accidents that attach to propositions. 

Under quantity, the author examines the subject (maḥkūm ʿalayh) — the thing judged about. It can be determinate and not sharable (like 'Zayd' or 'this specific person'), yielding a 'personal proposition' (shakhṣiyya). Or it can be a universal term with three quantifier possibilities: 
- (1) A universal quantifier covering all individuals → universal proposition (kulliyya). 
- (2) A non-universal quantifier covering some unspecified individuals → particular proposition (juz'iyya). 
- (3) No quantifier at all → indefinite/muhmala proposition. 

The author then clarifies two equivalences: (a) universal and personal are equivalent in logical force because a universal judgment is effectively a conjunction of singular judgments; (b) indefinite and particular are equivalent because an unquantified universal subject is naturally taken to refer to some individuals (unless context specifies otherwise). 

Finally, under quality, the passage only begins the classification: propositions are either affirmative (positive) when free from negation, or negative when a negation tool is applied. The enumeration of negative tools is cut off at the end of the page, but the framework is established.

Throughout, the author uses the example of a universal subject like 'every human' to illustrate quantification. An Islamic example: 'Every prayer (ṣalāt) is obligatory' is a universal affirmative; 'Some prayers are voluntary' is particular affirmative; 'No prayer is invalid' is universal negative; 'Some prayers are not invalid' is particular negative. The indefinite 'Prayer is obligatory' (without quantifier) would be treated as particular unless context implies universality.

Propositions
beginner

Classifying Propositions by Quality
Author defines propositional quantity and quality, then classifies propositions by quantity into four types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the quality of a proposition as affirmative or negative by checking for the presence of a negation tool.

Core Idea
Quality tells you whether the proposition asserts or negates the relation between subject and predicate.

In this lesson, we turn to the second accidental attribute: quality (kayf). The author explains that every proposition has a relation (nisba) that is either affirmed (positive) or denied (negative). We will learn to spot negation tools and classify propositions accordingly.

Guided Path
1
Locate the relation (nisba)
The relation is the connection between subject and predicate. Determine whether it is being asserted or negated.
2
Check for negation tools
Look for words like 'lā', 'mā', 'laysa', 'lam', 'lan' etc. that negate the predicate. If any such tool is present, the proposition is negative; otherwise, it is affirmative.
3
Classify accordingly
If no negation → affirmative (mūjaba). If negation → negative (sāliba).
4
Combine with quantity
The quality always combines with quantity to give four standard forms: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. Personal propositions also have quality.
Later in logic, these combinations are used to determine immediate inferences like obversion and contraposition.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القضية مشتقة من القضاء، والقضاء هو الحكم، وظاهر أن كل جملة خبرية لا بدَّ أن تتضمن حكماً موجباً أو سالباً.
الكمُّ والكيف في القضايا:
يقصد بالكم في القضية مقدار الأفراد التي ينطبق عليها الحكم الذي تشتمل عليه.
ويقصد بالكيف في القضية حال النسبة فيها هل هي موجبة؟ أو سالبة؟ أي: هل هي مثبتة أو منفية؟.
وكل قضية لا بد أن يتعلق الحكم فيها بمقدار ما من الأفراد، واحداً فما فوق، معيناً أو غير معين، ولا بد أيضاً أن تكون النسبة فيها موجبة أو سالبة.
إذن فمن عوارض القضايا الكم والكيف.
أقسام القضايا باعتبار الكم:
ثم إذا نظرنا إلى جانب الكم في القضايا وجدنا أن المحكوم عليه فيها إما أن يكون معيناً مخصوصاً غير قابل للشركة، وفي هذه الحالة تسمى القضية: (شخصية) وتسمى: (مخصوصة) وإما أن يكون كلياً غير معين، أي: قابلاً للشركة المفتوحة دون تحديد، وعندئذٍ فله احتمالات ثلاثة:
الأول: أن يكون مسوَّراً بسورٍ كلي، أي: مقترناً بما يدلُّ على أن الحكم فيها يشمل جميع أفراد الكلي، وفي هذه الحالة تسمى القضية (كُلِّيَّة).
والكُلِّيَّة لدى التحقيق هي، في قوة المخصوصة، أو نقول: إن المخصوصة هي في قوة الكلية، وذلك لأن انطباق الحكم على كل الأفراد في القضية الكلية بقوة تكرير الحكم ونسبته إلى كل فرد بخصوصه، وذلك بعدد الأفراد مهما كثرت.
الثاني: أن يكون مسوَّراً بسورٍ غير كُلِّي، أي: مقترناً بما يدلُّ على أن الحكم يشمل بعض أفراد الكُلِّي فقط دون تعيين لأفراد هذا البعض، وربما يكون البعض هو الأكثر في عدد الأفراد من البعض الذي لا يشمله الحكم، وحينما يكون المحكوم عليه مسوراً بسورٍ غير كُلِّي تسمى القضية (جزئية).
الثالث: أن يكون غير مسوَّرٍ بأي سور، أي: غير مقترن بما يدلُّ على شمول الحكم لكل الأفراد أو لبعض الأفراد من غير تعيين، وفي هذه الحالة تسمى القضية (مهملة).
والقضية المهملة لدى التحقيق هي في قوة القضية الجزئية وهي المسوَّرة بسور غير كُلِّي، وذلك لأن المهملة التي ليس فيها ما يدلُّ على الإحاطة الجزئية تدلُّ بطبيعتها على ذلك، فذكر السور الجزئي لا يزيد في معنى الإحاطة الجزئية شيئاً، وإن كان قد يزيد في نوع الدلالة، إذا كان فيه معنى الأقل أو الأكثر، أو كان فيه تحديد نسبة البعض بالنصف أو الثلث أو الربع أو نحو ذلك من غير تعيين أشخاص الأفراد، أما لفظ بعض وما أشبهه فليس فيه مزيد دلالة، لذلك فالمهملة تلازم الجزئية لأنها في قوتها.
فالقضايا إذن أربع:
١ - الشخصية (= المخصوصة).
٢ - الكُلِّيَّة.
وهما متماثلتان في القوة.
٣ - الجزئية.
٤ - المهملة.
وهما متماثلتان في القوة.

The Four Types of Propositions by Quantity
Author classifies propositions by quantity and equates indefinite with particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any proposition by its quantity (personal, universal, particular, indefinite) and explain why the indefinite is logically equivalent to the particular.

Core Idea
A proposition without a quantifier still indicates partial coverage, just like 'some'.

In Islamic logic, propositions are quantified to show how much of the subject is covered by the judgment. This lesson breaks down the four types of propositions by quantity (personal, universal, particular, indefinite) and clarifies a key point: the indefinite proposition—which has no quantifier—is logically equivalent to the particular. This insight is crucial when analyzing arguments in fiqh, kalam, and usul al-fiqh where quantifiers are often implicit.

Guided Path
1
Check for a quantifier
Look for any word that indicates coverage, such as 'kull' (every), 'ba'd' (some), or 'jamī' (all). If a quantifier is present, identify whether it is universal or non-universal.
2
Classify universal and particular
If the quantifier is universal (e.g., 'kull'), the proposition is universal (kulliyya). If the quantifier is non-universal (e.g., 'ba'd'), it is particular (juz'iyya). Note that personal propositions (shakhsiyya) have a proper name as subject, making them definite but not universal in scope.
3
Handle the indefinite proposition
If there is no quantifier at all, the proposition is indefinite (muhmala). According to the author, the indefinite naturally indicates partial coverage, so it is logically equivalent to a particular proposition. For example, 'Scholars are poets' (indefinite) means the same as 'Some scholars are poets' (particular).
4
Verify the equivalence
Test the equivalence by noting that adding a particular quantifier like 'some' does not change the logical force—the judgment still covers an unspecified part of the subject. Therefore, indefinite and particular are interchangeable in logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون البعض هو الأكثر في عدد الأفراد من البعض الذي لا يشمله الحكم،
وحينما يكون المحكوم عليه مسوراً بسورٍ غير كُلِّي تسمى القضية (جزئية).
الثالث: أن يكون غير مسوَّرٍ بأي سور، أي: غير مقترن بما يدلُّ على
شمول الحكم لكل الأفراد أو لبعض الأفراد من غير تعيين، وفي هذه الحالة
تسمى القضية (مهملة).
والقضية المهملة لدى التحقيق هي في قوة القضية الجزئية وهي
المسوَّرة بسور غير كُلِّي، وذلك لأن المهملة التي ليس فيها ما يدلُّ على
الإحاطة الجزئية تدلُّ بطبيعتها على ذلك، فذكر السور الجزئي لا يزيد في
معنى الإحاطة الجزئية شيئاً، وإن كان قد يزيد في نوع الدلالة، إذا كان فيه
معنى الأقل أو الأكثر، أو كان فيه تحديد نسبة البعض بالنصف أو الثلث أو
الربع أو نحو ذلك من غير تعيين أشخاص الأفراد، أما لفظ بعض وما أشبهه
فليس فيه مزيد دلالة، لذلك فالمهملة تلازم الجزئية لأنها في قوتها.
فالقضايا إذن أربع :
١ - الشخصية (= المخصوصة).
٢ - الكُلِّيَّة .
وهما متماثلتان في القوة.
٣ - الجزئية .
٤ - المهملة .
وهما متماثلتان في القوة.
The particular (al-ba'd) is more in number of individuals than the particular that the judgment does not include. When the subject is quantified by a non-universal quantifier, the proposition is called 'particular' (juz'iyya). Third: that it is not quantified by any quantifier, i.e., not accompanied by what indicates coverage of the judgment over all individuals or some individuals without specification; in this case, the proposition is called 'indefinite' (muhmala). The indefinite proposition, upon investigation, has the force of the particular proposition that is quantified by a non-universal quantifier. This is because the indefinite, which has nothing indicating particular coverage, indicates that by its nature. So mentioning the particular quantifier does not add anything to the meaning of particular coverage, although it may increase the type of indication, if it includes the meaning of 'less' or 'more', or if it specifies a proportion like half, third, quarter, and so on without specifying individual persons. As for the word 'some' (ba'd) and the like, there is no additional indication. Therefore, the indefinite entails the particular because it is in its force. Thus, the propositions are four: 1. Personal (= particularized), 2. Universal, and these two are equal in force. 3. Particular, 4. Indefinite, and these two are equal in force.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage deals with the classification of propositions by quantity (kam). It first clarifies the nature of the particular proposition (juz'iyya) as having a non-universal quantifier. Then it defines the indefinite proposition (muhmala) as having no quantifier at all. The author argues that the indefinite is logically equivalent to the particular because even without an explicit quantifier, it naturally indicates partial coverage; adding a quantifier like 'some' does not change the meaning of partial coverage, though it may add nuances like specifying a proportion (e.g., half, third). Hence, the four types of propositions by quantity are: personal (shakhsiyya, also called makhsusa), universal (kulliyya), particular (juz'iyya), and indefinite (muhmala). The personal and universal are equal in force (both are definite in scope), while the particular and indefinite are equal in force (both indicate partial coverage). For example, 'Some scholars are poets' (particular) and 'Scholars are poets' (indefinite) are equivalent in logical force.

قضية جزئية

Propositions

Classifying Propositions by Quality and Quantity
Author classifies propositions by quality and then combines with quantity to generate eight types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the quality and quantity of any Islamic proposition and determine its type among the eight logical categories.

Core Idea
Eight proposition types arise from crossing two qualities with four quantities.

In the previous lesson you learned the four quantity types: personal, universal, particular, and indefinite. Now we add quality: affirmative or negative. By combining them, we get eight distinct proposition types. This classification is the backbone of logical analysis in Islamic sciences, because every claim in fiqh, kalam, or usul al-fiqh falls into one of these eight categories. Let's see how to navigate them.

Guided Path
1
Determine the quality
Read the proposition and ask: Is the relation (nisba) affirmed or denied? If there is a negation tool (e.g., 'laysa', 'la'), it is negative (saliba); otherwise, it is affirmative (mujiba).
Negation tools are typically 'ليس', 'لا', 'ما' etc.
2
Determine the quantity
Examine the subject term. Is it a proper noun (personal), includes every individual (universal), some individuals (particular), or unspecified (indefinite)? Use the criteria from the previous lesson.
Quantifiers like 'كل' (every) indicate universal; 'بعض' (some) indicates particular.
3
Name the type
Combine quality and quantity. For example: universal + affirmative = universal affirmative; particular + negative = particular negative. There are eight combinations in total.
The tree diagram in the text shows all eight: personal affirmative/negative, universal affirmative/negative, particular affirmative/negative, indefinite affirmative/negative.
4
Apply to a categorical and conditional example
Test both a categorical proposition (e.g., 'كل مسكر حرام' – universal affirmative) and a conditional one (e.g., 'إن كان الشيء مسكرا فهو حرام' – also universal affirmative conditional). The eightfold division covers all proposition types.
The author explicitly states that this division includes categorical, conjunctive conditional, and disjunctive conditional propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام القضايا باعتبار الكيف:
وإذا نظرنا إلى جانب الكيف في القضايا وجدنا أن النَّسب فيها إما أن
تكون موجبة، وذلك إذا كانت هذه النسب خالية عن أدوات السلب (النفي).
وإما أن تكون سالبة، وذلك إذا كانت هذه النَّسب منفية بتسليط أداة من أدوات
السلب عليها، فهي إذن باعتبار الكيف قسمان:
١ - موجبة.
٢ - سالبة.
أقسام القضايا باعتبار الكم والكيف معاً:
وحينما نلاحظ أقسام القضايا باعتبار الكم - وهي الأقسام الأربعة التي أوضحناها - ونضم إليها قسمي الكيف نجد أن القضايا قد ارتقت أقسامها إلى ثمانية، لأن كل قسم من أقسامها باعتبار الكم ينقسم إلى قسميها باعتبار الكيف، أو نقول بعبارة أخرى: إن كل قسم من قسميها باعتبار الكيف ينقسم إلى أقسامها الأربعة باعتبار الكم، فتكون القضايا إذن كما يلي:

|

| | | |
