موجبة سالبة موجبة سالبة موجبة سالبة موجبة سالبة
وهذا التقسيم يشمل كل أنواع القضايا الآتية، وهي: الحملية، والشرطية المتصلة، والشرطية المنفصلة.
Sections of Propositions by Quality: When we look at the aspect of quality in propositions, we find that the relations in them are either affirmative, when these relations are free from tools of negation, or negative, when these relations are negated by applying a tool of negation to them. So they are, by quality, two divisions: 1. Affirmative. 2. Negative.
Sections of Propositions by Quantity and Quality Together: When we observe the sections of propositions by quantity—which are the four divisions we have explained—and join to them the two divisions by quality, we find that the propositions have increased their divisions to eight, because each division by quantity is divided into the two divisions by quality, or in other words: each of the two divisions by quality is divided into the four divisions by quantity. So the propositions are as follows: [tree diagram] And this division includes all the following types of propositions: categorical, conditional conjunctive, and conditional disjunctive.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage first introduces the classification of propositions by quality (kayf). A proposition is affirmative (mujiba) if its relation is free of negation, and negative (saliba) if a negation tool is applied to it. Then it combines quality with the previously established four quantity types to produce eight proposition types. The author explains that either each quantity type branches into two qualities, or each quality branches into four quantities—both give eight. A tree diagram illustrates the eight types: personal affirmative/negative, universal affirmative/negative, particular affirmative/negative, indefinite affirmative/negative. Finally, the author notes that this eightfold division applies to all three kinds of propositions: categorical (hamliyya), conditional conjunctive (shartiyya muttasila), and conditional disjunctive (shartiyya munfasila). For example, a universal affirmative categorical proposition like 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' fits under the universal affirmative branch.

Key Takeaway
Every proposition in Islamic logic has both a quality (affirmative or negative) and a quantity (personal, universal, particular, or indefinite); their combination yields exactly eight types.

Determination of the Subject in Personal Propositions
Author explains how the subject is determined in a personal proposition, using proper names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject in a personal proposition and recognize its determination through proper names, including singular, dual, and plural forms.

Core Idea
In personal propositions, the subject is determined by being a proper name, not a description.

In the study of propositions (qadaya), logicians distinguish between personal propositions (shakhsiyya or makhsusa) and quantified ones. The subject in a personal proposition is a specific individual or group designated by a proper name ('alam ism). This lesson examines the first method of determination (ta'yin) as explained by the author: the subject can be a singular, dual, or plural proper name. Understanding this is crucial for correctly analyzing arguments that involve specific individuals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition as personal
Determine if the proposition is about a specific individual or group rather than a general category. Look for a proper name as subject.
2
Locate the proper name
Find the subject that is a proper name ('alam ism), which could be singular, dual, or plural.
3
Verify the number and form
Check if the proper name is singular (e.g., Khalid), dual (e.g., al-Zaydan), or plural (e.g., al-Muhammadun) as per the examples.
Dual and plural proper names take definite articles and special endings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التعيين في المخصوصة:
يتعين المحكوم عليه في القضية المخصوصة (الشخصية) بأمور متعددة، منها ما يلي:
أ - أن يكون علماً اسماً، سواء أكان مفرداً أو مثنى أو مجموعاً، مثل: (خالد - سعيد - الزيدان - العمران - المحمدون).
فخالد قائد، والزيدان نحويان، والمحمدون حكماء، هذه قضايا
Determination (ta'yin) in the particularized (personal) proposition: The subject in the particularized (personal) proposition is determined by multiple matters, among which are: A - That it be a proper name, whether singular, dual, or plural, such as: (Khalid - Sa'id - the two Zayds - the two Amrs - the Muhammads). So 'Khalid is a leader', 'the two Zayds are grammarians', and 'the Muhammads are wise men' are propositions.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage discusses how the subject is specified (ta'yin) in a personal (makhsusa/shakhsiyya) proposition. The first method mentioned is that the subject is a proper noun ('alam ism). This proper noun can be singular (e.g., Khalid, Sa'id), dual (e.g., al-Zaydan, al-Amran), or plural (e.g., al-Muhammadun). The examples given illustrate each: 'Khalid is a leader' (singular proper subject), 'the two Zayds are grammarians' (dual), and 'the Muhammads are wise men' (plural proper subject). This shows that personal propositions are not limited to singular subjects; they can have dual or plural proper names as subjects, as long as they refer to specific individuals designated by those names.

Key Terms
تعيين (ta'yin): The specification of the subject in a personal proposition, e.g., by using a proper name.
مخصوصة (makhsusa): Synonym for personal proposition (shakhsiyya), where the subject is a specific individual or group designated by a proper name.
علم اسم ('alam ism): A proper noun that designates a specific person or thing.

Key Takeaway
The subject of a personal proposition is determined by a proper name, which can be singular, dual, or plural, and this determination is essential for logical analysis.

Propositions by Quality: Affirmative and Negative
Author divides propositions by quality into affirmative and negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between affirmative and negative propositions and classify any given proposition correctly.

Core Idea
Every proposition is either affirmative or negative — there is no third option.

This lesson introduces the most basic division of propositions: by quality. Understanding this distinction is essential for evaluating the truth conditions of statements and for more advanced logical operations like conversion and contradiction. We will learn to identify whether a proposition affirms or denies a predicate of its subject.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition's subject and predicate
Every proposition has a subject (mawdu') that something is said about, and a predicate (mahmul) that is affirmed or denied of it. For example, in 'Zayd is generous', 'Zayd' is the subject and 'generous' is the predicate.
In Arabic, subject and predicate often have case endings (raf' for subject, nasb for predicate in non-verbal sentences).
2
Check for a negation word
Look for words like 'laysa' (not), 'ma' (not), 'la' (no) that indicate denial. If the predicate is denied of the subject, the proposition is negative (saliba). If no negation is present, it is affirmative (mujiba).
Negation may be explicit or implicit; in logic, we consider the logical form.
3
Classify the proposition as affirmative or negative
If the predicate is affirmed, it is affirmative. Example: 'Every scholar is knowledgeable.' If denied, it is negative. Example: 'No man is a stone.'
Both universal and particular propositions can be affirmative or negative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السلب عليها، فهي إذن باعتبار الكيف قسمان: ١ - موجبة. ٢ - سالبة.
Negation [applies] to them, so they are, therefore, by quality two divisions: 1 - affirmative. 2 - negative.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage defines the division of propositions by quality (kayf). The author states that, after considering negation, propositions fall into two types: affirmative (mujiba) and negative (saliba). This is a fundamental classification in logic: every proposition either affirms a predicate of a subject or negates it. For example, 'Zayd is standing' is affirmative; 'Zayd is not standing' is negative.

Key Terms
قضية (qadiyya): a proposition
كيف (kayf): quality
موجبة (mujiba): affirmative proposition
سالبة (saliba): negative proposition

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a negative proposition?
A) Every scholar is knowledgeable.
B) No man is a stone.
C) Some animals are horses.
D) Zayd is generous.

Key Takeaway
The first step in analyzing any logical statement is to determine its quality: is it affirmative or negative?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following statements from Islamic texts as affirmative or negative: (a) 'Allah is One.' (b) 'No partner is to Him.' (c) 'The Prayer is obligatory.' (d) 'Muhammad is not a liar.'
?
Write one affirmative and one negative proposition about a topic in Fiqh (e.g., prayer, fasting, charity).

The Eight Types of Propositions by Quantity and Quality
Author combines quantity and quality divisions to generate eight proposition types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate and identify the eight types of propositions by combining the four quantity divisions with the two quality divisions.

Core Idea
Four quantities times two qualities equals eight proposition types.

In the previous lesson, we learned that propositions are divided by quantity into universal, particular, indefinite, and singular, and by quality into affirmative and negative. Now we bring these two divisions together to generate eight distinct types. This comprehensive classification is foundational for understanding logical reasoning in Islamic sciences, as it provides the building blocks for constructing valid arguments.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four quantity divisions
List the four types of propositions by quantity: universal (كلية), particular (جزئية), indefinite (مهملة), and singular (شخصية or مخصوصة).
Singular is also called 'makhsusa' in the text.
2
Recall the two quality divisions
List the two types by quality: affirmative (موجبة) and negative (سالبة).
3
Multiply quantity by quality
Each of the four quantity divisions branches into affirmative and negative. This gives 4 x 2 = 8 proposition types.
Alternatively, each quality division branches into the four quantities, giving the same result.
4
Enumerate the eight types
Write out the eight combinations: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative, indefinite affirmative, indefinite negative, singular affirmative, singular negative.
Memorize this list thoroughly as it appears in many logic texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحينما نلاحظ أقسام القضايا باعتبار الكم - وهي الأقسام الأربعة التي أوضحناها - ونضم إليها قسمي الكيف نجد أن القضايا قد ارتقت أقسامها إلى ثمانية، لأن كل قسم من أقسامها باعتبار الكم ينقسم إلى قسميها باعتبار الكيف، أو نقول بعبارة أخرى: إن كل قسم من قسميها باعتبار الكيف ينقسم إلى أقسامها الأربعة باعتبار الكم، فتكون القضايا إذن كما يلي: القضية تنقسم إلى: شخصية أو مخصوصة، كلية، جزئية، مهملة. وكل من هذه ينقسم إلى موجبة وسالبة. وهذا التقسيم يشمل كل أنواع القضايا الآتية، وهي: الحملية، والشرطية المتصلة، والشرطية المنفصلة.
And when we observe the divisions of propositions by quantity — which are the four divisions we explained — and join to them the two divisions of quality, we find that the divisions of propositions rise to eight, because each of their divisions by quantity divides into its two divisions by quality, or in other words: each of their two divisions by quality divides into its four divisions by quantity. Thus the propositions are as follows: The proposition divides into: singular or particular, universal, particular, indefinite. And each of these divides into affirmative and negative. And this division includes all the following types of propositions: categorical, conditional conjunctive, and conditional disjunctive.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage explains how the four quantity divisions (universal, particular, indefinite, singular) combine with the two quality divisions (affirmative, negative) to produce eight types of propositions. The author emphasizes that this classification applies to categorical propositions as well as to conditional conjunctive and disjunctive propositions. The eight types are: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative, indefinite affirmative, indefinite negative, singular affirmative, singular negative. This is a comprehensive enumeration that a student must memorize.

Key Takeaway
By combining the four quantity divisions with the two quality divisions, we obtain eight exhaustive proposition types that apply to categorical and conditional propositions alike.

Then provide a fiqh example for each type (e.g., 'All intoxicants are haram' for universal affirmative, 'No intoxicant is halal' for universal negative, 'Some animals are halal' for particular affirmative, 'Some animals are not halal' for particular negative, 'Intoxicants are haram' (indefinite) for indefinite affirmative, 'Intoxicants are not halal' for indefinite negative, 'This wine is haram' for singular affirmative, 'This wine is not halal' for singular negative).

Five Ways to Specify the Subject in Singular Propositions
Author enumerates five methods for identifying the subject in singular propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the five methods of specification (ism, kunya, laqab, al of covenant, demonstrative) is used in a given singular proposition from Islamic texts.

Core Idea
Singular propositions can have plural subjects but remain singular when the term is restricted to specific individuals.

In Islamic logic, a singular proposition (makhsusa) is one where the subject refers to a specific entity or group. Understanding how to identify such propositions is crucial for correct syllogistic reasoning. The author provides five clear criteria for determining when a proposition is singular, ranging from proper names to demonstratives.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the five categories
The author lists five ways to specify the subject: (a) proper name (ism), (b) patronymic (kunya), (c) nickname (laqab), (d) noun defined by 'al' of covenant, (e) demonstrative pronoun referring to a specific entity.
Each category yields a subject that denotes a concrete individual or set of individuals, not a universal.
2
Identify if the subject is a proper noun (ism)
Check if the subject is a proper name like 'Khalid', 'Sa'id', 'al-Zaydan' (dual), or 'al-Muhammaddun' (plural). These directly indicate specific persons.
Plural proper names like 'al-Muhammaddun' are still singular in reference because they refer to a specific group.
3
Check for kunya or laqab
A kunya starts with 'Abu' or 'Umm' (e.g., 'Abu Bakr'), while a laqab is a descriptive nickname (e.g., 'Badr al-Din'). Both specify a particular individual.
These are treated similarly to proper names in logic.
4
Look for 'al' of covenant or demonstrative pronouns
If the subject has 'al' with a specific referent (e.g., 'al-imam' meaning a known imam) or is a demonstrative like 'hatha' (this), it is singular provided the reference is definite.
The 'al' of covenant is distinct from 'al' for genus or universal.
5
Confirm the proposition is singular by testing specificity
A proposition is singular if the subject term applies only to one individual or to a specific group that cannot be shared with others outside that group. If the term could apply to anyone with the description, it is universal.
For example, 'al-Muhammaddun' refers only to those named Muhammad in a specific context, not to all named Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يتعين المحكوم عليه في القضية المخصوصة (الشخصية) بأمور متعددة، منها ما يلي: أ - أن يكون علماً اسماً، سواء أكان مفرداً أو مثنى أو مجموعاً، مثل: (خالد - سعيد - الزيدان - العمران - المحمدون). فخالد قائد، والزيدان نحويان، والمحمدون حكماء. ب ـ أن يكون علماً كُنيةً، مثل: (أبو بكر ـ أبو حفص). جـ ـ أن يكون علماً لقباً، مثل: (بدر الدين ـ إمام الحرمين ـ زين العابدين). د ـ أن يكون معرَّفاً بـ (أل) التي للعهد، سواء أكان المعهود المعين فرداً أو أفراداً مخصوصين. هـ ـ أن يكون اسم إشارة، مثل: (هذا ـ هذان ـ هؤلاء) بشرط أن يكون المشار إليه معيناً، سواء أكان فرداً أو أفراداً. وقد وضح لدينا أنه لا فرق في التعيين بين أن يكون المعين فرداً واحداً أو أفراداً، لأن الأفراد المعينين هم بمثابة الفرد الواحد المعين من جهة كون اللفظ الدال عليهم منحصراً فيهم وحدهم، غير قابل للشركة التي تستدعيها صفات كلية حيثما وجدت أطلق على ما توجد فيه لفظ الكلي.
The subject in the particular (singular) proposition is determined by multiple matters, including: a - It is a proper name, whether singular, dual, or plural, such as: (Khalid - Sa'id - al-Zaydan - al-'Umaran - al-Muhammaddun). So 'Khalid is a leader', 'al-Zaydan are grammarians', 'al-Muhammaddun are wise men'. b - It is a kunya (patronymic) name, like: (Abu Bakr - Abu Hafs). c - It is a nickname, like: (Badr al-Din - Imam al-Haramayn - Zayn al-'Abidin). d - It is defined by (al) of covenant, whether the covenanted specific thing is an individual or specific individuals. e - It is a demonstrative pronoun, like: (this - these two - these) on condition that the indicated thing is specific, whether an individual or individuals. And it has become clear to us that there is no difference in specification between the specific being a single individual or individuals, because specific individuals are like the single specific individual in terms of the word indicating them being confined to them alone, not susceptible to the commonality that universal attributes require wherever they exist the word 'universal' is applied to that in which they exist.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage explains how to determine whether a proposition is singular (makhsusa) by listing five criteria for the subject: (a) a proper name (ism), which can be singular, dual, or plural; (b) a kunya (patronymic like Abu Bakr); (c) a nickname (laqab like Badr al-Din); (d) a noun defined with 'al' of covenant (i.e., specific reference); (e) a demonstrative pronoun (like 'this') referring to a specific entity. The author adds that there is no difference in singularity whether the referent is one individual or multiple specific individuals, because the word is restricted to them and does not apply to others, unlike universal terms. For example, 'al-Muhammaddun' refers to a specific group of people named Muhammad, making the proposition singular despite plurality.

العلم

Key Takeaway
There are five ways to specify the subject in a singular proposition: proper name, kunya, laqab, al of covenant, and demonstrative pronoun; even plural subjects can be singular if the term is restricted to specific individuals.

Introduction to Quantifiers (Aswar): Universal Affirmative and Negative
Author introduces aswar and lists universal affirmative and negative quantifiers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify universal affirmative and negative quantifiers in Arabic propositions and distinguish them from particular quantifiers.

Core Idea
Quantifiers are the keys that unlock whether a statement covers all or some of its subject.

In this lesson, we begin our study of quantifiers (aswar) — the words that tell us the scope of a proposition's subject. Just as a city wall encloses the entire city, a universal quantifier encloses every member of the subject class. We will learn the most common universal quantifiers used by logicians and how to recognize them. This foundational tool is essential for correctly analyzing and forming categorical propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the etymology of 'sor'
The term 'sor' is derived from the wall (sur) around a city. A quantifier 'encloses' the subject just as a wall encloses a city. This metaphor helps us remember that quantifiers indicate the extent (universal or particular) of the subject.
2
Memorize the universal affirmative quantifiers
The passage lists: كل, جميع, عامة, أل الاستغراقية, طراً, قاطبة, كافة, أجمع, أجمعون. Any word that indicates generality and inclusiveness functions as a universal affirmative quantifier.
These are examples; there are more.
3
Memorize the universal negative quantifiers
The passage lists: لا شيء من, لا أحد, لا ديار. Also, any indefinite noun in the context of negation (especially with 'min') becomes universal negative. For example, 'ما جاءني رجل' means 'no man came to me'.
The 'indefinite in negation' rule is important.
4
Practice identifying quantifiers in sample propositions
Given a proposition like 'كل عالم فاضل' (every scholar is virtuous), identify 'كل' as universal affirmative. Given 'لا أحد في البيت' (no one is in the house), identify 'لا أحد' as universal negative.
Try to construct your own examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يستعمل المناطقة للألفاظ التي تدل على الإحاطة الكلية أو الإحاطة الجزئية كلمة: (السور) أخذاً من سور البلد، وهو الجدار الذي كان الناس يبنونه حول البلد للإحاطة بها، ولحفظها من مداهمة العدو، وفيما يلي بيان للأسوار الكلية والجزئية السالبة والموجبة: ١ - السُّور الكُلِّي الموجب يأتي بألفاظ كثيرة منها: (كلّ ـ جميع ـ عامة ـ (أل) الاستغراقية ـ طرّاً ـ قاطبة ـ كافة ـ أجمع ـ أجمعون) وهكذا كل ما يفيد العموم والشمول. ٢ - والسور الكلي السالب يأتي بألفاظ كثيرة منها: (لا شيء من ـ لا أحد ـ لا ديّار) وهكذا كل نكرة في سياق النفي لأن النكرة في سياق النفي تعمّ، فإذا اقترنت بـ (مِنْ) الظاهرة أو المقدرة كانت نصاً في العموم، أما
Logicians use the word 'al-sor' for the expressions that indicate universal coverage or particular coverage, taken from the wall (sur) of a city, which is the wall that people built around the city to surround it and protect it from enemy invasion. Below is a statement of the universal and particular quantifiers, negative and affirmative: 1 - The universal affirmative quantifier comes in many expressions including: 'kull', 'jami'', 'amma', 'al' of inclusiveness, 'tarran', 'qatibatan', 'kafatan', 'ajma', 'ajma'un', and so on, everything that indicates generality and inclusiveness. 2 - And the universal negative quantifier comes in many expressions including: 'la shay'a min', 'la ahad', 'la dayyar', and so on, every indefinite noun in the context of negation because an indefinite in the context of negation is general; if it is connected with 'min' explicit or implicit, it is a clear text in generality. As for
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of 'aswar' (quantifiers), which are words that indicate whether a proposition is universal or particular. The author explains the etymology: 'sor' is derived from the wall around a city, symbolizing that the quantifier encompasses the subject. Then two types of quantifiers are listed: (1) universal affirmative quantifiers, such as 'kull' (every), 'jami'' (all), 'amma' (general), and others; (2) universal negative quantifiers, such as 'la shay'a min' (nothing of), 'la ahad' (no one), and any indefinite noun in a negative context, because negation with an indefinite yields universality. The passage is incomplete as it cuts off before discussing particular quantifiers (affirmative and negative). A student should memorize these quantifiers and be able to identify them in propositions.

Key Terms
السور (al-sor): A quantifier: a word that indicates the quantity of a proposition (universal or particular). [Note: In later logic, sor may also refer to the copula in some contexts, but here it strictly means quantifier.]
السور الكلي الموجب (al-sor al-kulli al-mujib): Universal affirmative quantifier; includes words like 'kull' and 'jami''.
السور الكلي السالب (al-sor al-kulli al-salib): Universal negative quantifier; includes phrases like 'la shay'a min' and any indefinite in a negative context.

Universal quantifiers (affirmative and negative) are essential tools for determining the scope of a proposition; they must be memorized and correctly applied.

The Four Quantifiers in Categorical Propositions
Author categorizes the four quantifiers with expressions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the quantity and quality of any categorical proposition and classify it as one of the four quantifier types.

Core Idea
Every categorical proposition is either universal or particular, and either affirmative or negative.

In logic, the quantifier (sūr) determines how much of the subject is encompassed by the predicate. The source passage lists many Arabic expressions for each of the four types. Mastering these quantifiers is essential for evaluating syllogisms and definitions in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantifier expression
Find the word or phrase that indicates quantity in the proposition. The author lists examples like 'kull', 'jamīʿ', 'baʿḍ', 'lā shay'a min', etc.
Indefinite nouns in a negated context (e.g., 'lā aḥad') also act as universal negative quantifiers.
2
Determine universality or particularity
Check whether the quantifier refers to all individuals (universal) or some individuals (particular) of the subject class.
3
Determine affirmation or negation
Check whether the predicate is affirmed (positive) or denied (negative). This is shown by words of negation like 'laysa', 'lā', or negative particles.
4
Classify into one of four types
Combine the quantity and quality to classify: universal affirmative (A), universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), particular negative (O).
The author explicitly names these four categories.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأسوار في الكلية والجزئية:
يستعمل المناطقة للألفاظ التي تدل على الإحاطة الكلية أو الإحاطة الجزئية كلمة: (السور) أخذاً من سور البلد، وهو الجدار الذي كان الناس يبنونه حول البلد للإحاطة بها، ولحفظها من مداهمة العدو، وفيما يلي بيان للأسوار الكلية والجزئية السالبة والموجبة:
١ - السُّور الكُلِّي الموجب يأتي بألفاظ كثيرة منها: (كلّ ـ جميع ـ عامة ـ (أل) الاستغراقية ـ طرّاً ـ قاطبة ـ كافة ـ أجمع ـ أجمعون) وهكذا كل ما يفيد العموم والشمول.
٢ - والسور الكلي السالب يأتي بألفاظ كثيرة منها: (لا شيء من ـ لا أحد ـ لا ديّار) وهكذا كل نكرة في سياق النفي لأن النكرة في سياق النفي تعمّ، فإذا اقترنت بـ (مِنْ) الظاهرة أو المقدرة كانت نصاً في العموم، أما الظاهرة فنحو: ما جاءني من أحد، وأما المقدرة فنحو: لا أحد في الدار، أي: لا من أحدٍ في الدار.
ومن السور الكلي السالب ورود أداة (كل) وما شابهها على قضية منفية، وكذلك كل قضية جاء فيها السور الكلي قبل أداة السلب التي فيها، نحو: كلُّ إنسان ليس بجماد، وهذا ما يسمى: (عموم السلب).
٣ - والسور الجزئي الموجب يأتي بألفاظ كثيرة منها: (بعض - قسم - فريق - منهم - فئة - طائفة) وهكذا كل ما يفيد الإحاطة الجزئية.
٤ - والسور الجزئي السالب يأتي بألفاظ كثيرة، وتتلخص بأن يجمع في القضية بين سور جزئي وأداة من أدوات النفي، فيوجد في القضية مثلاً: (بعض) و (ليس) سواء تقدم النفي على السور الجزئي أو تأخر؛ ومثل الجمع بين (بعض وليس) الجمع بين أي سور جزئي وبين أي أداة من أدوات النفي، ومن أمثلة ذلك: بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان؛ وليس بعض الحيوان بإنسان.
ويأتي السور الجزئي أيضاً بأن يسبق النفي أداة من أدوات العموم، مثل: (ليس كل) و (ليس جميع) ونحو ذلك، فمثل هذا التعبير يفيد السلب الجزئي لا السلب الكلي وهذا ما يسمى: (سلب العموم) ومن أمثلة ذلك: ليس كل بناء مسجداً - ما كلُّ بيضاء شحمة - ولا كلُّ حمراء لحمة - فلا يمنع أن يكون بعض الأبنية مساجد، وبعض ما هي بيضاء هي شحمة، وبعض ما هي حمراء هي لحمة.
التحقيق في عموم السلب وسلب العموم:
إذا تقدمت أداة العموم على أداة السلب في القضية أفادت عموم السلب كما ذكرنا آنفاً؛ أي: أفادت أن النفي في القضية مسلط على كل فردٍ من أفراد الكلي.
وإذا تقدمت أداة السلب على أداة العموم في القضية أفادت سلب العموم، كما ذكرنا آنفاً أيضاً، أي: أفادت أن النفي في القضية مسلط على
The Quantifiers in Universality and Particularity: Logicians use the word 'quantifier' (as-sūr) for expressions that indicate universal encompassment or particular encompassment, taken from the wall of a city, which is the wall that people used to build around the city to encompass it and protect it from enemy assault. Below is an explanation of the universal and particular, negative and affirmative quantifiers: 1. The universal affirmative quantifier comes with many expressions, including: 'every', 'all', 'general', the inclusive 'alif lām', 'entirely', 'altogether', 'all together', 'the whole', 'all of them', and so on, everything that conveys generality and inclusiveness. 2. The universal negative quantifier comes with many expressions, including: 'nothing from', 'no one', 'no inhabitant', and so on, every indefinite noun in the context of negation, because the indefinite in the context of negation is general. If it is coupled with explicit or implied 'min', it is textual in indicating generality. As for the explicit: like 'no one came to me' (mā jā'anī min aḥad). As for the implied: like 'no one is in the house' (lā aḥada fī d-dār), i.e., 'not any one is in the house'. From the universal negative quantifier is the occurrence of the tool 'every' and its like on a negated proposition, as well as every proposition in which the universal quantifier precedes the tool of negation in it, like: 'Every human is not an inanimate object' (kullu insānin laysa bi-jamād). This is called: 'universality of negation' (ʿumūm as-salb). 3. The particular affirmative quantifier comes with many expressions, including: 'some', 'a portion', 'a group', 'among them', 'a faction', 'a party', and so on, everything that conveys particular encompassment. 4. The particular negative quantifier comes with many expressions, and its summary is that a particular quantifier and a tool of negation are combined in the proposition. For example, 'some' and 'not' appear in the proposition, whether the negation precedes the particular quantifier or follows it. The combination of 'some' and 'not' is like the combination of any particular quantifier and any tool of negation. Examples of that: 'Some animal is not a human' (baʿḍu l-ḥayawāni laysa bi-insān); 'Not some animal is a human' (laysa baʿḍu l-ḥayawāni bi-insān). The particular negative also comes by the negation preceding a tool of universality, like: 'not every' (laysa kull), 'not all' (laysa jamīʿ), and similar. Such an expression conveys particular negation, not universal negation. This is called: 'negation of universality' (salb al-ʿumūm). Examples: 'Not every building is a mosque' (laysa kullu binā'in masjidan); 'Not every white thing is fat' (mā kullu bayḍā'a shaḥma); 'Nor every red thing is meat' (wa lā kullu ḥamrā'a laḥma). So it is not prevented that some buildings are mosques, some white things are fat, and some red things are meat. The verification concerning universality of negation and negation of universality: If the tool of universality precedes the tool of negation in the proposition, it conveys universality of negation, as we mentioned above; i.e., it conveys that negation in the proposition is applied to every individual of the universal. If the tool of negation precedes the tool of universality in the proposition, it conveys negation of universality, as we also mentioned above; i.e., it conveys that negation in the proposition is applied to
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of the quantifiers (aswār) used in categorical propositions. The author begins by explaining the etymology of the term 'sūr', comparing it to the wall that surrounds a city, because the quantifier 'encompasses' the subject of the proposition. Then the author enumerates four types: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. For each, a list of Arabic expressions is provided. The universal affirmative includes words like kull, jamīʿ, etc., which indicate that the predicate applies to every member of the subject class. The universal negative includes phrases like lā shay'a min (nothing from), lā aḥad (no one), and importantly, any indefinite noun in a negated context (nakira fī siyāq an-nafy) becomes universal due to the generalizing force of negation. The author clarifies that if such an indefinite is accompanied by the particle min (explicitly or implicitly), it is explicitly universal in meaning. An example: 'No one came to me' means 'Not any person came'. The author also notes that if a universal quantifier like kull comes before a negation in a proposition, it yields 'universality of negation' (ʿumūm as-salb), i.e., every individual is negated. Example: 'Every human is not an inanimate object' means no human is inanimate. The particular affirmative quantifier uses words like baʿḍ (some), qism (a portion), etc., indicating that the predicate applies to at least one individual. The particular negative is formed by combining a particular quantifier with a negation, e.g., 'Some animal is not a human' (baʿḍu l-ḥayawāni laysa bi-insān) or 'Not some animal is a human' (laysa baʿḍu l-ḥayawāni bi-insān). Both mean that there exists an animal that is not a human. The author also explains that when negation precedes a universal quantifier, as in 'Not every building is a mosque' (laysa kullu binā'in masjidan), the result is 'negation of universality' (salb al-ʿumūm) — i.e., it denies that all buildings are mosques, not that no building is a mosque. This is a particular negative. The author gives illustrative examples from everyday life (white things are not always fat, red things are not always meat). Finally, the author provides a verification (taḥqīq) of the two cases: when the universal quantifier precedes the negation, it gives universality of negation; when the negation precedes the universal quantifier, it gives negation of universality. This distinction is crucial in logic to avoid ambiguity in propositional scope.

Key Terms
السور
السور الكلي الموجب
السور الكلي السالب
السور الجزئي الموجب
السور الجزئي السالب

C) ليس كل بناء مسجداً (Not every building is a mosque)

Key Takeaway
Master the four quantifier types (A, E, I, O) as they are the foundation for logical reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Distinguishing عموم السلب from سلب العموم
Author explains and distinguishes عموم السلب and سلب العموم

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'عموم السلب' (universality of negation) and 'سلب العموم' (negation of universality) based on word order and explain the logical difference using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
Word order changes meaning: quantifier before negation = all are negated; negation before quantifier = not all are affirmed.

The passage introduces two critical logical concepts: universality of negation (ʿumūm as-salb) and negation of universality (salb al-ʿumūm). Though similar, they have opposite scopes. Understanding this distinction prevents misinterpretation of propositions in theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the order of quantifier and negation
Look at the proposition and see whether the universal quantifier (like kull, jamīʿ) comes before the negation (like laysa, lā) or after it.
If the quantifier is first, it is 'عموم السلب'; if negation is first, it is 'سلب العموم'.
2
Interpret 'عموم السلب'
When the quantifier precedes negation, the negation applies to every individual of the subject. For example, 'Every human is not an inanimate object' means no human is inanimate.
This form is logically equivalent to the universal negative (E).
3
Interpret 'سلب العموم'
When negation precedes the quantifier, it denies that the predicate applies to all individuals. For example, 'Not every building is a mosque' means some buildings are not mosques, but some may be.
This form is logically equivalent to the particular negative (O).
4
Test with Islamic examples
Take a statement like 'Not every action is created by God' (laysa kullu fiʿlin makhluqan lillah). This is 'سلب العموم' and allows that some actions are created by God. If it were 'عموم السلب' ('Every action is not created by God'), it would deny any divine creation of actions.

Source Text

The combination of 'some' and 'not' is like the combination of any particular quantifier and any tool of negation. Examples of that: 'Some animal is not a human' (baʿḍu l-ḥayawāni laysa bi-insān); 'Not some animal is a human' (laysa baʿḍu l-ḥayawāni bi-insān). The particular negative also comes by the negation preceding a tool of universality, like: 'not every' (laysa kull), 'not all' (laysa jamīʿ), and similar. Such an expression conveys particular negation, not universal negation. This is called: 'negation of universality' (salb al-ʿumūm). Examples: 'Not every building is a mosque' (laysa kullu binā'in masjidan); 'Not every white thing is fat' (mā kullu bayḍā'a shaḥma); 'Nor every red thing is meat' (wa lā kullu ḥamrā'a laḥma). So it is not prevented that some buildings are mosques, some white things are fat, and some red things are meat. The verification concerning universality of negation and negation of universality: If the tool of universality precedes the tool of negation in the proposition, it conveys universality of negation, as we mentioned above; i.e., it conveys that negation in the proposition is applied to every individual of the universal. If the tool of negation precedes the tool of universality in the proposition, it conveys negation of universality, as we also mentioned above; i.e., it conveys that negation in the proposition is applied to
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of the quantifiers (aswār) used in categorical propositions. The author begins by explaining the etymology of the term 'sūr', comparing it to the wall that surrounds a city, because the quantifier 'encompasses' the subject of the proposition. Then the author enumerates four types: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. For each, a list of Arabic expressions is provided. The universal affirmative includes words like kull, jamīʿ, etc., which indicate that the predicate applies to every member of the subject class. The universal negative includes phrases like lā shay'a min (nothing from), lā aḥad (no one), and importantly, any indefinite noun in a negated context (nakira fī siyāq an-nafy) becomes universal due to the generalizing force of negation. The author clarifies that if such an indefinite is accompanied by the particle min (explicitly or implicitly), it is explicitly universal in meaning. An example: 'No one came to me' means 'Not any person came'. The author also notes that if a universal quantifier like kull comes before a negation in a proposition, it yields 'universality of negation' (ʿumūm as-salb), i.e., every individual is negated. Example: 'Every human is not an inanimate object' means no human is inanimate. The particular affirmative quantifier uses words like baʿḍ (some), qism (a portion), etc., indicating that the predicate applies to at least one individual. The particular negative is formed by combining a particular quantifier with a negation, e.g., 'Some animal is not a human' (baʿḍu l-ḥayawāni laysa bi-insān) or 'Not some animal is a human' (laysa baʿḍu l-ḥayawāni bi-insān). Both mean that there exists an animal that is not a human. The author also explains that when negation precedes a universal quantifier, as in 'Not every building is a mosque' (laysa kullu binā'in masjidan), the result is 'negation of universality' (salb al-ʿumūm) — i.e., it denies that all buildings are mosques, not that no building is a mosque. This is a particular negative. The author gives illustrative examples from everyday life (white things are not always fat, red things are not always meat). Finally, the author provides a verification (taḥqīq) of the two cases: when the universal quantifier precedes the negation, it gives universality of negation; when the negation precedes the universal quantifier, it gives negation of universality. This distinction is crucial in logic to avoid ambiguity in propositional scope.

Key Terms
عموم السلب
سلب العموم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) ليس كل بناء مسجداً (Not every building is a mosque)
D) كل شيء ليس بخالد (Everything is not eternal)

Key Takeaway
Watch the order: quantifier first = universal negation; negation first = particular negation.

Universal Negative Quantifier and 'Umum al-Salb
Defines universal negative quantifier and introduces 'عموم السلب'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish explicit and implicit universal negative propositions and explain the concept of 'umum al-salb.

Core Idea
When 'كل' precedes negation, negation covers every individual – that is 'umum al-salb.

This passage defines the universal negative quantifier and its two forms: explicit and implicit negation. You will learn how to recognize these forms and understand the key term 'umum al-salb, which is essential for analyzing proposition quantity and quality in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish explicit vs. implicit negation
Look at the two examples: 'ما جاءني من أحد' (explicit 'ما') and 'لا أحد في الدار' (implicit 'لا' as part of 'لا أحد'). Identify the difference in how negation is expressed.
Explicit negation uses a separate negative particle; implicit negation uses the quantifier itself to carry negation.
2
Recognize the universal negative quantifier structure
The passage states that when 'كل' occurs on a negated proposition, or when the universal quantifier precedes the negation tool, it is a universal negative quantifier. Example: 'كل إنسان ليس بجماد'.
The universal quantifier 'كل' is followed by the negation 'ليس'.
3
Define 'umum al-salb'
This term means the negation applies to every individual of the subject. Confirm that in 'كل إنسان ليس بجماد', the negation 'ليس' applies to every human, making it a universal negative.
Contrast with 'سلب العموم' (negation of universality), where the universal is negated, e.g., 'ليس كل إنسان بكاتب' (Not every human is a writer).
4
Apply to new examples
Given a proposition like 'كل عالم ليس بجاهل' (Every scholar is not ignorant), identify it as 'umum al-salb because the universal quantifier precedes negation.
Check that the negation covers each individual scholar.

الظاهرة فنحو: ما جاءني من أحد، وأما المقدرة فنحو: لا أحد في الدار، أي: لا من أحدٍ في الدار. ومن السور الكلي السالب ورود أداة (كل) وما شابهها على قضية منفية، وكذلك كل قضية جاء فيها السور الكلي قبل أداة السلب التي فيها، نحو: كلُّ إنسان ليس بجماد، وهذا ما يسمى: (عموم السلب).
The explicit [type] is like: 'ما جاءني من أحد' (No one came to me), and the implicit [type] is like: 'لا أحد في الدار' (No one is in the house), i.e., 'لا من أحدٍ في الدار'. And from the universal negative quantifier is the occurrence of the tool 'كل' (every) and its like on a negated proposition, and likewise every proposition in which the universal quantifier comes before the negation tool in it, such as: 'كل إنسان ليس بجماد' (Every human is not an inanimate object), and this is called: 'عموم السلب' (universality of negation).

Commentary
This passage defines the universal negative quantifier (السور الكلي السالب). The author distinguishes two forms: explicit (الظاهرة) where the negation is explicit, e.g., 'ما جاءني من أحد' (No one came to me), and implicit (المقدرة) where the negation is implied, e.g., 'لا أحد في الدار' (No one is in the house). Then the author states the rule: a universal quantifier (such as 'كل') applied to a negated proposition, or when the universal quantifier precedes the negation tool, yields what is called 'عموم السلب' (universality of negation). The example 'كل إنسان ليس بجماد' (Every human is not an inanimate object) illustrates that negation applies universally to every individual of the kind. This is a key logical concept in quantifying propositions.

Identifying Particular Affirmative Quantifiers in Arabic
Lists expressions for the particular affirmative quantifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which Arabic expressions function as particular affirmative quantifiers in logical propositions.

Core Idea
The particular affirmative quantifier asserts existence for at least some members.

In Islamic logic, quantifiers specify how much of the subject is included. The particular affirmative quantifier (السور الجزئي الموجب) indicates that the predicate applies to at least one individual of the subject. This lesson reviews the common Arabic words used for this quantifier, so you can recognize them in kalam or fiqh texts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the quantifier function
The particular affirmative quantifier asserts that the predicate holds for at least one member of the subject. It expresses existence not universality.
In Usul al-Fiqh, 'some' (بعض) is used in rulings that apply to a subset.
2
Learn the listed expressions
Memorize the words given: بعض, قسم, فريق, منهم, فئة, طائفة. All indicate partial encompassment.
Other synonyms may also work, as long as they convey partiality.
3
Distinguish from universal quantifiers
Contrast these with all-encompassing words like كل (all) or جميع (entire). Particular quantifiers do not cover every individual.
A common mistake is reading 'بعض' as 'some but not all'—in logic it means at least one.

3 - And the particular affirmative quantifier comes in many expressions, among them: (بعض - some, قسم - a part, فريق - a group, منهم - among them, فئة - a category, طائفة - a party), and likewise everything that indicates partial encompassment.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage enumerates the various expressions used for the particular affirmative quantifier (السور الجزئي الموجب) in Arabic. The author lists words like 'بعض' (some), 'قسم' (a part), 'فريق' (a group), 'منهم' (among them), 'فئة' (a category), 'طائفة' (a party), and any expression that conveys partial inclusion. In logic, these quantifiers assert that at least one individual of the subject term possesses the predicate. For example, 'بعض الحيوان إنسان' (Some animals are human) is a particular affirmative proposition.

Particular Negative Quantifier and Negation of Universality
Defines particular negative quantifier and 'salb al-umum'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct particular negative propositions in Arabic logical discourse, distinguishing 'salb al-umum' from universal negation.

Core Idea
'ليس كل' means 'not all', not 'none' -- a key distinction in Islamic logic.

In this lesson, we explore how the particular negative quantifier is formed in Arabic logic. The author shows that combining a particular quantifier with negation, or preceding a universal quantifier with negation, yields a specific type of judgment: partial negation, known as 'salb al-umum'. This distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting propositions in Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the structure of the particular negative quantifier
Identify the two components: a particular quantifier (e.g., بعض) and a negation tool (e.g., ليس). Note that the order can vary (negation before or after the quantifier).
Example: 'بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان' and 'ليس بعض الحيوان بإنسان' both work.
2
Contrast with other quantifier forms
Compare particular negative (بعض ليس) with universal negative (كل ليس) and particular affirmative (بعض). Understand that 'كل ليس' means none (universal negative), while 'ليس كل' means not all (particular negative).
The shift from 'كل ليس' to 'ليس كل' changes the logical meaning entirely.
3
Identify 'salb al-umum' in expressions
Recognize that phrases like 'ليس كل' or 'ليس جميع' express partial negation, not total negation. This is 'salb al-umum' (negation of universality).
This contrasts with 'umum al-salb' (universality of negation), which would be 'كل ليس'.
4
Apply to Islamic examples
Use the given example: 'ليس كل بناء مسجداً' means some buildings are mosques and some are not. Test your understanding by paraphrasing: 'Some buildings are not mosques' but not 'No building is a mosque'.
The example 'ما كل بيضاء شحمة' (not every white thing is fat) similarly means some white things are fat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤ - والسور الجزئي السالب يأتي بألفاظ كثيرة، وتتلخص بأن يجمع في القضية بين سور جزئي وأداة من أدوات النفي، فيوجد في القضية مثلاً: (بعض) و (ليس) سواء تقدم النفي على السور الجزئي أو تأخر؛ ومثل الجمع بين (بعض وليس) الجمع بين أي سور جزئي وبين أي أداة من أدوات النفي، ومن أمثلة ذلك: بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان؛ وليس بعض الحيوان بإنسان. ويأتي السور الجزئي أيضاً بأن يسبق النفي أداة من أدوات العموم، مثل: (ليس كل) و (ليس جميع) ونحو ذلك، فمثل هذا التعبير يفيد السلب الجزئي لا السلب الكلي وهذا ما يسمى: (سلب العموم) ومن أمثلة ذلك: ليس كل بناء مسجداً - ما كلُّ بيضاء شحمة - ولا كلُّ حمراء لحمة - فلا يمنع أن يكون بعض الأبنية مساجد، وبعض ما هي بيضاء هي شحمة، وبعض ما هي حمراء هي لحمة.
4 - And the particular negative quantifier comes in many expressions, and it is summarized by combining in the proposition a particular quantifier and a tool of negation. So in the proposition there appears, for example, (بعض - some) and (ليس - not), whether the negation precedes the particular quantifier or follows it. And like the combination of (some and not) is the combination of any particular quantifier with any negation tool. Examples of that: 'بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان' (Some animals are not human); and 'ليس بعض الحيوان بإنسان' (Not some animals are human). And the particular quantifier also comes such that the negation precedes a tool of generality, like: 'ليس كل' (not every) and 'ليس جميع' (not all), and so on. Such an expression conveys partial negation, not total negation, and this is called: (سلب العموم - negation of universality). Examples of that: 'ليس كل بناء مسجداً' (Not every building is a mosque) – 'ما كل بيضاء شحمة' (Not every white thing is fat) – 'ولا كل حمراء لحمة' (And not every red thing is meat) – so it does not prevent that some buildings are mosques, some white things are fat, and some red things are meat.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage defines the particular negative quantifier (السور الجزئي السالب). The author explains that it is formed by combining a particular quantifier (e.g., 'بعض') with a negation tool (e.g., 'ليس'), and the order can vary: 'بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان' or 'ليس بعض الحيوان بإنسان' both yield a particular negative. Additionally, the particular negative can be expressed by preceding a universal quantifier with negation, such as 'ليس كل' or 'ليس جميع'. The author emphasizes that 'ليس كل' yields partial negation, not total negation, and introduces the term 'سلب العموم' (negation of universality) for this. Examples are given from everyday and fiqh-like contexts: 'ليس كل بناء مسجداً' (Not every building is a mosque) means some buildings are mosques, but not all. This distinction is crucial for correct logical reasoning.

Key Terms
السور الجزئي السالب
سلب العموم

Key Takeaway
The particular negative quantifier denies the predicate of some, not all, members of the subject, and 'salb al-umum' (negation of universality) is the form that expresses this partial negation.

Distinguishing 'Umum al-Salb and Salab al-'Umum
Investigates the difference between عموم السلب and سلب العموم

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'umum al-salb and salab al-'umum in quantified Arabic propositions and identify examples of each correctly.

Core Idea
The order of quantifier and negation determines the logical scope.

In this passage, the author investigates the critical distinction between two related but opposite quantifier-negation structures: عموم السلب (universality of negation) and سلب العموم (negation of universality). While they may look similar, they lead to very different logical meanings. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly interpreting and constructing propositions in Islamic logical discourse, especially when evaluating statements in fiqh, usul, and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the order of quantifier and negation
Determine whether the universal quantifier (e.g., كل) comes before the negation (ليس/لا) or after it. The order is the key to the meaning.
If quantifier precedes negation, it is عموم السلب; if negation precedes quantifier, it is سلب العموم.
2
Translate the proposition into logical meaning
For عموم السلب (e.g., كل إنسان ليس بكاتب), the meaning is 'Every individual of the subject is negated' i.e., 'No human is a writer.' For سلب العموم (e.g., ليس كل إنسان بكاتب), the meaning is 'It is not the case that every individual has the predicate' i.e., 'Some humans are not writers.'
Be careful: ليس كل إنسان بكاتب does not mean 'No human is a writer' but 'Not every human is a writer.'
3
Examine the poetic examples
The author provides two lines: 'ما كل ما يتمنى المرء يدركه' and 'ما كل رأي الفتى يدعو إلى رشد'. In both, negation precedes the universal quantifier, so they are examples of سلب العموم. They negate the universality, not each individual.
The original universal claims are denied, resulting in a particular judgment.
4
Apply the distinction to evaluate truth conditions
Consider the truth of 'كل إنسان ليس بكاتب' (false because some humans are writers) vs. 'ليس كل إنسان بكاتب' (true because not all are writers). The same factual situation can make one true and the other false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التحقيق في عموم السلب وسلب العموم: إذا تقدمت أداة العموم على أداة السلب في القضية أفادت عموم السلب كما ذكرنا آنفاً؛ أي: أفادت أن النفي في القضية مسلط على كل فردٍ من أفراد الكلي. وإذا تقدمت أداة السلب على أداة العموم في القضية أفادت سلب العموم، كما ذكرنا آنفاً أيضاً، أي: أفادت أن النفي في القضية مسلط على بعض أفراد الكلي فقط، لا على كل أفراد الكلي. فإذا قلنا: ليس كل إنسان بكاتب، فإن معنى هذه القضية أن بعض الناس ليس بكاتب، وهي قضية صادقة، لأن الواقع كذلك. أما إذا قلنا: كل إنسان ليس بكاتب، فإن معنى هذه القضية أنه لا أحد من الناس بكاتب، وهي قضية كاذبة، لأن الواقع يخالفها. وافترق المثالان في الدلالة لأن المثال الأول قد سُلب فيه التعميم فقط، وسلب التعميم لا يعني توجيه السلب إلى كل فرد بخصوصه، أما المثال الثاني فقد عُمّم فيه السلب، أي: إن السلب فيها عام مسلط على كل الأفراد. ولا بد أن نلاحظ في عموم السلب أن يكون العموم مسلطاً على قضية سالبة، وأن نلاحظ في سلب العموم أن يكون السلب مسلطاً على العموم في قضية كلية. ومن أمثلة سلب العموم قول الشاعر: ما كل ما يتمنى المرء يدركه تأتي الرياح بما لا تشتهي السفن أي: بل ربما يدرك المرء بعض ما يتمنى لا كله، وذلك لأن القضية هي في الأصل قضية كلية، وهي: (كل ما يتمنى المرء يدركه) ثم سُلّط السلب على العموم فيها لا على كل فرد، فانتفى العموم فقط، وحينما ينتفي العموم ترجع القضية إلى أنها ليست كلية، وعندئذ تكون جزئية أو مهملة وهما في القوة سواء. وقول الآخر: (ما كلُّ رأي الفتى يدعو إلى رَشد) أي: بل قد يدعو بعض رأي الفتى إلى رشد، ولكن ليس كل رأيه يدعو إلى رشد، وذلك لأن القضية هي في الأصل قضية كلية، وهي: (كلُّ رأي الفتى يدعو إلى رشد) ثم سُلط السلب على العموم فيها فانتفى العموم فقط،
Investigation into 'عموم السلب' and 'سلب العموم': When the tool of generality precedes the tool of negation in a proposition, it conveys 'عموم السلب' (universality of negation) as we mentioned earlier; i.e., it conveys that the negation in the proposition applies to every individual of the universal. And when the tool of negation precedes the tool of generality in a proposition, it conveys 'سلب العموم' (negation of universality), as we also mentioned earlier; i.e., it conveys that the negation in the proposition applies only to some individuals of the universal, not to all individuals of the universal. If we say: 'ليس كل إنسان بكاتب' (Not every human is a writer), the meaning of this proposition is that some people are not writers, and it is a true proposition because reality is like that. But if we say: 'كل إنسان ليس بكاتب' (Every human is not a writer), the meaning of this proposition is that none of the people are writers, and it is a false proposition because reality contradicts it. And the two examples differ in signification because in the first example, generality (التعميم) alone is negated, and negation of generality does not mean directing negation to every individual specifically. As for the second example, negation is universalized (عُمّم فيه السلب), i.e., the negation in it is general and applies to all individuals. And we must note in 'عموم السلب' that generality (العموم) is applied to a negative proposition, and we must note in 'سلب العموم' that negation is applied to generality in a universal proposition. Among examples of 'سلب العموم' is the poet's saying: 'ما كل ما يتمنى المرء يدركه ... تأتي الرياح بما لا تشتهي السفن' (Not everything that a man wishes does he attain; the winds come with what ships do not desire) – i.e., rather, perhaps a man attains some of what he wishes, not all of it. That is because the proposition is originally a universal proposition: 'كل ما يتمنى المرء يدركه' (Man attains everything he wishes), then negation is applied to generality in it, not to every individual, so generality is negated. And when generality is negated, the proposition returns to not being universal, and at that point it becomes particular or unspecific, and both are equal in force. And the saying of another: '(ما كل رأي الفتى يدعو إلى رشد)' (Not every opinion of a young man leads to guidance) – i.e., rather, perhaps some of a young man's opinion leads to guidance, but not all of his opinion leads to guidance. That is because the proposition is originally a universal proposition: 'كل رأي الفتى يدعو إلى رشد' (Every opinion of a young man leads to guidance), then negation is applied to generality in it, so generality alone is negated.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed investigation (التحقيق) into the two key concepts: عموم السلب (universality of negation) and سلب العموم (negation of universality). The author reiterates the rule: when the universal quantifier (أداة العموم) precedes negation, it yields عموم السلب – negation applies to every individual. When negation precedes the universal quantifier, it yields سلب العموم – negation applies only to some individuals. The author illustrates with the pair: 'ليس كل إنسان بكاتب' (Not every human is a writer) vs. 'كل إنسان ليس بكاتب' (Every human is not a writer). The first is true because some people are not writers; the second is false because it claims no human writes. The distinction is that in the first, only the universality of the positive claim is negated, not each individual. The author then gives two poetic examples to reinforce the point: the famous line 'ما كل ما يتمنى المرء يدركه' (Not everything a man wishes does he attain), and 'ما كل رأي الفتى يدعو إلى رشد' (Not every opinion of a young man leads to guidance). In both, the original universal claim is negated by applying negation to the quantifier, resulting in a particular or indefinite judgment. The author stresses that when generality is negated, the proposition is no longer universal but becomes particular or unspecified, and these are logically equivalent in force. This is a fundamental distinction in the logic of quantified propositions.

Key Terms
عموم السلب
سلب العموم
التعميم

Key Takeaway
The order of the universal quantifier and negation in a proposition determines whether the negation applies to all individuals (عموم السلب) or only to the universal claim (سلب العموم), a distinction crucial for accurate logical analysis in Islamic sciences.

Distinguishing Salb al-Umum from Umum al-Salb in Negative Propositions
Author explains the distinction between negation of universality and universality of negation with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'salb al-umum' and 'umum al-salb' in any Arabic proposition and identify which logical form is being used in examples from Islamic texts.

Core Idea
Negation can strike the universal or the individual—knowing which changes the truth of a claim.

You have probably heard statements like 'Not everyone is a scholar' and 'No one is a scholar.' These sound similar but are logically opposite. This lesson teaches you to identify the two patterns—negation of universality (salb al-umum) and universality of negation (umum al-salb)—using examples from Arabic poetry, the Prophetic hadith, and the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantifier and negation position
Look for the universal quantifier (كل) and the negation word (ليس or لا). Determine if the negation directly precedes the quantifier (salb al-umum) or if the quantifier is attached to a negative predicate (umum al-salb).
In Arabic, 'not every' is salb al-umum; 'every...not' is umum al-salb.
2
Apply truth condition test
For salb al-umum, the proposition is true if at least one individual lacks the predicate. For umum al-salb, it is true only if all individuals lack the predicate. Test with a concrete case.
3
Classify the example from poetry
Analyze the line 'Not everything that a man wishes does he attain.' It starts with 'not every' (سلب العموم). Confirm that it denies the universal, not each individual.
4
Classify the hadith example
The Prophet said 'All of that did not happen.' The quantifier 'each' modifies the negative predicate, making it umum al-salb. Understand Dhul-Yadayn's response as recognition of this form.
5
Handle the Quranic objection
The verse 'Allah does not love every conceited boaster' might appear as salb al-umum but is not, because the negation attaches to the verb, not the quantifier. Analyze the structure to see why the quantifier retains its universality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

القضية أنه لا أحد من الناس بكاتب، وهي قضية كاذبة، لأن الواقع يخالفها. وافترق المثالان في الدلالة لأن المثال الأول قد سُلب فيه التعميم فقط، وسلب التعميم لا يعني توجيه السلب إلى كل فرد بخصوصه، أما المثال الثاني فقد عُمّم فيه السلب، أي: إن السلب فيها عام مسلط على كل الأفراد. ولا بد أن نلاحظ في عموم السلب أن يكون العموم مسلطاً على قضية سالبة، وأن نلاحظ في سلب العموم أن يكون السلب مسلطاً على العموم في قضية كلية. ومن أمثلة سلب العموم قول الشاعر: ما كل ما يتمنى المرء يدركه تأتي الرياح بما لا تشتهي السفن أي: بل ربما يدرك المرء بعض ما يتمنى لا كله، وذلك لأن القضية هي في الأصل قضية كلية، وهي: (كل ما يتمنى المرء يدركه) ثم سُلّط السلب على العموم فيها لا على كل فرد، فانتفى العموم فقط، وحينما ينتفي العموم ترجع القضية إلى أنها ليست كلية، وعندئذ تكون جزئية أو مهملة وهما في القوة سواء. وقول الآخر: (ما كلُّ رأي الفتى يدعو إلى رَشد) أي: بل قد يدعو بعض رأي الفتى إلى رشد، ولكن ليس كل رأيه يدعو إلى رشد، وذلك لأن القضية هي في الأصل قضية كلية، وهي: (كلُّ رأي الفتى يدعو إلى رشد) ثم سُلط السلب على العموم فيها فانتفى العموم فقط، وإذا انتفت الصفة الكلية فقط فليس معنى ذلك أن يكون النفي مسلطاً على كل فرد. ومن أمثلة عموم السلب قول الشاعر أبي النجم : قد أصبحت أم الخيار تدعي علي ذنباً كله لم أصنع أي : لم أصنع شيئاً منه، وذلك لأن القضية هي في الأصل سالبة مهملة، وهي : (الذنب المنسوب إلي لم أصنعه) ثم سلط العموم عليها فصارت : (كل الذنب المنسوب إلي لم أصنعه). ومن أمثلة عموم السلب ما جاء في حديث سهو الرسول ﷺ في الصلاة وهو في الصحيحين، وفيه على ما روى أبو هريرة أن النبي ﷺ صلى إحدى صلاتي العشي ركعتين ثم سلم، فقال المسلمون : قصرت الصلاة، وفي القوم رجل في يديه طول يقال له : ذو اليدين، واسمه على ما جاء عند مسلم : (الخرباق)، فقال : يا رسول الله؛ أنسيت أم قصرت الصلاة؟ فقال : «كل ذلك لم يكن» فقال ذو اليدين : قد كان بعض ذلك يا رسول الله. فقول الرسول صلوات الله عليه : «كل ذلك لم يكن» هو من عموم السلب، أي : لم يكن شيء من ذلك، ولذلك قال ذو اليدين رداً لنفي النسيان وقصر الصلاة معاً : قد كان بعض ذلك يا رسول الله، إذ فهم أن المراد عموم السلب، فالقضية في الأصل سالبة مهملة، وهي : (النسيان والقصر لم يكونا) ثم سلط العموم عليها، فصارت (كل ذلك لم يكن). إشكال ودفعه : وأما قول الله تعالى : ﴿ والله لا يحب كل مختال فخور ﴾ فليس من سلب العموم الذي تسبق فيه أداة السلب أداة العموم، وذلك لأن السلب في قوله تعالى : ﴿ لا يحب ﴾ ليس مسلطاً على العموم في ﴿ كل مختال ﴾ وإنما هو مسلط على المحبة، والمحبة كلي مهمل، و ﴿ كل مختال فخور ﴾ قد جاء مفعولاً به للمحبة المنفية، لذلك فإن لفظة ﴿ كل ﴾ تبقى على عمومها، ولا
Some individuals of the universal only, not all individuals of the universal. For if we say: 'Not every man is a writer,' the meaning of this proposition is that some men are not writers, and it is a true proposition because reality is so. But if we say: 'Every man is not a writer,' the meaning of this proposition is that no man is a writer, and it is a false proposition because reality contradicts it. The two examples differ in signification because in the first example, the negation is applied only to the universal, and negation of the universal does not mean directing the negation to each individual in particular. As for the second example, the negation is universalized, i.e., the negation in it is general and imposed on all individuals. We must observe that in 'umum al-salb' (universality of negation) the universality is imposed on a negative proposition, and we must observe that in 'salb al-umum' (negation of universality) the negation is imposed on the universality in a universal proposition. Among examples of 'salb al-umum' is the poet's saying: 'Not everything that a man wishes does he attain; the winds come with what the ships do not desire.' Meaning: rather, perhaps a man attains some of what he wishes, not all of it. This is because the proposition is originally a universal proposition: 'Everything a man wishes he attains.' Then the negation is imposed on the universality in it, not on each individual, so the universality is negated only. When the universality is negated, the proposition reverts to not being universal, and then it is particular or unspecified, both being equivalent in force. And another poet's saying: 'Not every opinion of the youth leads to guidance.' Meaning: rather, some opinions of the youth may lead to guidance, but not all of his opinions lead to guidance. This is because the proposition is originally a universal proposition: 'Every opinion of the youth leads to guidance.' Then the negation is imposed on the universality in it, so the universality is negated only. And when only the universal quality is negated, that does not mean the negation is imposed on each individual. Among examples of 'umum al-salb' is the poet Abi al-Najm's saying: 'Umm al-Khiyar has started to accuse me of a sin, all of which I did not commit.' Meaning: I did not commit any of it. This is because the proposition is originally an unspecified negative proposition: 'The sin attributed to me I did not commit.' Then the universality was imposed on it, so it became: 'All the sin attributed to me I did not commit.' And among examples of 'umum al-salb' is what came in the hadith of the Prophet's ﷺ forgetfulness in prayer, which is in the two Sahihs. In it, according to what Abu Huraira narrated, that the Prophet ﷺ prayed one of the two afternoon prayers two rak'ahs and then made salam. The Muslims said: 'The prayer has been shortened.' Among the people was a man with long hands called Dhul-Yadayn, and his name as in Muslim's narration is al-Khirbaq. He said: 'O Messenger of Allah, did you forget or was the prayer shortened?' He said: 'All of that did not happen.' Dhul-Yadayn said: 'Some of that did happen, O Messenger of Allah.' The Prophet's ﷺ statement 'All of that did not happen' is an instance of 'umum al-salb', i.e., none of that happened. Therefore Dhul-Yadayn replied, rejecting the negation of both forgetfulness and shortening together: 'Some of that did happen, O Messenger of Allah,' understanding that what was meant was the universality of negation. So the proposition is originally an unspecified negative proposition: 'Forgetfulness and shortening were not.' Then the universality was imposed on it, so it became: 'All of that was not.' An objection and its rebuttal: As for the saying of Allah Most High: 'And Allah does not love every conceited boaster' (4:36), it is not an instance of 'salb al-umum' in which the tool of negation precedes the tool of universality. This is because the negation in His saying 'does not love' is not imposed on the universality in 'every conceited boaster', but rather it is imposed on the love, and love is an unspecified universal, and 'every conceited boaster' comes as the object of the negated love. Therefore the word 'every' remains in its universality, and...
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes two important logical concepts regarding negative propositions: salb al-umum (negation of universality) and umum al-salb (universality of negation). The author begins by contrasting two propositions: 'Not every man is a writer' (salb al-umum) and 'Every man is not a writer' (umum al-salb). The first is true because it merely denies that all men are writers, allowing some to be writers. The second is false because it claims that no man is a writer. The key difference lies in what the negation attaches to: in salb al-umum, the negation attaches to the universal quantifier, so only the universality is negated, not each individual. In umum al-salb, the negation is universalized, i.e., the negation applies to every individual. The author then provides illustrative examples from Arabic poetry. For salb al-umum, the line 'Not everything that a man wishes does he attain' shows that the original universal proposition 'Everything a man wishes he attains' had its universal negated, resulting in a particular proposition. Similarly, 'Not every opinion of the youth leads to guidance' follows the same pattern. For umum al-salb, the poet Abi al-Najm's line 'Umm al-Khiyar has started to accuse me of a sin, all of which I did not commit' exemplifies that the original unspecified negative 'The sin attributed to me I did not commit' becomes 'All the sin... I did not commit' by imposing universality on the negation. The famous hadith about the Prophet's forgetfulness in prayer is also analyzed: the Prophet said 'All of that did not happen,' which is umum al-salb meaning nothing of the sort occurred. Dhul-Yadayn's response shows he understood it as a universal negation. Finally, the author addresses a potential objection concerning the Quranic verse 'And Allah does not love every conceited boaster.' This might appear to be salb al-umum, but it is not, because the negation 'does not love' is not applied to the universal 'every'; rather, 'every conceited boaster' is the object of the negated love. Thus the word 'every' retains its universal meaning, and the verse is not an instance of salb al-umum.
