Distinguishing Universal Negation from Negation of the Universal
Author defines عموم السلب and distinguishes it from سلب العموم with examples and Quranic resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between universal negation (عموم السلب) and negation of the universal (سلب العموم) in Arabic propositions, and correctly identify which form appears in Quranic verses and hadith.

Core Idea
Universal negation denies the predicate of every individual; negation of the universal denies the universal itself—confusing them changes the meaning of revelation.

This lesson introduces a critical logical distinction that appears frequently in Islamic texts: the difference between a proposition that negates a predicate of every individual (عموم السلب) and one that negates the universal quantifier over the subject (سلب العموم). The author uses examples from poetry, hadith, and the Quran to illustrate the distinction and resolve a potential confusion about a Quranic verse. Mastering this distinction will sharpen your ability to interpret legal and theological texts accurately.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition type
The author starts with a negated indefinite proposition (قضية سالبة مهملة). Recognize that this is a simple negation without quantifier.
Example: 'أم الخيار تدعي علي ذنبا كله لم أصنع' – 'the sin attributed to me I did not commit'.
2
Apply the universal quantifier
See how the addition of 'كل' (every) changes the meaning. If the quantifier is added after the negation, it becomes universal negation: negation applies to each individual.
The proposition becomes 'كل الذنب المنسوب إلي لم أصنعه' – 'Every sin attributed to me I did not commit'.
3
Compare with negation of the universal
In سلب العموم, the negation comes before the universal quantifier, negating the universality itself. For example, 'ليس كل شيء' means 'not every thing'.
This would be equivalent to 'Some things are not...' or 'Not all...'.
4
Analyze the Quranic verse
Examine how the author argues that Q. 31:18 'والله لا يحب كل مختال فخور' is not سلب العموم but عموم السلب because the negation ('لا يحب') is on the predicate, not on the quantifier 'كل'.
The verse is equivalent to 'كل مختال فخور لا يحبه الله' – 'Every insolent boaster, Allah does not love him'.
5
Resolve the confusion
Understand how this distinction resolves the confusion mentioned by Ibn Hisham and other scholars regarding the Quranic verse.
The key is to see whether the negation applies to the quantifier or to the predicate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا انتفت الصفة الكلية فقط فليس معنى ذلك أن يكون النفي مسلطاً على كل فرد. ومن أمثلة عموم السلب قول الشاعر أبي النجم : قد أصبحت أم الخيار تدعي علي ذنباً كله لم أصنع أي : لم أصنع شيئاً منه، وذلك لأن القضية هي في الأصل سالبة مهملة، وهي : (الذنب المنسوب إلي لم أصنعه) ثم سلط العموم عليها فصارت : (كل الذنب المنسوب إلي لم أصنعه). ومن أمثلة عموم السلب ما جاء في حديث سهو الرسول ﷺ في الصلاة وهو في الصحيحين، وفيه على ما روى أبو هريرة أن النبي ﷺ صلى إحدى صلوتي العشي ركعتين ثم سلم، فقال المسلمون : قصرت الصلاة، وفي القوم رجل في يديه طول يقال له : ذو اليدين، واسمه على ما جاء عند مسلم : (الخرباق)، فقال : يا رسول الله ؛ أنسيت أم قصرت الصلاة؟ فقال : «كل ذلك لم يكن» فقال ذو اليدين : قد كان بعض ذلك يا رسول الله . فقول الرسول صلوات الله عليه : «كل ذلك لم يكن» هو من عموم السلب، أي : لم يكن شيء من ذلك، ولذلك قال ذو اليدين رداً لنفي النسيان وقصر الصلاة معاً : قد كان بعض ذلك يا رسول الله، إذ فهم أن المراد عموم السلب، فالقضية في الأصل سالبة مهملة، وهي : (النسيان والقصر لم يكونا) ثم سلط العموم عليها، فصارت (كل ذلك لم يكن). إشكال ودفعه : وأما قول الله تعالى : ﴿ والله لا يحب كل مختال فخور ﴾ فليس من سلب العموم الذي تسبق فيه أداة السلب أداة العموم، وذلك لأن السلب في قوله تعالى : ﴿ لا يحب ﴾ ليس مسلطاً على العموم في ﴿ كل مختال ﴾ وإنما هو مسلط على المحبة، والمحبة كلي مهمل، و ﴿ كل مختال فخور ﴾ قد جاء مفعولاً به للمحبة المنفية، لذلك فإن لفظة ﴿ كل ﴾ تبقى على عمومها، ولا تتأثر بالسلب، إذ هو غير مسلط عليها، فالجملة في قوة قولنا : ﴿ كلُّ مختالٍ فخور لا يحبه الله ﴾ فهي إذن من عموم السلب لا من سلب العموم . هذا ما وضح لنا بالتأمل في هذا النص القرآني، وبه ينحل الإشكال الذي ذكره بعض العلماء حول قاعدة عموم السلب وسلب العموم، لا سيما ما أشار إليه ابن هشام في مغني اللبيب.
And if only the universal attribute is negated, that does not mean that the negation is applied to every individual. Examples of universal negation (عموم السلب) include the saying of the poet Abu Najm: 'Umm al-Khiyar has claimed against me a sin, all of which I did not commit,' meaning: I did not commit any of it. That is because the proposition originally is a negated indefinite [proposition], which is: 'The sin attributed to me I did not commit.' Then the universal quantifier is applied to it, so it becomes: 'Every sin attributed to me I did not commit.' Another example of universal negation is what came in the Hadith of the Prophet's forgetfulness in prayer, which is in the two Sahihs. According to what Abu Hurairah narrated: The Prophet ﷺ prayed one of the two afternoon prayers two rak'ahs and then said the taslim. The Muslims said: 'The prayer has been shortened.' Among the people was a man with long hands called Dhu al-Yadayn – his name according to Muslim is al-Khirbaq. He said: 'O Messenger of Allah, did you forget or was the prayer shortened?' He said: 'All of that did not happen.' Dhu al-Yadayn said: 'Some of that did happen, O Messenger of Allah.' So the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him, 'All of that did not happen' is an instance of universal negation, i.e., none of that happened. Therefore Dhu al-Yadayn replied to deny both forgetfulness and shortening together: 'Some of that did happen, O Messenger of Allah,' understanding that the intended meaning was universal negation. The proposition originally is a negated indefinite: 'Forgetfulness and shortening did not occur.' Then the universal quantifier was applied, so it became 'All of that did not occur.' [Then] an objection and its rebuttal: As for the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'And Allah does not love every insolent boaster' (31:18), it is not an instance of negation of the universal (سلب العموم) in which the tool of negation precedes the tool of universality. That is because the negation in 'does not love' is not applied to the universality in 'every insolent boaster'; rather it is applied to love, and love is an indefinite universal. And 'every insolent boaster' occurs as the object of the negated love. Therefore the word 'every' remains on its universality and is not affected by the negation, since it is not applied to it. So the sentence is equivalent to our saying: 'Every insolent boaster, Allah does not love him.' So it is an instance of universal negation, not negation of the universal. This is what became clear to us through reflection on this Quranic text, and by it the difficulty that some scholars mentioned regarding the rule of universal negation and negation of the universal is resolved, especially what Ibn Hisham alluded to in Mughni al-Labib.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage clarifies the logical distinction between universal negation (عموم السلب) and negation of the universal (سلب العموم). The author begins by noting that if only the universal attribute is negated, it does not mean that the negation applies to every individual. He then provides examples. The first example is from the poet Abu Najm: 'Umm al-Khiyar has claimed against me a sin, all of which I did not commit.' The original proposition is a negated indefinite: 'The sin attributed to me I did not commit.' When the universal quantifier is applied, it becomes 'Every sin attributed to me I did not commit.' This is عموم السلب because the negation covers each individual. The second example is from the hadith where the Prophet said 'All of that did not happen' in response to questions about forgetting or shortening the prayer. The companion Dhu al-Yadayn understood it as عموم السلب, objecting that some of it did happen. The author then addresses a potential objection from Qur'an 31:18: 'And Allah does not love every insolent boaster.' He argues that this is not an instance of سلب العموم (negation of the universal) but rather عموم السلب. The negation is on love, not on the universal quantifier. The phrase 'every insolent boaster' remains universally quantified, but the negation applies to the predicate 'loves' for each individual. Thus the meaning is 'Every insolent boaster, Allah does not love him,' which is عموم السلب. The author concludes that this resolves a confusion mentioned by Ibn Hisham.

Key Terms
عموم السلب
سلب العموم
قضية سالبة مهملة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the hadith 'كل ذلك لم يكن', which logical form is expressed?
A) سلب العموم (Negation of the universal)
B) عموم السلب (Universal negation)
C) قضية مهملة (Indefinite proposition)
D) قضية جزئية (Particular proposition)

Key Takeaway
Universal negation (عموم السلب) negates the predicate of every individual, while negation of the universal (سلب العموم) negates the universal quantifier itself; the order of words determines which is intended.

Distributive vs Collective Universal
Author defines and distinguishes distributive universal from collective universal with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish distributive universal from collective universal in a proposition and correctly classify examples from Islamic texts.

Core Idea
Distributive universal: each individual gets the judgment; collective: only the whole does.

In logic, not all universal statements mean the same thing. Sometimes a judgment applies to every single member of a group independently; other times it only applies to the group as a whole. This distinction, between the distributive universal (الكل الاستغراقي) and the collective universal (الكل المجموعي), is crucial for correctly interpreting legal and theological propositions. The passage below lays out the difference with clear examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate
Look at the proposition and find what is being said about what. The subject is often introduced by 'كل' (every/all).
The examples start with 'كل السيارات', 'كل الناس', etc.
2
Test the judgment on a single individual
Ask: does the judgment hold true for one random individual of the subject, taken alone? If yes, it is likely distributive. If not, consider collective.
For 'كل إنسان له قلب', each individual has a heart. For 'كل زوايا المثلث تساوي قائمتين', a single angle does not equal 180°.
3
Check the condition of conjunction
If the judgment requires the individuals to be combined or assembled to be true, it is collective. If it holds for each individually, it is distributive.
The author states collective requires 'شرط اجتماعها' (condition of their conjunction).
4
Classify the proposition
Based on the test, decide whether the proposition uses distributive or collective universal. Remember that the same wording can sometimes be ambiguous.
Context often clarifies which meaning is intended.

الكل الاستغراقي والكل المجموعي: أ ـ قد يتناول الحكم في القضية كل فرد من أفراد الكلي فيها دون شرط اجتماعه مع غيره . مثل : كل السيارات الصالحة تسير بمحرك آلي . أي : كل واحدة منها ذات محرك آلي تسير به . ومثل : كل الناس ذو رأس وقلب . أي : كل فرد منهم يستقل بأن له رأساً وقلباً، دون شرط اجتماعه مع غيره ومشاركته له في رأس وقلب واحد . ومثل : كل الأبقار الحلوبة تدر لبنها عقب الولادة . أي : كل واحدة منها لها هذه الصفة، دون شرط اجتماعها مع غيرها . وعندئذ تكون القضية من قسم القضايا الكلية، لأن الحكم فيها كان على سبيل الاستغراق الإفرادي، أي : كل واحد من الأفراد له الحكم نفسه كاملاً ، وهذا هو الكل الاستغراقي الذي يتناول جميع الأفراد . ب ـ وقد يتناول الحكم في القضية مجموع أفراد الكلي بشرط اجتماعها . مثل : كل زوايا المثلث تساوي زاويتين قائمتين، فالحكم هنا لا يثبت أن كل زاوية على حدة من زوايا المثلث تساوي قائمتين، ولكن يثبت أن مجموع الزوايا بشرط اجتماعها يساوي قائمتين . ومثل : كل قطرات المطر الغزير تكوّن السيل الجارف. فالحكم هنا
The distributive universal (الكل الاستغراقي) and the collective universal (الكل المجموعي): A) The judgment in the proposition may apply to every individual of the universal [subject] without condition of its conjunction with others. Example: 'Every functional car runs on a motor engine.' i.e., each one of them has a motor engine and runs with it. Another example: 'Every human has a head and a heart.' i.e., every individual independently has a head and a heart, without condition of his conjunction with others and his sharing with them in one head and one heart. Another example: 'Every milk-producing cow yields milk after birth.' i.e., each one of them has this attribute, without condition of its conjunction with others. Then the proposition belongs to the class of universal propositions, because the judgment in it is on the basis of distributive inclusion, i.e., each one of the individuals has the same judgment completely. And this is the distributive universal that encompasses all individuals. B) The judgment in the proposition may apply to the sum total of the individuals of the universal subject under the condition of their conjunction. Example: 'All angles of a triangle equal two right angles.' The judgment here does not establish that each angle individually of the angles of the triangle equals two right angles, but establishes that the sum of the angles under the condition of their conjunction equals two right angles. Another example: 'All drops of heavy rain form a torrent.' The judgment here
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage defines two types of universal quantifiers: the distributive universal (الكل الاستغراقي) and the collective universal (الكل المجموعي). The author first explains that in the distributive type, the judgment applies to each individual of the subject independently, without requiring the conjunction of others. Three examples are given: 'Every functional car runs on a motor engine' (each car individually), 'Every human has a head and a heart' (each person independently), and 'Every milk-producing cow yields milk after birth' (each cow individually). In such cases, the proposition is a universal proposition because the judgment is on the basis of 'distributive inclusion' (استغراق إفرادي), meaning each individual fully receives the judgment. This is the distributive universal. The author then describes the collective type, where the judgment applies to the sum total of individuals under the condition that they are combined. Two examples are provided: 'All angles of a triangle equal two right angles' (the sum of the angles, not each angle individually) and 'All drops of heavy rain form a torrent' (the collective effect of the drops). The passage cuts off at this point, but the distinction is clear: one must be careful whether a universal statement refers to each member separately or to the group as a whole.

الكل الاستغراقي
الكل المجموعي
الاستغراق الإفرادي

Key Takeaway
Always test a universal proposition on a single individual: if the judgment holds for each alone, it is distributive; if it requires the group, it is collective.

Distributive vs Collective Universal
Author defines and exemplifies distributive and collective universal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between distributive and collective universal propositions in any qadiyya and correctly classify examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Distributive universal applies to each individual; collective universal applies only to the group together.

When we say 'every Muslim', do we mean each Muslim individually or all Muslims as a single entity? The author clarifies a key distinction in propositional logic that has direct consequences for interpreting legal rulings, theological claims, and collective statements in the Islamic tradition. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion when analyzing universal judgments.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the author's definitions of distributive universal (al-kull al-istighraqi) and collective universal (al-kull al-majmu'i). Note the key difference: distributive applies to each individual separately; collective applies only to the sum when combined.
2
Analyze the examples
For each example given by the author, determine whether the predicate applies to each individual of the subject or to the group as a whole. For instance, 'Every human has a head and a heart' is distributive; 'Every angle of a triangle equals two right angles' is collective.
Focus on the logical quantifier, not the wording.
3
Examine subcategories
Note the author's subcategorization of collective universals into those requiring all individuals (like the triangle example) and those requiring only most (like the rain droplet example). Understand how these resemble particular propositions in requiring combination.
These are not standard universal propositions; they fall outside the scope of true universals.
4
Apply to new examples
Test your understanding by classifying new propositions from Islamic sciences as distributive or collective. Consider statements from fiqh, hadith, or kalam.
For example, 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' is distributive; 'The Ummah will not agree on error' is collective.

Source Text

الكل الاستغراقي والكل المجموعي: أ ـ قد يتناول الحكم في القضية كل فرد من أفراد الكلي فيها دون شرط اجتماعه مع غيره . مثل : كل السيارات الصالحة تسير بمحرك آلي . أي : كل واحدة منها ذات محرك آلي تسير به . ومثل : كل الناس ذو رأس وقلب . أي : كل فرد منهم يستقل بأن له رأساً وقلباً، دون شرط اجتماعه مع غيره ومشاركته له في رأس وقلب واحد . ومثل : كل الأبقار الحلوبة تدر لبنها عقب الولادة . أي : كل واحدة منها لها هذه الصفة، دون شرط اجتماعها مع غيرها . وعندئذ تكون القضية من قسم القضايا الكلية، لأن الحكم فيها كان على سبيل الاستغراق الإفرادي، أي : كل واحد من الأفراد له الحكم نفسه كاملاً ، وهذا هو الكل الاستغراقي الذي يتناول جميع الأفراد . ب ـ وقد يتناول الحكم في القضية مجموع أفراد الكلي بشرط اجتماعها . مثل : كل زوايا المثلث تساوي زاويتين قائمتين، فالحكم هنا لا يثبت أن كل زاوية على حدة من زوايا المثلث تساوي قائمتين، ولكن يثبت أن مجموع الزوايا بشرط اجتماعها يساوي قائمتين . ومثل : كل قطرات المطر الغزير تكوّن السيل الجارف. فالحكم هنا أيضاً لا يثبت أن كل قطرة من هذه القطرات على حدة تكوّن السيل الجارف، ولكن يثبت أن مجموع القطرات بشرط اجتماعها هي التي تكوّن السيل الجارف. ومن الظاهر أن الحكم في مثل هذه القضايا يلاحظ فيه المعنى الجمعي بشرط اجتماع الأفراد، لا أن كل فرد على سبيل الاستغراق له الحكم نفسه. وقضايا من هذا القبيل تخرج عن دائرة القضايا الكلية، لأن الحكم فيها على المجموع بشرط الاجتماع، لا على الجميع على سبيل الاستغراق الإفرادي. وكل قضية من هذا القبيل: ١ - إما أن تكون بمثابة القضية المخصوصة إذا كان يشترط فيها اجتماع كل الأفراد حتى يصدق الحكم، وذلك كالمثال الأول: كل زوايا المثلث تساوي زاويتين قائمتين، إذ ما لم تجتمع كلها فإنها لا تساوي ذلك. ومثل: كل أرجل الكرسي هي التي تعدل قيامه، إذ بعضها لا يكفي للتعديل. ٢ - وإما أن تكون بمثابة القضية الجزئية، إذا كان يكفي فيها اجتماع قسم من الأفراد، وذلك كالمثال الثاني: كل قطرات المطر الغزير تكوّن السيل الجارف، إذ يكفي اجتماع معظمها دون جميعها. ومثل: كل نجوم السماء زينة لها، إذ لا تتم الزينة إلا باجتماعها، ولكن لا يشترط اجتماع الجميع حتى تتحقق الزينة، بل يكفي اجتماع الأكثر.
The distributive universal and the collective universal: A - The judgment in a proposition may apply to each individual of the universal without the condition of its combination with others. Example: Every car that works runs by an automatic engine. Meaning: each one of them has an automatic engine and runs by it. And example: Every human has a head and a heart. Meaning: each individual among them independently has a head and a heart, without the condition of combining with others and sharing one head and heart. And example: Every dairy cow yields its milk after birth. Meaning: each one of them has this characteristic, without the condition of combining with others. And then the proposition is from the class of universal propositions, because the judgment in it is on the basis of individual distribution, i.e., each one of the individuals has the same judgment in full, and this is the distributive universal which encompasses all individuals. B - And the judgment in a proposition may apply to the sum of individuals of the universal with the condition of their combination. Example: Every angle of a triangle equals two right angles. Here the judgment does not establish that each single angle of the triangle equals two right angles, but it establishes that the sum of angles with the condition of their combination equals two right angles. And example: Every droplet of heavy rain forms the torrent. Here also the judgment does not establish that each single droplet forms the torrent, but it establishes that the sum of droplets with the condition of their combination forms the torrent. And it is apparent that the judgment in such propositions observes the collective meaning with the condition of combination of individuals, not that each individually has the same judgment. And propositions of this sort fall outside the scope of universal propositions, because the judgment in them is on the whole with the condition of combination, not on all on the basis of individual distribution. And every proposition of this sort: 1 - Either it is like a particular proposition if it requires the combination of all individuals for the judgment to be true, such as the first example: Every angle of a triangle equals two right angles, because if they do not all combine, they do not equal that. And example: Every leg of a chair is what levels its standing, because some are not sufficient for leveling. 2 - Or it is like a partial proposition if a combination of some individuals suffices, such as the second example: Every droplet of heavy rain forms the torrent, because the combination of most of them suffices without all of them. And example: Every star of the sky is an adornment for it, because adornment is only achieved by their combination, but it does not require the combination of all for adornment to be realized; rather the combination of most suffices.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes two types of universal quantifier: the distributive universal (al-kull al-istighraqi) and the collective universal (al-kull al-majmu'i). The author begins with the distributive: here the judgment applies to each individual of the universal independently, without needing combination. Examples are given: every functioning car runs by engine (each car independently), every human has a head and heart (each human individually), every dairy cow yields milk after birth (each cow alone). Such propositions are truly universal because the predicate is distributed to each individual. Then the collective universal is presented: here the judgment applies to the sum of individuals only when they combine. Examples: every angle of a triangle equals two right angles (only the sum of angles has that property, not each angle individually), every droplet of heavy rain forms the torrent (only the collective mass of droplets forms the torrent). The author notes that such propositions are not universal in the logical sense; they fall outside universal propositions because the judgment is on the whole with condition of combination. Finally, the author further subdivides collective universal into two types: (1) those that require all individuals (like the triangle example — all angles must be present), which resembles a particular proposition in requiring all; and (2) those that suffice with most individuals (like rain droplets — most drops form the torrent), resembling a partial proposition. This distinction is crucial in logic and Islamic legal theory: for instance, 'Every Sahaba is trustworthy' is distributive (each Sahaba individually trustworthy), while 'The Ummah is protected from error' is collective (the entire community collectively).

الكل المجموعي
القضية المخصوصة
القضية الجزئية

The Categorical Proposition as the First Division
Author begins classification of propositions based on relation between terms, listing categorical as first type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the categorical proposition and distinguish it from other proposition types based on the relation (idafa) between its terms.

Core Idea
The categorical proposition is the simplest: it predicates directly without any condition.

The author begins a new classification of propositions by the relation between subject and predicate. He lists three main divisions but only names the first: the categorical proposition. Understanding this classification is crucial because it determines how arguments are structured in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criterion for classification
Notice that the classification is based on the relation (al-idafa) between the limits (hudud), i.e., the subject and predicate of the proposition.
In Arabic, 'idafa' here means logical relation, not the grammatical construct.
2
Extract the first division from the list
The author explicitly states the first type: the categorical proposition (al-qadiyya al-hamliyya). The other two are not named in this passage but are introduced later.
3
Define the categorical proposition
A categorical proposition is one where a predicate is affirmed or denied of a subject directly, without any condition or connection other than simple predication.
This definition is implied by the term 'hamliyya' (from haml = predication) and is standard in Islamic logic.
4
Distinguish from other proposition types
Contrast with conditional (if-then) and disjunctive (either-or) propositions which involve additional logical connectives. The categorical is the simplest form.
In fiqh, 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' is categorical; 'If it rains then prayer in mosque is excused' is conditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام القضايا باعتبار الإضافة بين حدودها: تنقسم القضايا باعتبار الإضافة القائمة بين حدودها إلى ثلاثة أقسام رئيسية، وهي: ١ - القضية الحَمْلِيّة.
Divisions of propositions according to the relation between their terms: Propositions are divided according to the relation subsisting between their terms into three main divisions, namely: 1- The categorical proposition.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the classification of propositions. The author states that propositions can be divided based on the relation (al-idafa) between their limits (hudud), i.e., the subject and predicate. He announces three main divisions but only names the first: the categorical proposition (al-qadiyya al-hamliyya). The categorical proposition is one where the predicate is affirmed or denied of the subject directly, without any condition. The other two types—conditional and disjunctive—are to follow. This classification is foundational in logic because it determines the structure of arguments. In Islamic legal reasoning, categorical propositions are common: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' is categorical. The division into three is based on the nature of the relation: simple predication (categorical), conditional connection (if-then), and disjunctive connection (either-or). The passage sets the stage for detailed discussion.

Key Terms
القضية الحَمْلِيّة (al-qadiyya al-hamliyya): A proposition in which a predicate is affirmed or denied of a subject directly, without any condition or connection other than simple predication. [Note: Stable across logic tradition; also called categorical proposition.]
الإضافة (al-idafa): The relation between the subject and predicate in a proposition; the logical connection that determines the type of proposition. [Note: In grammar, idafa means genitive construction; here it is used in its logical sense of relation.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Propositions are classified by the relation between their terms; the first and simplest type is the categorical proposition, where a predicate is directly affirmed or denied of a subject.

The Collective Universal: When 'All' Doesn't Mean 'Each'
Author distinguishes collective from distributive universal judgments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a universal proposition requires a distributive or collective interpretation by analysing whether the predicate depends on the conjunction of individuals.

Core Idea
Not all universal propositions are distributive; some require collective reading where truth depends on the aggregate.

You already know that universal propositions (al-qadiyya al-kulliyya) typically assert something about every individual. But what if a statement like 'All angles of a triangle sum to 180°' is only true when the angles are considered together? The author introduces a crucial nuance: some universal judgments apply collectively, not distributively. This lesson will help you spot such propositions and understand why they exit the strict domain of universals.

Guided Path
1
Recall the standard universal (distributive) reading
A universal proposition like 'Every human is mortal' means: take any individual human, and that individual is mortal. This is istighrāq ifrādī (distributive coverage). The predicate holds for each member separately.
This is the default interpretation in classical logic.
2
Identify predicates that require conjunction
Some predicates describe a property that only emerges when individuals come together. For example, 'form a torrent' cannot be true of a single raindrop; it requires many drops collectively. The judgment is on the aggregate under the condition of their conjunction.
The author calls this al-ma'nā al-jam'ī bi-sharṭ al-ijtimā' (collective meaning under condition of conjunction).
3
Apply the test of counterexample
Take the proposition 'All angles of a triangle equal two right angles.' If you test a single angle, it does not equal two right angles. So the proposition cannot be distributive. Only when all angles are taken together does the predicate apply.
Thus the proposition is not truly universal (kulliyya) in the distributive sense; it exits that domain.
4
Classify as collective universal
If the predicate fails when applied to individuals alone but holds when all individuals are conjoined, the proposition is a collective universal. You can now distinguish it from standard universals.
Such propositions are likened to specific (makhsūṣa) or particular (juz'iyya) propositions depending on whether all or most individuals are required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أيضاً لا يثبت أن كل قطرة من هذه القطرات على حدة تكوّن السيل الجارف، ولكن يثبت أن مجموع القطرات بشرط اجتماعها هي التي تكوّن السيل الجارف. ومن الظاهر أن الحكم في مثل هذه القضايا يلاحظ فيه المعنى الجمعي بشرط اجتماع الأفراد، لا أن كلّ فردٍ على سبيل الاستغراق له الحكم نفسه. وقضايا من هذا القبيل تخرج عن دائرة القضايا الكلية، لأن الحكم فيها على المجموع بشرط الاجتماع، لا على الجميع على سبيل الاستغراق الإفرادي. وكلُّ قضية من هذا القبيل: ١ - إما أن تكون بمثابة القضية المخصوصة إذا كان يشترط فيها اجتماع كل الأفراد حتى يصدق الحكم، وذلك كالمثال الأول: كل زوايا المثلث تساوي زاويتين قائمتين، إذ ما لم تجتمع كلُّها فإنها لا تساوي ذلك. ومثل: كلُّ أرجل الكرسي هي التي تعدل قيامه، إذ بعضها لا يكفي للتعديل. ٢ - وإما أن تكون بمثابة القضية الجزئية، إذا كان يكفي فيها اجتماع قسم من الأفراد، وذلك كالمثال الثاني: كلُّ قطرات المطر الغزير تكوّن السيل الجارف، إذ يكفي اجتماع معظمها دون جميعها. ومثل: كلُّ نجوم السماء زينة لها، إذ لا تتم الزينة إلا باجتماعها، ولكن لا يشترط اجتماع الجميع حتى تتحقق الزينة، بل يكفي اجتماع الأكثر.
Again, it is not established that each individual drop of these drops forms the torrent, but it is established that the aggregate of drops under the condition of their conjunction is what forms the torrent. And it is apparent that the judgment in such propositions regards the collective meaning under the condition of the conjunction of individuals, not that each individual on a distributive basis has that same judgment. Propositions of this kind exit the domain of universal propositions, because the judgment therein is upon the aggregate under the condition of conjunction, not upon all on a distributive individual basis. Every proposition of this kind: 1 – Either it is like the specific proposition if the conjunction of all individuals is required for the judgment to be true. That is like the first example: All angles of the triangle equal two right angles, for if they do not all come together, they do not equal that. And like: All legs of the chair are what make it balanced, for some of them are insufficient for balance. 2 – Or it is like the particular proposition if the conjunction of some of the individuals suffices. That is like the second example: All drops of heavy rain form the torrent, for the conjunction of most of them suffices without all of them. And like: All stars of the sky are an adornment for it, for adornment is not achieved except by their conjunction, but the conjunction of all is not required for adornment to be realized; rather, the conjunction of most suffices.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage defines a special class of universal propositions where the predicate applies to the subject as a collective whole, not to each individual distributively. The author distinguishes two types: those that require the conjunction of all individuals (like 'all angles of a triangle sum to 180°') — these are like specific (makhsūṣa) propositions because truth depends on all members; and those where most suffice (like 'all drops of heavy rain form a torrent' or 'all stars adorn the sky'). This nuance is important in Islamic legal reasoning, e.g., when considering collective obligations (furūḍ kifāya) where the duty is on the community as a whole, not on each individual separately. The author emphasizes that such propositions are not truly universal (kulliyya) in the distributive sense, as the judgment does not apply to each individual taken alone.

Two Types of Collective Propositions: All-Required vs Most-Sufficient
Author divides collective propositions into two subtypes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between collective propositions that require the conjunction of all individuals and those where most suffice, and apply this to Islamic legal examples.

Core Idea
Collective universal propositions split into two: those needing all individuals for truth, and those where most suffice.

In the previous lesson, you learned that some universal propositions are collective. But not all collective propositions are alike. The author further divides them: some require every single individual to be conjoined for the predicate to be true, while others only require the majority. This distinction has practical implications in Islamic law, especially for communal obligations.

Guided Path
1
Review the collective universal concept
Recall that in a collective proposition, the predicate applies to the group under a condition of conjunction. The question now: does it require all members or just most?
The author gives two subcategories: like the specific proposition (al-makhsūṣa) requiring all, and like the particular (al-juz'iyya) where most suffice.
2
Identify the 'all-required' subtype
In the first subtype, truth depends on every single individual being included. Example: 'All angles of a triangle sum to 180°' – if any angle is missing, the sum changes. So the proposition is true only if all angles are taken together.
The author calls this 'like the specific proposition' (bi-mithl al-qadiyya al-makhsūṣa).
3
Identify the 'most-sufficient' subtype
In the second subtype, the predicate holds even if a few individuals are absent, as long as the majority are present. Example: 'All drops of heavy rain form a torrent' – even if a few drops evaporate, the remaining majority still form a torrent.
The author calls this 'like the particular proposition' (bi-mithl al-qadiyya al-juz'iyya).
4
Apply to an Islamic legal example
Consider the communal obligation of Friday prayer (Salat al-Jumu'ah). The statement 'All resident adult males must attend Friday prayer' – is it all-required or most-sufficient? In practice, if a majority attend, the obligation is fulfilled; the few absent do not invalidate it. This resembles the most-sufficient subtype.
Contrast with 'All witnesses must give testimony for the hadd penalty to apply' – that requires all witnesses to be present (all-required).

Three Types of Propositions by Relation of Terms: Categorical Propositions
Author divides propositions into categorical, conditional conjunctive, and conditional disjunctive, then explains categorical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish categorical propositions from conditional propositions and identify categorical propositions in Islamic texts.

Core Idea
A categorical proposition makes a direct affirmation or denial without any condition.

In Islamic logic, propositions are classified by how their terms relate. The author introduces three kinds: categorical, conditional conjunctive, and conditional disjunctive. This lesson focuses on the categorical proposition, which is the simplest form—a direct judgment unlinked to any other proposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Read the author's classification: propositions are divided into (1) categorical (ḥamliyya), (2) conditional conjunctive (sharṭiyya muttaṣila), and (3) conditional disjunctive (sharṭiyya munfaṣila). Note the basis: the relation between the terms.
2
Recognize the defining feature of categorical
A categorical proposition is defined as a judgment that simply affirms or denies one thing of another without any conditional link. The judgment stands alone.
The author emphasizes that the judgment is 'not linked to another judgment nor conditioned by it'.
3
Examine the examples
The author gives four examples: 'Zayd is a human' (affirmative), 'Zayd is not a stone' (negative), 'Rain has fallen' (affirmative), 'The sun has not set' (negative). Each is a simple assertion.
Notice that the predicate is directly attached or detached from the subject.
4
Distinguish from conditional propositions
Compare with the conditional types (to be covered later). If the judgment is linked to another judgment by 'if...then' or 'either...or', it is not categorical.
For example, 'If Zayd is human, he is mortal' is conditional, not categorical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام القضايا باعتبار الإضافة بين حدودها: تنقسم القضايا باعتبار الإضافة القائمة بين حدودها إلى ثلاثة أقسام رئيسية، وهي: ١ - القضية الحَمْلِيّة. ٢ - القضية الشرطية المتصلة. ٣ - القضية الشرطية المنفصلة. وذلك لأن الحكم الذي تشتمل عليه الجملة الخبرية (القضية) له ثلاثة أوجه : الوجه الأول : أن يكون الحكم في القضية قائماً على مجرد نسبة شيء إلى شيء آخر أو نفي هذه النسبة بينهما، مثل: زيد إنسان - زيد ليس بحجر - نزل المطر - لم تغرب الشمس . فزيد إنسان : قضية قائمة على حكم موجب هو نسبة الإنسانية إلى زيد، وهذا الحكم غير مرتبط بحكم آخر ولا مشروط به . وزيد ليس بحجر: قضية قائمة على حكم سالب هو نفي نسبة الحجرية إلى زيد، وهذا الحكم غير مرتبط بحكم آخر ولا مشروط به . ونزل المطر: قضية قائمة على حكم موجب هو نسبة النزول إلى المطر، وهذا الحكم أيضاً غير مرتبط بحكم آخر ولا مشروط به . ولم تغرب الشمس : قضية قائمة على حكم سالب هو نفي نسبة الغروب إلى الشمس، وهذا الحكم أيضاً غير مرتبط بحكم آخر ولا مشروط به . وكلُّ قضية (أي : جملة خبرية) من هذا القبيل تسمى : (قضية حملية) وذلك لأن الإضافة فيها قائمة على فكرة حمل أحد حديها على الآخر. الوجه الثاني : أن يكون الحكم في القضية مرتبطاً ارتباطاً شرطياً بحكم آخر، أو يشتمل الحكم فيها على نفي هذا الارتباط الشرطي، أي: فالحكم فيها لا يقتصر على مجرد نسبة شيء إلى شيء آخر أو نفي هذه النسبة بينهما، وإنما
Sections of propositions with respect to the relation between their terms: Propositions are divided with respect to the relation between their terms into three main sections: 1 – The categorical proposition. 2 – The conditional conjunctive proposition. 3 – The conditional disjunctive proposition. That is because the judgment contained in the declarative sentence (the proposition) has three aspects: The first aspect: That the judgment in the proposition be based on mere affirmation of a thing to another thing or negation of that relation between them, such as: Zayd is a human; Zayd is not a stone; rain has fallen; the sun has not set. So 'Zayd is a human' is a proposition based on an affirmative judgment, which is the affirmation of humanness to Zayd, and this judgment is not linked to another judgment nor conditioned by it. And 'Zayd is not a stone' is a proposition based on a negative judgment, which is the negation of stoneness to Zayd, and this judgment is not linked to another judgment nor conditioned by it. And 'rain has fallen' is a proposition based on an affirmative judgment, which is the affirmation of falling to the rain, and this judgment also is not linked to another judgment nor conditioned by it. And 'the sun has not set' is a proposition based on a negative judgment, which is the negation of setting to the sun, and this judgment also is not linked to another judgment nor conditioned by it. And every proposition (i.e., declarative sentence) of this kind is called a 'categorical proposition', because the relation therein is based on the idea of predicating one of its two terms of the other. The second aspect: That the judgment in the proposition be linked conditionally to another judgment, or the judgment therein includes the negation of this conditional link, i.e., the judgment therein is not limited to mere affirmation of a thing to another thing or negation of that relation between them, but rather
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents the first major classification of propositions in Islamic logic. The author divides propositions into three types based on the relation between their terms (al-iḍāfa bayna ḥudūdihā). The first type is the categorical proposition (ḥamliyya), where the judgment is a simple affirmation or denial of one term of another, without any condition linking it to another judgment. The author gives clear examples: 'Zayd is a human' (affirmative), 'Zayd is not a stone' (negative), 'Rain has fallen' (affirmative), 'The sun has not set' (negative). Each is independent; the judgment does not depend on any other proposition. The second type is the conditional conjunctive (sharṭiyya muttaṣila), which involves an 'if...then' link (not fully detailed here). The third is the conditional disjunctive (sharṭiyya munfaṣila), which involves 'either...or'. This classification is foundational, as it determines the form of arguments (syllogisms) that can be constructed. In Islamic legal reasoning, categorical propositions are used in analogical arguments (qiyās) — e.g., 'Wine is intoxicating, therefore forbidden' — where the predicate is affirmed directly.

قضية حملية
قضية شرطية متصلة
قضية شرطية منفصلة

The Three Types of Propositions and the Categorical Proposition
Author lists the three proposition types and defines the categorical proposition with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of propositions in Arabic logic and distinguish the categorical proposition from conditional ones by applying the criterion of a self-contained judgment.

Core Idea
A categorical proposition is a simple affirmation or negation, unrestrained by any conditional link.

In this passage, the author lays out the foundational classification of propositions in Islamic logic. Propositions are divided into three types based on the nature of the judgment they contain. The first type, the categorical proposition (al-qadiyya al-hamliyya), is defined as a judgment that simply affirms or denies a predicate of a subject without any connection to another judgment. Understanding this classification is essential for analyzing arguments in Kalam, Fiqh, and other Islamic sciences, as it determines how premises are structured.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
The author lists three types: categorical (unconditional), conditional conjunctive, and conditional disjunctive. Note the order.
2
Understand the defining criterion
The categorical proposition is defined by the fact that its judgment is not linked to another judgment and is not conditional upon it.
3
Apply the criterion to the examples given
Examine how 'Zaid is a human' and 'Rain fell' are self-contained judgments, while conditional propositions would require a condition (e.g., 'If it rains, the ground gets wet').
4
Recognize the terminology
The Arabic term 'qadiyya hamliyya' means 'categorical proposition', derived from 'haml' (predication), indicating that one term is predicated of the other.

And 'Rain fell' is a proposition based on a positive judgment, which is the affirmation of falling to rain, and this judgment also is not connected to another judgment nor conditional upon it. And 'The sun has not set' is a proposition based on a negative judgment, which is the negation of the relationship of setting to the sun, and this judgment also is not connected to another judgment nor conditional upon it. Commentary
This passage classifies propositions into three types based on the nature of the judgment they contain. The author begins by listing the three: the unconditional (categorical), the conditional conjunctive, and the conditional disjunctive. He then explains the first type: the categorical proposition, where the judgment is a simple affirmation or negation of a relationship between two things, without any connection to another judgment. Examples are given: 'Zaid is a human' (affirmative), 'Zaid is not a stone' (negative), 'Rain fell' (affirmative), 'The sun has not set' (negative). Each example is analyzed to show that the judgment stands alone, not conditional upon anything else. The passage concludes by naming such propositions 'categorical' (hamliyya) because they involve predicating one term of the other.

Key Terms

حكم
جملة خبرية

Key Takeaway
A categorical proposition stands on its own, affirming or denying a predicate of a subject without any condition.

The Conditional Conjunctive Proposition
Author defines conditional conjunctive propositions and analyzes three examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the parts of a conditional conjunctive proposition and distinguish it from categorical propositions.

Core Idea
Conditional propositions connect two judgments, they don't assert the truth of either.

In this lesson, we examine the second type of proposition: the conditional conjunctive. Unlike categorical propositions that simply affirm or deny a relationship between two terms, conditional propositions express a conditional connection between two complete judgments. The author defines this type and illustrates it with three examples drawn from the familiar relation between sunrise and daytime.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining feature
Look for a conditional particle (like 'if'). The proposition must contain two sub-judgments (antecedent and consequent) connected by a conditional relationship.
2
Distinguish the two parts
In the proposition 'If A then B', identify A as the antecedent (the condition) and B as the consequent (the result). Note that the proposition does not affirm A or B alone.
3
Recognize the type of connection
Determine whether the proposition affirms or denies the connection. The author shows three possibilities: affirming the connection (positive), denying the connection of a negative consequent, and denying the connection of an incompatible consequent.
4
Test with an Islamic example
Apply this structure to a sharii ruling: 'If the traveler shortens the prayer, then the prayer is valid.' Identify the antecedent, consequent, and the conditional connection.

The second aspect: that the judgment in the proposition is conditionally connected to another judgment, or the judgment in it includes the negation of this conditional connection, meaning: the judgment in it is not limited to merely affirming a relationship of something to something else or negating this relationship between them, but rather includes affirming the connection between two judgments in two propositions or negating the connection between them. This conditional connection is indicated in speech by a conditional tool (particle), and after this conditional connection the two propositions become one proposition. For example: 1. If the sun is rising, then daytime exists. 2. Absolutely not if the sun is rising, then daytime does not exist. 3. Absolutely not if the sun is rising, then night exists. So in the first example we find the judgment affirming the connection of the existence of daytime to the sun being rising, and it is a conditional connection between a positive judgment and another positive judgment, due to the existence of implication (luzum) between the two judgments. Therefore, the judgment in it after the connection does not definitively assert the existence of daytime, but definitively asserts its existence if the sun is rising. So the proposition gives a positive judgment of the correlation between the two judgments it contains. In the second example we find the judgment negating the connection of the non-existence of daytime to the sun being rising, and it is a negation of a conditional connection between a negative judgment and another positive judgment, because the non-existence of daytime does not necessarily accompany nor coincide with the sun being rising; rather the opposite is true. Therefore, the judgment in it negates the conditional connection between the two judgments it contains. In the third example we find the judgment negating the connection of the existence of night to the sun being rising, and it is a negation of a conditional connection between a positive judgment and another positive judgment, because the existence of night does not necessarily accompany nor coincide with the sun being rising; rather there is opposition between them. Therefore, the judgment in it negates the conditional connection between the two judgments it contains. And every proposition (i.e., declarative sentence) of this kind is called a 'conditional conjunctive proposition' (qadiyya shartiyya muttasila), because the relation in it is based on the idea of a continuous conditional connection between its two parts.

Commentary
This passage explains the second type of proposition, the conditional conjunctive. The author defines it as a proposition where the judgment involves a conditional connection to another judgment, either affirming or negating that connection. The conditional connection is indicated by a conditional particle (like 'if'). The two judgments become a single proposition. Three examples are given, all based on the relationship between sunrise and daytime. The first example affirms the connection: 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists.' This shows an implication (luzum). The second example negates the connection: 'It is not the case that if the sun is rising, then daytime does not exist.' This negates a connection between a negative consequent and the antecedent. The third example negates the connection between the antecedent and an incompatible consequent: 'It is not the case that if the sun is rising, then night exists.' The author emphasizes that these propositions do not assert the truth of the antecedent or consequent independently, but only the connection between them. The passage concludes by naming such propositions 'conditional conjunctive' (shartiyya muttasila).

Definition and Components of Connected Conditional Propositions
Definition and examples of connected conditional propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the antecedent, consequent, and type of connection (affirming or negating) in any connected conditional proposition from an Islamic logical or legal text.

Core Idea
A conditional proposition does not assert its parts, only the connection between them.

Conditional propositions are the building blocks of hypothetical reasoning in Islamic logic. In this lesson, we explore the definition of the connected conditional proposition (al-qadiyya al-shartiyya al-muttasila) as laid out by the author. Through three carefully chosen examples, you will learn to distinguish between affirming a connection based on implication (luzum) and negating a connection based on opposition (ta'anud).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of the conditional
Every connected conditional proposition has an antecedent (the condition) and a consequent (the result). In the first example, the antecedent is 'the sun is rising' and the consequent is 'daytime exists'. Recognise that these are not asserted as facts independently; only their connection is asserted.
The antecedent comes after the conditional tool (e.g., 'if').
2
Determine whether the connection is affirmed or negated
The author gives three examples: one affirms the connection (example 1), and two negate it (examples 2 and 3). Look for the phrase 'absolutely not' (laysa al-batta) which signals negation of the conditional connection, not negation of the consequent itself.
Negation applies to the entire conditional relation, not to the individual parts.
3
Examine the logical relation: luzum vs. ta'anud
In example 1, the connection is based on implication (luzum): the rising of the sun necessarily entails daytime. In example 3, the connection is negated because of opposition (ta'anud): the rising of the sun and the existence of night are incompatible. Example 2 negates the connection because the opposite (daytime not existing) is incompatible with the antecedent.
Luzum is a necessary accompaniment; ta'anud is mutual exclusion.
4
Understand the shift from categorical to conditional
The author explains that a conditional proposition originates from two separate categorical propositions. However, the speaker's goal is not to assert those categoricals as true, but to highlight the conditional relation. This is a key insight: a conditional proposition is not about the truth of its parts but about the truth of the connection.
This distinguishes a conditional from a conjunction or disjunction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

١ - إذا كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود.
٢ - ليس البتة إذا كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار ليس بموجود.
٣ - ليس البتة إذا كانت الشمس طالعة فالليل موجود.
ففي المثال الأول نجد الحكم بإثبات ارتباط وجود النهار بأن تكون الشمس طالعة، وهو ارتباط شرطي بين حكم موجب وحكم موجب آخر، وذلك لوجود اللزوم بين الحكمين، إذن فالحكم فيها بعد الارتباط لا يقطع بوجود النهار، ولكن يقطع بوجوده إذا كانت الشمس طالعة، فتعطي القضية حكماً إيجابياً بالترابط بين الحكمين اللذين اشتملت عليهما.
وفي المثال الثاني نجد الحكم بنفي ارتباط عدم وجود النهار بأن تكون الشمس طالعة، وهو نفي لارتباط شرطي بين حكم سالب وحكم آخر موجب، وذلك لأن عدم وجود النهار لا يلازم ولا يصاحب كون الشمس طالعة، بل العكس هو الصحيح، إذن فالحكم فيها يسلب الارتباط الشرطي بين الحكمين اللذين اشتملت عليهما.

وكلُّ قضية (أي: جملة خبرية) من هذا القبيل تسمى: (قضية شرطية متصلة) وذلك لأن الإضافة فيها قائمة على فكرة الارتباط الشرطي المتصل بين حديها.
وإذا تأملنا في هذه القضية الشرطية وجدنا أنها في الأصل هي قضيتان منفكتان: (الشمس طالعة - النهار موجود) وهاتان القضيتان المنفكتان هما حمليتان، ولكن قد لا يكون غرض المتكلم أن يقول: (إن النهار موجود فعلاً) وإنما يكون غرضه أن يكشف الاتصال بين طلوع الشمس ووجود النهار، فيعلق الحكم بوجود النهار على الحكم بطلوع الشمس، ويصدر بذلك حكماً شرطياً.
It includes affirming the connection between two judgments in two propositions or negating the connection between them. This conditional connection is indicated in speech by a tool from the conditional tools, and after this conditional connection the two propositions become one proposition. Such as: 1 - If the sun is rising, then daytime exists. 2 - Absolutely not if the sun is rising, then daytime does not exist. 3 - Absolutely not if the sun is rising, then night exists. In the first example we find the judgment affirming the connection of the existence of daytime with the sun being rising, which is a conditional connection between a positive judgment and another positive judgment, due to the existence of implication (luzum) between the two judgments. Therefore, the judgment in it after the connection does not decide the existence of daytime, but decides its existence if the sun is rising, so the proposition gives a positive judgment of correlation between the two judgments it contains. In the second example we find the judgment negating the connection of the non-existence of daytime with the sun being rising, which is a negation of a conditional connection between a negative judgment and another positive judgment, because the non-existence of daytime does not accompany nor go along with the sun being rising; rather the opposite is correct. Therefore, the judgment in it negates the conditional connection between the two judgments it contains. In the third example we find the judgment negating the connection of the existence of night with the sun being rising, which is a negation of a conditional connection between a positive judgment and another positive judgment, because the existence of night does not accompany nor go along with the sun being rising; rather there is opposition (ta'anud) between them. Therefore, the judgment in it negates the conditional connection between the two judgments it contains. And every proposition (i.e., declarative sentence) of this kind is called: (connected conditional proposition) because the addition in it is based on the idea of the connected conditional connection between its two limits. And if we contemplate this conditional proposition, we find that originally it is two separate propositions: (The sun is rising - Daytime exists). And these two separate propositions are categorical, but the speaker's purpose may not be to say: (Indeed daytime exists actually), rather his purpose is to reveal the connection between the rising of the sun and the existence of daytime, so he suspends the judgment of the existence of daytime on the judgment of the rising of the sun, and thereby issues a conditional judgment.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage defines the connected conditional proposition (al-qadiyya al-shartiyya al-muttasila). The author begins by stating that this type of proposition involves affirming or negating a connection between two judgments. The conditional connection is indicated by conditional tools like 'if' (idha). After the connection, two separate propositions become one compound proposition. The author then provides three examples:

1. 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists': This affirms a positive connection between the antecedent (sun rising) and consequent (daytime exists) because there is an implication (luzum) between them. Note that the proposition does not assert that daytime exists actually; it only asserts that daytime exists if the sun rises.

2. 'Absolutely not if the sun is rising, then daytime does not exist': This negates the connection between the antecedent and a negative consequent (daytime does not exist). The negation is correct because the sun rising implies daytime exists, not its opposite.

3. 'Absolutely not if the sun is rising, then night exists': This negates the connection between the antecedent and a positive consequent that is incompatible (night exists). Again, the negation is correct because there is opposition (ta'anud) between the antecedent and consequent.

The author then summarizes that all such propositions are called 'connected conditional propositions' because the relation is based on a connected conditional link between the two parts (the antecedent and consequent). He further explains that a conditional proposition originates from two separate categorical propositions, but the speaker's intent is not to assert the truth of either part independently; rather, it is to reveal the conditional connection. Thus, the judgment is suspended on the condition.

Key Terms
قضية شرطية متصلة (qadiyya shartiyya muttasila): A proposition that connects two judgments such that the truth of the consequent is affirmed or negated conditionally upon the antecedent, indicated by a conditional tool like 'if'. [Note: This term is stable across classical logic works; al-Farabi and Ibn Sina use it similarly.]
ارتباط شرطي (irtibat sharti): The conditional connection between the two parts of a conditional proposition, which can be affirmed or negated.
لزوم (luzum): Implication or necessary accompaniment between the antecedent and consequent; here it is the basis for affirming the connection. [Note: In some later texts, luzum may be distinguished from 'in' (conditional) in terms of modal force; here it is used simply as logical implication.]
تعاند (ta'anud): Mutual opposition or incompatibility; here it describes the relation between the antecedent and a consequent that is opposed to what it implies.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It affirms a connection between two positive judgments.
B) It negates a connection between a positive and a negative judgment.
C) It negates a connection between two positive judgments.
D) It affirms a connection based on ta'anud.

Key Takeaway
A connected conditional proposition expresses a conditional link between two judgments, not the truth of either judgment independently.

Disjunctive Propositions: Definition and Resolution into Conditionals
Author describes disjunctive propositions as judgments between possibilities, and resolves them into multiple conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze any disjunctive proposition into its equivalent set of conditional propositions.

Core Idea
A disjunctive proposition is not a simple either-or; it is a pack of conditionals.

Disjunctive propositions express hesitation between alternatives. The author explains that such propositions are not merely two contradictory statements; they logically resolve into multiple conditional propositions. Understanding this resolution is key to working with disjunctive syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive structure
Look for the phrase 'imma...imma' in the proposition. This signals a disjunctive judgment between two or more possibilities. Note the alternatives listed.
The number of alternatives may be two or more; the analysis focuses on two for simplicity.
2
Determine the type of disjunction
Check whether the disjunction is exclusive (one and only one alternative can be true) or inclusive (at least one is true). In the examples given (even/odd, rising/setting), it is exclusive: both cannot be true and one must be true.
Exclusive disjunction is called 'mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluww' in standard terminology.
3
Resolve into conditional pairs
For two alternatives A and B in an exclusive disjunction, write four conditionals: (1) If A then not B, (2) If B then not A, (3) If not A then B, (4) If not B then A. This captures both exclusivity and exhaustiveness.
This is the logical equivalence: the disjunction is true exactly when all four conditionals hold.
4
Verify the equivalence
Confirm that the set of conditionals means the same as the original disjunction. For example, 'Either the number is even or odd' is equivalent to the four conditionals about evenness and oddness.
This verification is a mental check; the author explicitly states 'this proposition is in terms of meaning equivalent to four connected conditionals.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث :
أن يكون الحكم في القضية متردداً بين احتمالين فأكثر، وحينما يلاحظ من يريد إصدار الحكم انحصار التردد بين عددٍ من الوجوه أو الاحتمالات فإنه يعبر عن ذلك بمثل قوله : إما أن يكون الأمر كذا وإما أن يكون الأمر كذا، أي : لا يخلو عن واحد منهما أو لا يخلو عنهما، مثل:
١ - إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً وإما أن يكون فرداً.
٢ - جليس السوء إما أن يغويك وإما أن يوقعك في التهمة.
٣ - إما أن تكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن تكون غاربة.
وإذا حللنا الحكم في القضايا التي من هذا القبيل وجدناه يرجع من جهة المعنى إلى قضيتين شرطيتين فأكثر.
فقولنا: (إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً وإما أن يكون فرداً) ينحل من جهة المعنى إلى ما يلي :
إذا كان العدد زوجاً فهو غير فرد. وإذا كان العدد فرداً فهو غير زوج.
وإذا لم يكن العدد زوجاً فهو فرد. وإذا لم يكن العدد فرداً فهو زوج.
إذن فهذه القضية من جهة المعنى بقوة أربع قضايا شرطية متصلة.
وقولنا: (جليس السوء إما أن يغويك وإما أن يوقعك في التهمة) ينحل من جهة المعنى إلى ما يلي :
إذا كان الجليس يغويك فهو لا يوقعك في التهمة. وإذا كان الجليس يوقعك في التهمة فهو لا يغويك.
وإذا لم يكن الجليس يغويك فهو يوقعك في التهمة. وإذا لم يكن الجليس يوقعك في التهمة فهو يغويك.
The third aspect: That the judgment in the proposition is hesitant between two or more possibilities. And when the one who wants to issue a judgment observes the limitation of hesitation to a number of aspects or possibilities, he expresses that with his statement: Either the matter is such and such, or the matter is such and such; i.e., it is not free from one of them, or it is not free from both. For example: 1 - Either the number is even, or it is odd. 2 - The evil companion either will mislead you, or will cause you to fall into suspicion. 3 - Either the sun is rising, or it is setting. And if we analyze the judgment in propositions of this kind, we find that in terms of meaning, it returns to two or more conditional propositions. For our statement: (Either the number is even, or it is odd) resolves in terms of meaning into the following: If the number is even, then it is not odd. And if the number is odd, then it is not even. And if the number is not even, then it is odd. And if it is not odd, then it is even. Therefore, this proposition is in terms of meaning equivalent to four connected conditional propositions. And our statement: (The evil companion either will mislead you or will cause you to fall into suspicion) resolves in terms of meaning into the following: If the companion misleads you, then he does not cause you to fall into suspicion. And if the companion causes you to fall into suspicion, then he does not mislead you. And if the companion does not mislead you, then he causes you to fall into suspicion. And if the companion does not cause you to fall into suspicion, then he misleads you.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage describes the third aspect of judgments in propositions: the disjunctive proposition (also called al-qadiyya al-shartiyya al-munfasila in standard terminology). The author characterizes it as a judgment that is hesitant or divided between two or more possibilities. When the judge perceives that the hesitation is confined to a limited set of alternatives, he expresses it using 'either...or' (imma...imma). The author gives three examples:
1. Either the number is even, or it is odd. (This is an exclusive disjunction because a number cannot be both even and odd; it's a case of mutual exclusivity.)
2. The evil companion either will mislead you or cause you to fall into suspicion. (This could be exclusive or non-exclusive depending on context; the author analyzes it as exclusive based on the resolution into conditionals.)
3. Either the sun is rising, or it is setting. (Again exclusive.)

Then the author shows that these disjunctive propositions can be analyzed in terms of meaning into multiple conditional propositions. For the first example, 'Either the number is even or it is odd' resolves into four conditionals: (1) If it is even, it is not odd. (2) If it is odd, it is not even. (3) If it is not even, it is odd. (4) If it is not odd, it is even. This analysis reveals that the disjunctive proposition is logically equivalent to a conjunction of these conditionals, capturing both the exclusivity (either-or but not both) and the exhaustiveness (one must be true). For the second example, a similar resolution is given, though the text on page 84 cuts off mid-analysis. The author intends to show that disjunctive propositions are reducible to connected conditionals, which is a key insight in classical logic for understanding the logical relationships between alternatives.

Disjunctive Conditionals: Reduction and Types
Author analyzes disjunctive conditionals, reducing them to multiple conjunctive conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce any disjunctive conditional proposition into its equivalent set of conjunctive conditional propositions and classify its type.

Core Idea
Disjunctive conditionals reduce to multiple conjunctive conditionals, revealing different logical relations.

The author introduces the third type of proposition: the disjunctive conditional, where the judgment vacillates between two or more possibilities. By analyzing examples, he shows that such propositions reduce to sets of conjunctive conditionals, each with its own logical structure. This reduction is essential for understanding the precise meaning of disjunctive statements in Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive proposition
Locate the statement with 'either...or' (إما أن يكون) and note the disjuncts.
The examples give three distinct types of disjunction.
2
List all truth possibilities
Determine which combinations of truth values for the disjuncts are possible: both true, both false, or one true one false.
This depends on whether the disjunction is exclusive, inclusive, or complementary.
3
Translate each possibility into a conjunctive conditional
For each disjunct's truth or falsity, write an 'if...then' statement that captures the consequence.
For example, from 'P or Q' we derive: if P then not Q, if Q then not P, if not P then Q, if not Q then P.
4
Classify the disjunction type
Determine whether the disjunction is maniat al-jam (mutually exclusive but not exhaustive), maniat al-khuluw (exhaustive but not exclusive), or maniat al-jam wal-khuluw (both exclusive and exhaustive).
The author's examples illustrate each type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : أن يكون الحكم في القضية متردداً بين احتمالين فأكثر، وحينما يلاحظ من يريد إصدار الحكم انحصار التردد بين عددٍ من الوجوه أو الاحتمالات فإنه يعبر عن ذلك بمثل قوله : إما أن يكون الأمر كذا وإما أن يكون الأمر كذا، أي : لا يخلو عن واحد منهما أو لا يخلو عنهما، مثل: ١ - إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً وإما أن يكون فرداً. ٢ - جليس السوء إما أن يغويك وإما أن يوقعك في التهمة. ٣ - إما أن تكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن تكون غاربة. وإذا حللنا الحكم في القضايا التي من هذا القبيل وجدناه يرجع من جهة المعنى إلى قضيتين شرطيتين فأكثر. فقولنا: (إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً وإما أن يكون فرداً) ينحل من جهة المعنى إلى ما يلي : إذا كان العدد زوجاً فهو غير فرد. وإذا كان العدد فرداً فهو غير زوج. وإذا لم يكن العدد زوجاً فهو فرد. وإذا لم يكن العدد فرداً فهو زوج. إذن فهذه القضية من جهة المعنى بقوة أربع قضايا شرطية متصلة. وقولنا: (جليس السوء إما أن يغويك وإما أن يوقعك في التهمة) ينحل من جهة المعنى إلى ما يلي : إذا لم يُغوك جليس السوء أوقعك في التهمة. وإذا لم يوقعك في التهمة أغواك. أي: ولا يخلو الأمر عن واحد منهما وربما يجتمع الأمران. وقولنا: (إما أن تكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن تكون غاربة) ينحل من جهة المعنى إلى ما يلي: إذا كانت طالعة فهي غير غاربة. وإذا كانت غاربة فهي غير طالعة. أي: فلا يجتمع الأمران، ولكن قد يخلو الحال عنهما، وذلك بأن تكون مكسوفة أو محجوبة بالسحاب. وكلُّ قضية من هذا القبيل تسمى: (قضية شرطية منفصلة) وذلك لأن الإضافة فيها قائمة على فكرة الانفصال في الصيغة بين حكمين فأكثر، وبتحليلها ترجع إلى عدد من القضايا الشرطية المتصلة. خلاصة الوجوه: بهذا التحليل لهذه القضايا الثلاث يتضح لنا أن القضية الحملية تشتمل على حكم بين شيئين دون ارتباط بحكم آخر، فهي بسيطة، وأن القضية الشرطية المتصلة تشتمل على حكم بين شيئين مرتبط بحكم آخر، فهي مركبة تركيباً متوسطاً، وأن القضية الشرطية المنفصلة تشتمل على حكم مترددٍ بين شيئين فأكثر، وهي تنحل من جهة المعنى إلى شرطيتين متصلتين فأكثر، فهي مركبة تركيباً أعلى.
The third aspect: That the judgment in the proposition is vacillating between two or more possibilities. When the one who intends to issue the judgment observes that the vacillation is confined to a number of aspects or possibilities, he expresses that by saying, for example: 'Either the matter is such, or the matter is such,' i.e., it is not free from one of them, or it is not free from both, such as: 1. Either the number is even or it is odd. 2. The evil companion either leads you astray or causes you to be suspected. 3. Either the sun is rising or it is setting. And when we analyze the judgment in propositions of this kind, we find it, from the perspective of meaning, reduces to two or more conditional propositions. So our statement: 'Either the number is even or it is odd' reduces, from the perspective of meaning, to the following: If the number is even, then it is not odd. If the number is odd, then it is not even. If the number is not even, then it is odd. If the number is not odd, then it is even. Thus this proposition, from the perspective of meaning, is equivalent to four conjunctive conditional propositions. And our statement: 'The evil companion either leads you astray or causes you to be suspected' reduces, from the perspective of meaning, to the following: If the evil companion does not lead you astray, he causes you to be suspected; and if he does not cause you to be suspected, he leads you astray. That is, the matter is not free from one of the two, and perhaps both may be combined. And our statement: 'Either the sun is rising or it is setting' reduces, from the perspective of meaning, to the following: If it is rising, then it is not setting; and if it is setting, then it is not rising. That is, the two do not combine, but it is possible for the situation to be free of both, such as when it is eclipsed or hidden by clouds. Every proposition of this kind is called a 'disjunctive conditional proposition' because the attachment in it is based on the idea of disjunction in form between two or more judgments, and by analysis it reduces to a number of conjunctive conditional propositions. Summary of the aspects: By this analysis of these three propositions, it becomes clear to us that the categorical proposition contains a judgment between two things without connection to another judgment, so it is simple; the conjunctive conditional proposition contains a judgment between two things connected to another judgment, so it is moderately compound; and the disjunctive conditional proposition contains a judgment vacillating between two or more things, and it reduces from the perspective of meaning to two or more conjunctive conditionals, so it is more highly compound.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is an analytical exposition of disjunctive conditional propositions (القضية الشرطية المنفصلة). The author begins by describing the third type of proposition, where the judgment is vacillating between two or more possibilities. The speaker expresses this with 'either...or' constructions. Three examples are given: a mathematical example (number parity), a moral example (evil companion), and a natural example (sun position). The author then shows how each disjunctive statement can be reduced to a set of conjunctive conditional statements. For the parity example, it reduces to four conjunctive conditionals covering all possibilities. For the evil companion, it reduces to two conjunctive conditionals indicating that at least one of the outcomes occurs (and possibly both). For the sun example, it reduces to two conjunctive conditionals that are mutually exclusive but not exhaustive (they allow for a third state, eclipse). The author defines the disjunctive conditional as a proposition where the relation is based on disjunction and reduces to multiple conjunctive conditionals. Finally, a summary contrasts the three proposition types: categorical (simple, between two things without another judgment), conjunctive conditional (moderately compound, linking two judgments), and disjunctive conditional (more highly compound, vacillating and reducible to multiple conjunctives).

Three Types of Propositions and Their Complexity
Author analyzes disjunctive propositions and summarizes three proposition types with their complexity levels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any proposition as categorical, conjunctive conditional, or disjunctive conditional, and determine its complexity level.

Core Idea
Proposition type determines argument complexity: simple, medium, or higher compound.

In this passage, the author introduces the three fundamental proposition types in Islamic logic. He uses concrete examples—such as the evil companion and the sun—to illustrate how disjunctive propositions work, and then summarizes each type's complexity. Understanding this classification is essential for analyzing the structure of any argument in the Islamic intellectual tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three proposition types
Find the terms in the Arabic for categorical (حملیة), conjunctive conditional (شرطیة متصلة), and disjunctive conditional (شرطیة منفصلة). Notice that the author presents them in order of increasing complexity.
2
Understand the definitions
Read the author's definitions: categorical is a judgment between two things without connection to another judgment; conjunctive conditional has a judgment between two things connected to another judgment (antecedent-consequent); disjunctive conditional alternates between two or more judgments.
Focus on the phrase 'مرتبط بحكم آخر' (connected to another judgment) for conditionals.
3
Compare complexity levels
Note the author's explicit ranking: categorical is simple (بسیطة), conjunctive conditional is medium compound (مركبة تركيباً متوسطاً), disjunctive conditional is higher compound (مركبة تركيباً أعلى). This is based on whether the proposition contains sub-judgments.
Disjunctive is 'higher' because it resolves into multiple conjunctive conditionals.
4
Classify examples
Given a proposition, decide which type it is. For instance, 'The world is created' is categorical; 'If the moon is eclipsed, then the sun is not visible' is conjunctive conditional; 'Either this act is wajib or it is haram' is disjunctive conditional.
Use the presence of 'إما...أو' for disjunctive and 'إذا...فإن' for conjunctive.

If an evil companion does not lead you astray, he causes you to fall into suspicion. And if he does not cause you to fall into suspicion, he leads you astray. That is, the matter is not free from one of the two, and perhaps both combine. And our statement: (Either the sun is rising or the sun is setting) resolves in terms of meaning to the following: If it is rising then it is not setting, and if it is setting then it is not rising. That is, the two do not combine, but the situation may be devoid of both, such as when it is eclipsed or obscured by clouds. Every proposition of this kind is called a (disjunctive conditional proposition), because the addition in it is based on the idea of disjunction in form between two or more judgments, and when analyzed it returns to a number of conjunctive conditional propositions.
