Commentary
This passage introduces the three fundamental types of propositions in logic: categorical (حملیة), conjunctive conditional (شرطیة متصلة), and disjunctive conditional (شرطیة منفصلة). The author begins with an example about an evil companion to illustrate a disjunctive situation, then moves to the logical example of the sun (either rising or setting). He shows that a disjunctive proposition can be analyzed as a combination of conjunctive conditionals: 'If it is rising then it is not setting' and 'If it is setting then it is not rising.' He notes that the two parts cannot both be true (mutually exclusive) but may both be false (if the sun is eclipsed). The author then summarizes the three types: categorical is simple, involving a judgment between two things without another judgment; conjunctive conditional is medium compound, involving a judgment between two things connected to another judgment (the antecedent and consequent); disjunctive conditional is higher compound, involving an alternation between two or more things and resolvable into multiple conjunctive conditionals. This classification is foundational for understanding the structure of arguments.

What is a Categorical Proposition?
Author defines categorical proposition and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given Islamic theological statement is a categorical proposition by checking if it involves attributing or denying a predicate to a subject.

Core Idea
The categorical proposition is a judgment that ascribes or denies something about something else.

The author begins by distilling the essence of the categorical proposition from the earlier discussion. He gives two equivalent definitions and four examples from Islamic theology. In this lesson, we will learn to apply these definitions to any statement we encounter in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two core elements
Look for two things: the thing being talked about (subject) and what is said about it (predicate). The judgment either attaches the predicate to the subject (positive) or separates them (negative).
2
Test the first definition
Ask: Does the statement attribute something to something else or deny it? If yes, it is a categorical proposition according to the first definition.
Example: 'Allah is our Lord' attributes Lordship to Allah.
3
Test the second definition
Ask: Does the statement judge a positive or negative relation between an ascribee and an ascription? If yes, it fits the second definition.
Example: 'Allah has no partner' judges a negative relation.
4
Apply to new examples
Take a statement from the Quran or Hadith and check if it is a categorical proposition using the above criteria.
For instance, 'The prayer is obligatory' – it attributes obligation to prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من البيان السابق للقضايا نستطيع أن نستخلص تعريفاً للقضية الحملية فنقول: هي التي يكون الحكم فيها قائماً على إسناد شيءٍ إلى شيءٍ آخر أو نفيه عنه. أو هي التي يحكم فيها بعلاقة موجبة أو سالبة بين مُسند ومسندٍ إليه. الأمثلة: أ ـ فقولنا: (الله ربنا) قضية حملية لأنها اشتملت على حكم بإسناد وصف الربوبية إلى الله تعالى، أو نقول بعبارة أخرى: إنها قد اشتملت على حكم بعلاقة موجبة بين الله تعالى ووصفه بالربوبية. ب ـ وقولنا : (الله لا شريك له قضية حملية، لأنها اشتملت على حكم بنفي وجود شريك الله تعالى، أو نقول بعبارة أخرى: إنها قد اشتملت على حكم بعلاقة سالبة بين الله تعالى وبين شريك له . جـ ـ وقولنا : (سمع الله لمن حمده قضية حملية، لأنها اشتملت على حكم بإسناد سماع قول الحامد إلى الله تعالى . د ـ وقولنا : (لا يعلم الناس الغيب قضية حملية، لأنها اشتملت على حكم بنفي علم الغيب عن الناس. ومن الملاحظ أن المثالين الأولين هما من قبيل الجمل الاسمية، وأما المثالان الأخيران فهما من قبيل الجمل الفعلية . فالقضية الحملية إذن فيها مسند ومسند إليه بحسب اصطلاح النحاة، أو محكوم به ومحكوم عليه بحسب اصطلاح البلاغيين، وأما في اصطلاح المناطقة فالمسند إليه هو (الموضوع) سواء أكان مبتدأ أو فاعلاً أو ما هو قائم مقام أحدهما، والمسند هو (المحمول) سواء أكان خبراً أو فعلاً أو ما هو قائم مقام أحدهما . وسبب هذه التسمية أن المناطقة يلاحظون أن المسند إليه يوضع أولاً في التصور ثم يحمل عليه المسند، فسموا المسند إليه موضوعاً والمسند محمولاً وسموا القضية كلها قضية حملية نسبة إلى معنى الحمل الموجب أو السالب الذي يجري فيها، أما النسبة بين الموضوع والمحمول فهي الرابطة الملاحظة في الذهن، وأما في الكلام فيدلُّ عليها دال ما، وإما أن يكون مجرد الاقتران اللفظي، كقولنا : (جاء موسى) وإما أن يكون حركة إعراب ظاهرة، كقولنا : (جاء محمد) وإما أن يستخدم للدلالة عليها ضمير الفصل، وهو رابط غير زماني، كقولنا : (الله هو ربنا أو فعل الكون، وهو رابط زماني، كقولنا : (إن الله كان عليماً حكيماً).
From the previous statement about propositions we can extract a definition for the categorical proposition, so we say: It is that in which the judgment is based on attributing something to something else or denying it from it. Or: It is that in which a positive or negative relation is judged between an ascribee and an ascription. Examples: A) Our statement: (Allah is our Lord) is a categorical proposition because it includes a judgment by attributing the description of Lordship to Allah Almighty, or in other words: it includes a judgment of a positive relation between Allah Almighty and His description of Lordship. B) Our statement: (Allah has no partner) is a categorical proposition because it includes a judgment by negating the existence of a partner to Allah Almighty, or in other words: it includes a judgment of a negative relation between Allah Almighty and a partner for Him. C) Our statement: (Allah hears whoever praises Him) is a categorical proposition because it includes a judgment by attributing hearing the praise of the praiser to Allah Almighty. D) Our statement: (People do not know the unseen) is a categorical proposition because it includes a judgment by negating knowledge of the unseen from people. It is observed that the first two examples are of the nominal sentence type, while the last two are of the verbal sentence type. The categorical proposition therefore contains an ascribee and an ascription according to the terminology of grammarians, or a judged and a judge according to the terminology of rhetoricians, but in the terminology of logicians the ascribee is the (subject) whether it is a mubtada', a fa'il, or what stands in place of either, and the ascription is the (predicate) whether it is a khabar, a fi'l, or what stands in place of either. The reason for this naming is that the logicians observe that the ascribee is placed first in conception then the ascription is predicated on it, so they named the ascribee 'subject' and the ascription 'predicate', and they named the entire proposition a categorical proposition relative to the meaning of positive or negative predication that runs in it. As for the relation between subject and predicate, it is the copula observed in the mind, while in speech it is indicated by some indicator, either mere verbal juxtaposition, like our saying: (Musa came), or an apparent inflectional vowel, like our saying: (Muhammad came), or a separating pronoun is used to indicate it, which is a non-temporal link, like our saying: (Allah, He is our Lord), or the verb of existence, which is a temporal link, like our saying: (Indeed Allah is All-Knowing, All-Wise).
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage defines the categorical proposition (قضية حملية) and explains its essential components. The author first gives two equivalent definitions: a judgment based on attributing or denying something to something else, or a judgment of a positive or negative relation between an ascribee (مسند إليه) and an ascription (مسند). He then provides four examples from Islamic theological context: two nominal sentences ('Allah is our Lord' and 'Allah has no partner') and two verbal sentences ('Allah hears whoever praises Him' and 'People do not know the unseen'). These illustrate positive and negative judgments. The author then explains the terminology: grammarians call the two parts 'ascribee' (مسند إليه) and 'ascription' (مسند); rhetoricians call them 'judged' (محكوم عليه) and 'judge' (محكوم به); logicians call them 'subject' (موضوع) and 'predicate' (محمول). The reason for the logicians' terms is that the subject is placed first mentally, then the predicate is 'carried' (يحمل) upon it. The relation between them is the copula (رابطة), which is mentally understood but in speech can be indicated by mere word order (e.g., 'Musa came' - juxtaposition), inflection (e.g., 'Muhammad came' - the damma indicates the subject), a separating pronoun (e.g., 'Allah, He is our Lord' - the pronoun 'He' acts as a non-temporal link), or a verb of existence (e.g., 'Indeed Allah is All-Knowing, All-Wise' - كان is a temporal copula). This careful analysis shows that the categorical proposition is the simplest unit of judgment in logic.

Key Terms
قضية حملية
موضوع
محمول
رابطة

D) All men are mortal.

A categorical proposition is any statement that ascribes or denies something about a subject.

Identifying Subject and Predicate in Categorical Propositions
Author explains subject-predicate terminology and its correlation with grammar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject (موضوع) and predicate (محمول) in any Arabic categorical proposition from Islamic sources, regardless of its grammatical form.

Core Idea
In logic, the subject is what you talk about, and the predicate is what you say about it, regardless of grammar.

The author explains that the terms 'subject' and 'predicate' in logic correspond to the grammatical ascribee and ascription. But they are not always a noun and a verb – the predicate can be a verb, a khabar, or anything that completes the sense. In this lesson, we will practise extracting subject and predicate from different sentence types.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the logical subject
The subject is the thing being talked about. It can be a noun (mubtada'), a verb's agent (fa'il), or anything that stands in place of those. In 'Zaid came', Zaid is subject; in 'The book is useful', the book is subject.
2
Recognize the logical predicate
The predicate is what is said about the subject. It can be a noun (khabar), a verb (fi'l), or its substitute. In 'Zaid came', 'came' is predicate; in 'The book is useful', 'useful' is predicate.
3
Distinguish from grammatical terms
Note that logical subject is not always the grammatical subject. In 'It is Zaid who came', the logical subject is still Zaid, even if grammatically it is a khabar.
The author's examples: 'Allah is our Lord' – subject Allah, predicate our Lord; 'Allah hears whoever praises Him' – subject Allah, predicate hears.
4
Practice on new sentences
Take sentences from the Quran, like 'Indeed, Allah is Forgiving and Merciful' and identify subject and predicate.
In this example, the subject is Allah, the predicate is 'Forgiving and Merciful' (as khabar of inna).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من البيان السابق للقضايا نستطيع أن نستخلص تعريفاً للقضية الحملية فنقول: هي التي يكون الحكم فيها قائماً على إسناد شيءٍ إلى شيءٍ آخر أو نفيه عنه. أو هي التي يحكم فيها بعلاقة موجبة أو سالبة بين مُسند ومسندٍ إليه. الأمثلة: أ ـ فقولنا: (الله ربنا) قضية حملية لأنها اشتملت على حكم بإسناد وصف الربوبية إلى الله تعالى، أو نقول بعبارة أخرى: إنها قد اشتملت على حكم بعلاقة موجبة بين الله تعالى ووصفه بالربوبية. ب ـ وقولنا : (الله لا شريك له قضية حملية، لأنها اشتملت على حكم بنفي وجود شريك الله تعالى، أو نقول بعبارة أخرى: إنها قد اشتملت على حكم بعلاقة سالبة بين الله تعالى وبين شريك له . جـ ـ وقولنا : (سمع الله لمن حمده قضية حملية، لأنها اشتملت على حكم بإسناد سماع قول الحامد إلى الله تعالى . د ـ وقولنا : (لا يعلم الناس الغيب قضية حملية، لأنها اشتملت على حكم بنفي علم الغيب عن الناس. ومن الملاحظ أن المثالين الأولين هما من قبيل الجمل الاسمية، وأما المثالان الأخيران فهما من قبيل الجمل الفعلية . فالقضية الحملية إذن فيها مسند ومسند إليه بحسب اصطلاح النحاة، أو محكوم به ومحكوم عليه بحسب اصطلاح البلاغيين، وأما في اصطلاح المناطقة فالمسند إليه هو (الموضوع) سواء أكان مبتدأ أو فاعلاً أو ما هو قائم مقام أحدهما، والمسند هو (المحمول) سواء أكان خبراً أو فعلاً أو ما هو قائم مقام أحدهما . وسبب هذه التسمية أن المناطقة يلاحظون أن المسند إليه يوضع أولاً في التصور ثم يحمل عليه المسند، فسموا المسند إليه موضوعاً والمسند محمولاً وسموا القضية كلها قضية حملية نسبة إلى معنى الحمل الموجب أو السالب الذي يجري فيها، أما النسبة بين الموضوع والمحمول فهي الرابطة الملاحظة في الذهن، وأما في الكلام فيدلُّ عليها دال ما، وإما أن يكون مجرد الاقتران اللفظي، كقولنا : (جاء موسى) وإما أن يكون حركة إعراب ظاهرة، كقولنا : (جاء محمد) وإما أن يستخدم للدلالة عليها ضمير الفصل، وهو رابط غير زماني، كقولنا : (الله هو ربنا أو فعل الكون، وهو رابط زماني، كقولنا : (إن الله كان عليماً حكيماً).
From the previous statement about propositions we can extract a definition for the categorical proposition, so we say: It is that in which the judgment is based on attributing something to something else or denying it from it. Or: It is that in which a positive or negative relation is judged between an ascribee and an ascription. Examples: A) Our statement: (Allah is our Lord) is a categorical proposition because it includes a judgment by attributing the description of Lordship to Allah Almighty, or in other words: it includes a judgment of a positive relation between Allah Almighty and His description of Lordship. B) Our statement: (Allah has no partner) is a categorical proposition because it includes a judgment by negating the existence of a partner to Allah Almighty, or in other words: it includes a judgment of a negative relation between Allah Almighty and a partner for Him. C) Our statement: (Allah hears whoever praises Him) is a categorical proposition because it includes a judgment by attributing hearing the praise of the praiser to Allah Almighty. D) Our statement: (People do not know the unseen) is a categorical proposition because it includes a judgment by negating knowledge of the unseen from people. It is observed that the first two examples are of the nominal sentence type, while the last two are of the verbal sentence type. The categorical proposition therefore contains an ascribee and an ascription according to the terminology of grammarians, or a judged and a judge according to the terminology of rhetoricians, but in the terminology of logicians the ascribee is the (subject) whether it is a mubtada', a fa'il, or what stands in place of either, and the ascription is the (predicate) whether it is a khabar, a fi'l, or what stands in place of either. The reason for this naming is that the logicians observe that the ascribee is placed first in conception then the ascription is predicated on it, so they named the ascribee 'subject' and the ascription 'predicate', and they named the entire proposition a categorical proposition relative to the meaning of positive or negative predication that runs in it. As for the relation between subject and predicate, it is the copula observed in the mind, while in speech it is indicated by some indicator, either mere verbal juxtaposition, like our saying: (Musa came), or an apparent inflectional vowel, like our saying: (Muhammad came), or a separating pronoun is used to indicate it, which is a non-temporal link, like our saying: (Allah, He is our Lord), or the verb of existence, which is a temporal link, like our saying: (Indeed Allah is All-Knowing, All-Wise).

The Copula: How Subject and Predicate Are Linked
Author categorizes types of copula indicators.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the copula (رابطة) in any Arabic categorical proposition from Islamic texts, recognizing that it can be implicit or explicit in various forms.

Core Idea
The copula is the silent relation in the mind, but in speech it appears through word order, inflection, pronouns, or the verb 'to be'.

The author explains that the connection between subject and predicate is mentally observed as a copula (رابطة). However, in Arabic speech, this relation can be indicated in several ways. In this lesson, we will learn to detect the copula in different types of sentences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the copula as the relation
The copula is the relation of attribution or denial between subject and predicate. It is always present in the mind, even if not said aloud.
2
Identify copula by word order
In a simple nominal sentence like 'Zaid is standing', the juxtaposition of subject and predicate indicates the copula. In Arabic, 'Zaidun qa'imun' uses case endings to show the relation.
Example: جاء موسى (Musa came) – the word order alone shows the copula.
3
Identify copula by inflection
In 'Muhammad came' (جاء محمد), the damma on Muhammad indicates the subject and thus the copula is shown by inflection.
4
Identify copula by a separating pronoun
In 'Allah, He is our Lord' (الله هو ربنا), the pronoun 'هو' acts as a non-temporal link.
This is called ضمير الفصل.
5
Identify copula by a verb of existence
In 'Indeed Allah is All-Knowing, All-Wise' (إن الله كان عليماً حكيماً), the verb كان is a temporal copula.

Identifying Particular Affirmative and Particular Negative Propositions
Author lists the first two of eight types of categorical propositions with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any categorical proposition as particular affirmative or particular negative by checking subject definiteness and predicate relation.

Core Idea
Particular propositions have a definite subject; their quality depends on affirmation or denial of the predicate.

In this lesson, we learn the first two of eight types of categorical propositions: particular affirmative (makhsusa mujiba) and particular negative (makhsusa saliba). The author gives clear definitions and examples from Islamic sources. Understanding these will help you analyze logical statements in Islamic sciences like fiqh and tafsir.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
Look at the subject of the proposition. Is it a definite individual or group (e.g., 'Muhammad', 'the four Caliphs', 'Bilal')? If yes, the proposition is particular in quantity.
A definite subject refers to a specific entity, not a universal quantification like 'every' or 'all'.
2
Check the relation
Determine whether the predicate is affirmed or denied of the subject. Look for negation words like 'ليس' (not) or 'لا' (no).
Affirmation means the predicate is asserted; negation means it is denied.
3
Classify the proposition
Combine your findings: if subject is definite and relation is affirmative, it is particular affirmative. If subject is definite and relation is negative, it is particular negative.
Match with the Arabic terms: 'مخصوصة موجبة' for affirmative, 'مخصوصة سالبة' for negative.
4
Verify with examples
Check the examples given: 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' is particular affirmative; 'Musaylima is not a messenger' is particular negative. Note that the subject can be singular or plural as long as it is definite.
The examples show that particular propositions can refer to individuals or groups (e.g., 'the four Rightly-Guided Caliphs').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام الحملية :
وبالرجوع إلى أقسام القضايا باعتبار الكم والكيف معاً نلاحظ أن القضية الحملية تنقسم إلى ثمانية أقسام، وفيما يلي بيانها مع أمثلتها :
١ - مخصوصة موجبة :
وهي التي يكون موضوعها معيناً كما سبق وتكون النسبة فيها موجبة،
مثل : (محمد رسول الله - يؤذن بلال بليل - روى الشيخان صحاح الأحاديث -
الخلفاء الراشدون الأربعة خيرة أصحاب رسول الله - الحرمان الشريفان تشد
الرحال إليهما) .
هذه الأمثلة هي من قسم القضايا الحملية المخصوصة الموجبة .
٢ - مخصوصة سالبة :
وهي التي يكون موضوعها معيناً وتكون النسبة فيها سالبة، مثل :
(مسيلمة ليس برسول - لا ينفع الحجر الأسود ولا يضر - وما أنت بتابع قبلتهم
- ذلك الكتاب لا ريب فيه) .
Divisions of the categorical proposition: Returning to the divisions of propositions in terms of quantity and quality together, we observe that the categorical proposition is divided into eight divisions. Below is their explanation with examples: 1 - Particular affirmative: It is that whose subject is definite as previously stated and the relation in it is affirmative, such as: 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah', 'Bilal calls the adhan at night', 'Al-Shaykhayn narrated the authentic hadiths', 'The four Rightly-Guided Caliphs are the best of the companions of the Messenger of Allah', 'The two noble sanctuaries have their journeys intensified'. These examples are from the division of particular affirmative categorical propositions. 2 - Particular negative: It is that whose subject is definite and the relation in it is negative, such as: 'Musaylima is not a messenger', 'The Black Stone neither benefits nor harms', 'And you are not a follower of their qibla', 'That book has no doubt in it'.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage begins the classification of categorical propositions by quantity and quality. The author states that when considering both quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) together, categorical propositions fall into eight types. Here, only the first two are given: particular affirmative (makhsusa mujiba) and particular negative (makhsusa saliba). A particular proposition has a definite subject — that is, it refers to a specific individual or group (like 'Muhammad' or 'Musaylima'). The particular affirmative is exemplified by statements such as 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah', where the subject 'Muhammad' is a particular person and the predicate is affirmed of him. The particular negative is exemplified by 'Musaylima is not a messenger', where the same type of definite subject has a predicate negated of it. The examples given are all from Islamic sources: names of prophets, companions, and Islamic concepts. This classification is essential for understanding the structure of arguments in logic.

Key Terms

Identifying Universal Negative, Particular Affirmative, and Particular Negative Propositions
Author defines universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative propositions with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify categorical propositions as universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative based on their quantifier and relation.

Core Idea
The quantifier (universal/particular) and relation (affirmative/negative) together determine the proposition type

In Islamic logic, categorical propositions are classified by two features: the quantifier covering the subject and the quality of the relation. The author introduces three types: universal negative (no S is P), particular affirmative (some S is P), and particular negative (some S is not P). Understanding these distinctions is essential for analyzing arguments in the Islamic sciences, especially when interpreting verses with universal or particular scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantifier word
Look for words that indicate universality (e.g., 'لا شيء', 'كل', 'ما من') or particularity (e.g., 'بعض', 'فريق', 'منهم'). These determine whether the proposition is universal or particular.
Universal quantifiers often appear as negation words or 'every' / 'all' in Arabic.
2
Determine the relation
Check if the proposition affirms or denies the predicate of the subject. Affirmative uses 'is' (like 'هو' or implied), negative uses 'ليس' or 'لا' or 'ما'.
Some negative markers may be embedded in the quantifier, e.g., 'لا شيء' combines universal quantifier and negation.
3
Classify the proposition
Combine the quantifier type (universal or particular) with the relation type (affirmative or negative) to classify as universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative.
The three types covered here correspond to E, I, and O in traditional logic.
4
Test with examples
Apply the classification to the Qur'anic examples given. For instance, 'لا شريك لله' is universal negative; 'بعض الحيوان إنسان' is particular affirmative; 'بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان' is particular negative.
Practice ensures you can recognize these in real texts.

٤ - كلية سالبة : وهي التي يكون موضوعها كلياً مسوراً بسور كلي وتكون النسبة فيها سالبة، مثل: (لا شيء من الحجر بإنسان ـ لا شريك لله ـ ما اتخذ الله من ولد ـ ولم يكن له صاحبة ـ لا تأخذه سنةٌ ولا نوم ـ لا غالب لكم اليوم من الناس). ٥ - جزئية موجبة : وهي التي يكون موضوعها كلياً مسوراً بسور جزئي وتكون النسبة فيها موجبة، مثل: (بعض الحيوان إنسان ـ فريق في الجنة ـ وفريق في السعير ـ ورفع بعضكم فوق بعض درجات ـ فمنهم من يمشي على بطنه ـ ومنهم من يمشي على رجلين ـ ومنهم من يمشي على أربع ـ ومن أهل الكتاب من إن تأمنه بقنطار يؤده إليك ـ ومن الناس من يتخذ من دون الله أنداداً ـ وقليل من عبادي الشكور). ٦ - جزئية سالبة : وهي التي يكون موضوعها كلياً مسوراً بسور جزئي وتكون النسبة فيها سالبة، مثل: (بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان ـ ولكن أكثر الناس لا يشكرون ـ وما بعضهم بتابع قبلة بعض ـ ومنهم من إن تأمنه بدينار لا يؤده إليك «أي : بعض أهل الكتاب لا أمانة له» ـ وما أكثر الناس ولو حرصت بمؤمنين).

Commentary
This passage defines three types of categorical propositions: universal negative (كلية سالبة), particular affirmative (جزئية موجبة), and particular negative (جزئية سالبة). Each definition specifies the subject ('موضوع) being universal, the quantifier (سور) being universal or particular, and the relation (نسبة) being negative or affirmative. The author provides multiple examples from the Qur'an and common Arabic usage to illustrate each type. For the universal negative, examples include 'Nothing of stone is human' and divine attributes like 'There is no partner to Allah.' For the particular affirmative, examples include 'Some animals are human' and Qur'anic verses about groups in Paradise and Hell. For the particular negative, examples include 'Some animals are not human' and 'But most people are not grateful.' The examples demonstrate how the quantifier 'some' (بعض) or its equivalents operate in natural language.

Indefinite Propositions: Why They Equal Particulars
Author defines indefinite affirmative and negative propositions and explains their equivalence to particulars due to 'al' for genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify indefinite propositions (مهملة) and explain why they are logically equivalent to particular propositions based on the function of the definite article 'al' for genus.

Core Idea
A universal subject without a quantifier is indefinite and equals a particular, because 'al' often indicates genus, not all individuals.

In this passage, the author defines two types of categorical propositions that lack an explicit quantifier: indefinite affirmative and indefinite negative. You have learned previously about universal and particular propositions. The key insight here is that when the subject includes the definite article but no quantifier, the logical force is particular, not universal. This is due to the linguistic principle that 'al' can indicate the genus rather than universal coverage. Understanding this prevents misinterpretation of many Qur'anic verses and everyday statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and quantifier status
Look at the subject term of the proposition. Does it have an explicit quantifier like 'كل' (all) or 'بعض' (some)? If not, it is indefinite (مهملة).
2
Check the definite article 'al'
Determine whether the definite article 'al' on the subject indicates the genus (للجنس) or universal coverage (للاستغراق). The passage says in indefinite propositions, 'al' is for the genus.
If 'al' were for universal coverage, the proposition would be universal, but context often shows it is not.
3
Apply the equivalence rule
Since 'al' is for genus, the proposition does not claim all individuals. Therefore, an indefinite affirmative is equivalent to a particular affirmative (بعض), and an indefinite negative is equivalent to a particular negative (بعض لا).
Example: 'Man eats beef' → 'Some men eat beef'.
4
Test with Qur'anic examples
The author cites verses like ﴿ وقوم نوح لما كذبوا الرسل أغرقناهم ﴾ and ﴿ لا يسأم الإنسان من دعاء الخير ﴾. Interpret these as indefinite (particular) propositions based on the rule.
Check why reading them as universal would contradict context or common sense.
5
Distinguish from universal
If 'al' were for universal, the proposition would apply to every individual. For instance, 'Men are stronger than women' as universal would be false because some men are not stronger. The indefinite reading ('some men') avoids falsity.
This semantic test confirms the 'al' is for genus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٧ - مهملة موجبة : وهي التي يكون موضوعها كلياً غير مقترن لا بسور كلي ولا بسور جزئي، وتكون النسبة فيها موجبة، وهي كما سبق في قوة الجزئية الموجبة، مثل: (الإنسان يأكل لحوم البقر «فهي في قوة : بعض الإنسان يأكل لحوم البقر» ـ الرجال أقوى على الحرب من النساء «فهي في قوة : بعض الرجال أقوى على الحرب من النساء» ـ سيقول السفهاء من الناس ما ولاهم عن قبلتهم التي كانوا عليها ـ إن الناس قد جمعوا لكم) ولا بد من ملاحظة أن (أل) في الرجال وفي السفهاء وفي الناس هي للجنس لا للاستغراق، ولذلك فهي لا تفيد العموم، فالقضية إذن مهملة من السور فهي في قوة الجزئية، ومثل قوله تعالى: ﴿ وقوم نوح لما كذبوا الرسل أغرقناهم ﴾. ٨ - مهملة سالبة : وهي التي يكون موضوعها كلياً غير مقترن لا بسور كلي ولا بسور جزئي، وتكون النسبة فيها سالبة، وهي كما سبق في قوة الجزئية السالبة، مثل: (الناس لا يركبون البقر فهذه القضية المهملة في قوة قولنا : بعض الناس لا يركبون البقر - لا يغرق القادرون على السباحة فهي في قوة قولنا : بعض القادرين على السباحة لا يغرقون وذلك لأن (أل) في الناس وفي القادرين ليست للاستغراق، فالقضية إذن مهملة من السور فهي في قوة الجزئية. ومثل قوله تعالى : ﴿ لا يسأم الإنسان من دعاء الخير ﴾.
7 - Indefinite affirmative: It is that whose subject is universal, not connected to a universal quantifier nor a particular quantifier, and the relation in it is affirmative, and it is, as mentioned before, in the force of the particular affirmative, like: (Man eats beef - so it is in force: Some men eat beef - Men are stronger in war than women - so it is in force: Some men are stronger in war than women - The fools among the people will say: What has turned them from their qiblah which they used to face - Indeed the people have gathered against you) and it is necessary to note that (al) in men (al-rijal) and in the fools (al-sufaha') and in the people (al-nas) is for the genus, not for universal coverage, and therefore it does not indicate generality, so the proposition is indefinite regarding the quantifier, thus it is in force of the particular, and similar is the saying of Allah the Exalted: {And the people of Noah when they denied the messengers, We drowned them}. 8 - Indefinite negative: It is that whose subject is universal, not connected to a universal quantifier nor a particular quantifier, and the relation in it is negative, and it is, as mentioned before, in the force of the particular negative, like: (People do not ride cows - so this indefinite proposition is in force of our saying: Some people do not ride cows - Those who can swim do not drown - so it is in force of our saying: Some of those who can swim do not drown, and that is because (al) in people and in those who can swim is not for universal coverage, so the proposition is indefinite regarding the quantifier, thus it is in force of the particular. And similar is the saying of Allah the Exalted: {Man does not tire of supplication for good}).
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage defines two more types of categorical propositions: indefinite affirmative (مهملة موجبة) and indefinite negative (مهملة سالبة). The author explains that these propositions have a universal subject but no explicit quantifier, and that they are equivalent in logical force to the respective particular propositions. This equivalence is justified by noting that the definite article (أل) in expressions like 'al-rijal' (men) or 'al-nas' (people) is for the genus (للجنس), not for universal coverage (للاستغراق). Therefore, such statements do not claim universality; they only refer to some individuals. The author provides examples: 'Man eats beef' means 'Some men eat beef'; 'Men are stronger than women' means 'Some men are stronger'; and similarly for negative examples. Qur'anic verses are cited as support: {And the people of Noah...} and {Man does not tire of supplication for good} are interpreted as particular rather than universal. This clarification is crucial to avoid misunderstanding indefinite propositions as universal.

Defining the Conditional Conjunctive Proposition
Author defines the conditional conjunctive proposition and lists its conditional tools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a conditional conjunctive proposition and distinguish it from a categorical proposition by recognizing its structure and conditional tools.

Core Idea
A conditional proposition judges the connection, not the truth of its parts.

The previous lesson introduced the two-part classification of propositions: categorical and conditional. Now we delve into the first type of conditional: the conjunctive (muttaṣila). The author provides a clear definition and illustrates it with common Arabic conditional particles. Understanding this foundation is essential before we explore how conditional propositions function in logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine whether the proposition is categorical (ḥamliyya) or conditional (sharṭiyya). Categorical propositions assert or deny a predicate of a subject. Conditional propositions assert or deny a connection between two judgments.
The passage explicitly contrasts the conditional with the categorical.
2
Locate the two component judgments
A conditional conjunctive proposition contains two complete judgments (like 'if Zayd comes' and 'then Amr goes'). Identify the antecedent (the part introduced by the conditional tool) and the consequent.
3
Check if the judgment is about the connection
The definition states that the judgment affirms or negates a conditional connection (irtibāṭ sharṭī) between the two component judgments. It is not about the truth of each judgment individually.
For example, 'If the sun rises, then it is day' is true even if the sun is not currently rising; the connection is what matters.
4
Classify as affirmative or negative
If the proposition affirms the conditional connection, it is affirmative (mūjiba). If it negates the connection, it is negative (sāliba). Determine which one based on the wording.
The passage gives this classification explicitly.
5
Recognize the conditional tools
Look for the tools listed: إن، إذا، لولا، كلما، مهما، متى، حيثما, etc. These particles establish the conditional relationship between the two judgments.
The passage ends mid-sentence but lists key tools; additional tools can be found in other lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرح القضية الشرطية المتصلة : ونستطيع أن نستخلص تعريفاً للقضية الشرطية المتصلة من البيان السابق لها فنقول : هي التي يكون الحكم فيها قائماً على إثبات الارتباط الشرطي أو نفيه بين حكم وحكم آخر. أو نقول : هي التي تشتمل على حكم بإثبات أو نفي ارتباط شرطي بين حكم وحكم آخر. فالتي فيها إثبات الارتباط الشرطي موجبة والتي فيها نفي الارتباط الشرطي سالبة . وأدوات الشرط التي تعقد الارتباط كثيرة، منها الأدوات التالية (إذا - لو - مهما - كلما - لولا - إن - متى - حيثما وهكذا إلى سائر الأدوات التي تفيد
Explanation of the conditional conjunctive proposition: And we can derive a definition of the conditional conjunctive proposition from the previous explanation of it, so we say: It is that in which the judgment is based on affirming or negating the conditional connection between one judgment and another judgment. Or we say: It is that which contains a judgment affirming or negating a conditional connection between one judgment and another judgment. So that in which there is affirmation of the conditional connection is affirmative, and that in which there is negation of the conditional connection is negative. And the conditional tools that establish the connection are many, among them the following tools: (if - if (alternative) - whenever - whenever (every time) - were it not - if (conditional) - when - wherever, and so forth to the rest of the tools that indicate...
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage provides a definition of the conditional conjunctive proposition (القضية الشرطية المتصلة). The author explains that it is a proposition where the judgment concerns the affirmation or negation of a conditional connection between two judgments. Two formulations are given: first, that the judgment is 'based on' (قائم) the affirmation/negation of the connection; second, that the proposition 'contains' (تشتمل) a judgment to that effect. The author then notes that such propositions are classified as affirmative if they affirm the connection, and negative if they negate it. Finally, a list of conditional tools (أدوات الشرط) is provided, including common Arabic particles like if (إن, إذا), whenever (كلما, مهما), were it not (لولا), when (متى), wherever (حيثما), etc. These tools serve to establish the conditional relationship between the two component judgments. The lesson sets the stage for understanding how conditional propositions function in logical reasoning.

The Indefinite Affirmative Proposition and the Definite Article for Genus
Explains that the definite article in indefinite affirmative propositions indicates genus, not universality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the definite article 'al-' in a proposition indicates genus rather than universality and classify the proposition as indefinite (muhmala) with particular force.

Core Idea
The definite article 'al-' in indefinite propositions signals genus, not all individuals.

In this lesson, we examine how the definite article 'al-' functions in Arabic logical propositions. You will learn that in indefinite affirmative propositions, 'al-' indicates the genus (jins) rather than universal quantification (istighraq). This distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting propositions in Islamic texts, especially when evaluating arguments in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh. Understanding this nuance will allow you to avoid a common logical fallacy: mistaking a particular claim for a universal one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of the proposition
Look for the word carrying the definite article 'al-' in the proposition. In the passage, the examples are 'الرجال', 'السفهاء', 'الناس'.
The subject is the term that bears the definite article.
2
Determine the function of 'al-'
Check if 'al-' indicates the entire genus (all individuals of that genus) or just the genus itself without universal quantification. The passage states it is for the genus.
The passage explicitly says: 'هي للجنس لا للاستغراق'.
3
Assess the quantifier
If 'al-' is for genus, the proposition lacks a universal quantifier (سور). Thus it is indefinite (مهملة).
An indefinite proposition has no explicit quantifier.
4
Convert to logical force
An indefinite affirmative proposition is equivalent in force to a particular affirmative (جزئية موجبة). It affirms something of some individuals of the genus.
The passage says: 'فهي في قوة الجزئية'.
5
Apply to the Quranic example
The verse 'وقوم نوح لما كذبوا الرسل أغرقناهم' affirms drowning of the people of Noah without implying all people. Recognize it as an indefinite affirmative.
The example illustrates the concept in a scriptural context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(أل) في الرجال وفي السفهاء وفي الناس هي للجنس لا للاستغراق، ولذلك فهي لا تفيد العموم، فالقضية إذن مهملة من السور فهي في قوة الجزئية، ومثل قوله تعالى: ﴿ وقوم نوح لما كذبوا الرسل أغرقناهم ﴾.
The definite article (al-) in 'men', 'fools', and 'people' is for the genus, not for universality, and therefore it does not indicate generality. Thus the proposition is indefinite in terms of quantifier, so it is in the force of a particular. An example is His statement, exalted: 'And the people of Noah, when they denied the messengers, We drowned them.'
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage defines the indefinite affirmative proposition (مهملة موجبة). The author begins by analyzing the definite article 'al-' in words like 'men', 'fools', and 'people', stating that it here signifies the genus (جنس) rather than universal quantification (استغراق). Consequently, such a proposition lacks a universal quantifier and is therefore indefinite (مهملة). In logical force, it reduces to a particular affirmative, since it affirms something of some individuals of the genus. The Quranic verse 'And the people of Noah... We drowned them' is cited as an example: it affirms drowning of the people of Noah without implying all people in general, so it is an indefinite affirmative. This clarification guards against the misconception that every 'al-' is universal.

The Indefinite Negative Proposition: Definition and Logical Force
Defines the indefinite negative proposition and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an indefinite negative proposition and determine its logical equivalence as a particular negative.

Core Idea
An indefinite negative proposition is equivalent to a particular negative, not a universal.

In classical logic, propositions are classified by quantity (universal/particular/indefinite) and quality (affirmative/negative). The indefinite negative is often misunderstood as universal. This passage clarifies its true logical force.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject term
Look for the subject noun. In the examples, 'people' and 'those able to swim' are universal terms (kullī) but used without a quantifier.
The subject is a generic term, not a specific group.
2
Check for quantifier
See if the subject is preceded by a universal quantifier like كل or a particular quantifier like بعض. In an indefinite proposition, no quantifier is present.
The absence of quantifier makes it indefinite.
3
Determine the copula
If the proposition is negative (contains لا or ما), then it is a negative proposition.
The negative copula is clear in the Arabic.
4
Convert to particular negative
Because the subject is generic without quantifier, the proposition is logically equivalent to a particular negative: 'Some [subject] are not [predicate].'
This is a key logical insight: indefinite = particular in force.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مهملة سالبة: وهي التي يكون موضوعها كلياً غير مقترن لا بسور كلي ولا بسور جزئي، وتكون النسبة فيها سالبة، وهي كما سبق في قوة الجزئية السالبة، مثل: (الناس لا يركبون البقر) فهذه القضية المهملة في قوة قولنا : بعض الناس لا يركبون البقر - لا يغرق القادرون على السباحة فهي في قوة قولنا : بعض القادرين على السباحة لا يغرقون وذلك لأن (أل) في الناس وفي القادرين ليست للاستغراق، فالقضية إذن مهملة من السور فهي في قوة الجزئية. ومثل قوله تعالى : ﴿ لا يسأم الإنسان من دعاء الخير ﴾.
Indefinite negative: It is that whose subject is universal not combined with either a universal quantifier or a particular quantifier, and the relation is negative. It is as previously mentioned in the force of a particular negative. Example: 'People do not ride cows.' So this indefinite proposition is in the force of our saying: 'Some people do not ride cows.' 'Those able to swim do not drown' is in the force of our saying: 'Some of those able to swim do not drown.' That is because 'al-' in 'people' and 'those able to swim' is not for universality. Thus the proposition is indefinite in terms of quantifier, so it is in the force of a particular. And like His statement, exalted: 'Man does not tire of supplicating for good.'
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage defines the indefinite negative proposition (مهملة سالبة). The author states three conditions: the subject is a universal term, it is not accompanied by any quantifier (neither universal nor particular), and the relation (predicate) is negative. Such a proposition is logically equivalent to a particular negative. Two examples are given: 'People do not ride cows' and 'Those able to swim do not drown.' In each, the 'al-' indicates the genus, not universality, so the proposition does not assert that all people or all capable swimmers lack the predicate, only some. The Quranic verse 'Man does not tire of supplicating for good' also illustrates this: it is not universal, but indefinite. This demonstrates how the absence of quantifiers affects logical force.

C) All men tire of supplicating for good.
D) Some men tire of supplicating for good.

Key Takeaway
An indefinite negative proposition, lacking a quantifier, is logically equivalent to a particular negative, not a universal negative.

Propositions

Quantifiers of Conjunctive Conditionals
Author lists the four quantifiers of conjunctive conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify the four quantifiers (aswār) of conjunctive conditional propositions.

Core Idea
The quantifier of a conditional determines its scope: universal or particular, affirmative or negative.

Just as categorical propositions have quantifiers like 'every' and 'some', conjunctive conditionals have their own quantifiers. The author lists four specific Arabic phrases that signal the universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. Mastering these quantifiers is crucial for interpreting conditional statements in Islamic logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four types of quantifiers from categoricals
Categorical propositions have universal affirmative (A), universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), particular negative (O). Conditionals have the same four combinations.
The quantifier applies to the conditional link, not the parts.
2
Learn the universal affirmative quantifier
The passage states that universal affirmative uses words like 'kullamā', 'mā', 'haythumā', 'matā' that convey generality. Example: 'Kullamā awqadū nāran lil-ḥarbi aṭfa'ahā Allāh' (Whenever they kindle a fire for war, Allah extinguishes it).
These tools imply that the conditional holds for every instance.
3
Learn the universal negative quantifier
Universal negative combines 'laysa al-battata' (not at all) with a non-universal conditional tool like 'idhā' or 'law'. Example: 'Laysa al-battata idhā kāna al-insānu maqṭū‘a al-yadayni fahuwa ghayru kātib' (Not at all if a person has his hands cut off, then he is a writer).
This denies the conditional link universally.
4
Learn the particular quantifiers
Particular affirmative uses 'qad yakūnu idhā' or 'rubamā yakūnu idhā' (it may be that if...). Particular negative uses 'qad lā yakūnu idhā', 'rubamā lā yakūnu idhā', or 'laysa kullamā'.
'Laysa kullamā' means 'not every time', indicating a particular negative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الارتباط الشرطي بين قضيتين، مما هو معروف في اللغة، سواء أكانت الأداة عاملة عند النحاة أو غير عاملة، والمهم عند المنطقي هو تأدية المعنى بأي تعبير كان.
وأسوار الشرطية المتصلة هي كما يلي:
١ - سور الكلية الموجبة، يكون بكل أداة تفيد العموم، مثل: (كلما - مهما - حيثما - متى).
٢ - سور الكلية السالبة يكون بالجمع بين: (ليس البتة) وبين أداة من أدوات الشرط التي لا تفيد العموم مثل: (ليس البتة إذا).
٣ - سور الجزئية الموجبة يكون بعبارة: (قد يكون إذا) أو (ربما يكون إذا) أو نحو ذلك.
٤ - سور الجزئية السالبة يكون بعبارة: (قد لا يكون إذا) أو (ربما لا يكون إذا) أو نحو ذلك، ويكون بالجمع بين أداة من أدوات النفي وبين أداة من أدوات الشرط التي تفيد العموم، مثل: (ليس كلما).
وظاهر أن القضية الشرطية المتصلة تشتمل على جزأين:
١ - الجزء الأول منهما هو الحكم المعلق به.
٢ - الجزء الثاني منهما هو الحكم المتعلق.
والتعليق إما أن يكون مثبتاً فتكون القضية موجبة، وإما أن يكون سالباً (منفياً) فتكون القضية سالبة.
وقد اصطلح المناطقة على أن يسموا الجزء الأول (مقدماً) ويسموا الجزء الثاني (تالياً).
وفيما يلي تصوير جدولي للشرطية المتصلة مطبق على الأمثلة:
أداة السلب أداة الربط الشرطي المقدم تابع أداة الربط التالي
إذا (كانت الشمس طالعة) فـ (النهار موجود)
لو (كان فيهما آلهة إلا الله) لـ (فسدتا)
كلما (أوقدوا ناراً للحرب) (أطفأها الله)
وإذا (قرىء عليهم القرآن) (لا يسجدون)
موجبات
ليس البتة كلما (كان الحيوان ذا جناحين) فـ (هو يطير في الجو)
ليس البتة إذا (كان العدد فرداً) فـ (هو قابل للقسمة بمتساويين من غير كسر)
ليس البتة متى (كان الإنسان مقطوع اليدين) فـ (هو غير كاتب)
سالبات
من خلال الأمثلة نلاحظ أن أداة الشرط هي التي عقدت الاتصال بين المقدم والتالي، وأن الحكم الموجب الذي تشتمل عليه القضية الشرطية المتصلة هو إثبات ربط حكم بحكم آخر، وأن الحكم السالب الذي تشتمل عليه هو نفي هذا الربط، سواء أكان الحكم المربوط حكماً موجباً أو سالباً، وسواء أكان الحكم المربوط به حكماً موجباً أو سالباً أيضاً، فالإيجاب والسلب في القضية الشرطية المتصلة يلاحظ فيه الربط الشرطي، وأما المقدم الذي هو في الأصل قضية حملية فنضعه في القضية الشرطية ضمن قوس، مهما كان وضعه السابق موجباً أو سالباً، وكذلك التالي الذي هو في الأصل قضية حملية فإننا نضعه أيضاً في القضية الشرطية ضمن قوس، مهما كان وضعه السابق موجباً أو سالباً، ثم يأتي التعليق الشرطي بينهما، فإذا كان تعليقاً موجباً اكتفي بمجرد أداة الشرط، وإذا كان المراد سلب التعليق أضيف قبل أداة الشرط ما يدل على نفي وجود الارتباط بين المقدم والتالي .
ويستخدم لذلك أي تعبير كلامي توافق عليه قواعد اللغة .
The conditional connection between two propositions, as is known in language, whether the tool is operative according to grammarians or not operative, and what matters to the logician is conveying the meaning in any expression it may be. The quantifiers of the conjunctive conditional are as follows: 1 - The universal affirmative quantifier is by every tool that conveys generality, such as: (kullamā, mā, haythumā, matā). 2 - The universal negative quantifier is by combining (laysa al-battata) with a tool of condition that does not convey generality, such as: (laysa al-battata idhā). 3 - The particular affirmative quantifier is by the phrase: (qad yakūnu idhā) or (rubamā yakūnu idhā) or the like. 4 - The particular negative quantifier is by the phrase: (qad lā yakūnu idhā) or (rubamā lā yakūnu idhā) or the like, and it is by combining a tool of negation with a tool of condition that conveys generality, such as: (laysa kullamā). It is apparent that the conjunctive conditional proposition contains two parts: 1 - The first part is the ruling to which something is attached. 2 - The second part is the attached ruling. The attachment is either affirmative, so the proposition is affirmative, or negative (negated), so the proposition is negative. The logicians have agreed to call the first part 'antecedent' (muqaddam) and the second part 'consequent' (tālī). Below is a table depiction of the conjunctive conditional applied to examples: [table] From the examples we note that the conditional tool is what establishes the connection between the antecedent and consequent, and that the affirmative ruling contained in the conjunctive conditional is the affirmation of linking one ruling to another, and the negative ruling contained in it is the negation of this linking, whether the linked ruling is affirmative or negative, and whether the ruling to which it is linked is also affirmative or negative. Thus affirmation and negation in the conjunctive conditional are observed in the conditional link. As for the antecedent, which is originally a categorical proposition, we place it in the conditional proposition within brackets, whatever its previous status affirmative or negative; and likewise the consequent, which is originally a categorical proposition, we also place it in the conditional proposition within brackets, whatever its previous status affirmative or negative. Then the conditional attachment comes between them. If it is an affirmative attachment, the mere conditional tool suffices; and if one intends the negation of the attachment, he adds before the conditional tool what indicates the negation of the existence of the connection between antecedent and consequent. Any verbal expression that the rules of language agree upon is used for that.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage classifies and explains the structure of conjunctive conditional propositions (al-shartiyya al-muttasila). It begins by defining the conditional link as a connection between two propositions, emphasizing that the logician cares only about the meaning, not grammatical details. Then it lists the quantifiers (aswār) for conditionals: universal affirmative (e.g., kullamā), universal negative (laysa al-battata + non-universal tool), particular affirmative (qad yakūnu idhā), and particular negative (e.g., laysa kullamā). The author then identifies the two parts of a conditional: the ruling to which something is attached (later called muqaddam) and the attached ruling (later called tālī). The attachment itself can be affirmative (making the conditional affirmative) or negative (making it negative). Logicians term these 'antecedent' and 'consequent'. A table illustrates with examples: some from natural phenomena (sun shining implies day exists) and from the Qur'an ('If there were in them gods besides Allah, they would have corrupted' and 'Whenever they kindle a fire for war, Allah extinguishes it' – here the affirmation is on the link, not the parts). The author explains that in an affirmative conditional, the mere conditional tool (like 'if' or 'whenever') suffices; in a negative conditional, a negation word (like 'laysa al-battata') is placed before the tool. Importantly, the antecedent and consequent themselves are categorical propositions placed in brackets regardless of their internal quality; the affirmation or negation is solely on the conditional link between them. Finally, any linguistic expression agreed upon by grammar rules can be used for this purpose. An Islamic example of a universal affirmative conditional: 'Whenever prayer is established, it restrains from immorality' (inna al-salata tanhā ‘an al-fahshā’ – but that's categorical; better: 'If the prayer is established, it prevents immorality' – though not a perfect conditional. A better Islamic example from the table: 'Whenever they kindle a fire for war, Allah extinguishes it' (Qur'an 5:64) illustrates a universal affirmative conditional. For universal negative: 'Not whenever an animal has wings does it fly in the air' – this is a logical example but can be replaced with an Islamic one: 'Not if a person is poor is he necessarily ungrateful' – but the text's own examples are fine. The commentary should stick to the text's own examples.

Propositions

Parts of a Conditional: Antecedent (Muqaddam) and Consequent (Tālī)
Author defines the two parts of conjunctive conditionals and the affirmative/negative nature of the link.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the antecedent and consequent in a conjunctive conditional and determine whether the conditional link is affirmative or negative.

Core Idea
The truth of a conditional depends on the link between its two parts, not on the truth of the parts themselves.

Every conjunctive conditional consists of an antecedent (muqaddam) and a consequent (tālī) connected by a conditional tool. The author explains that the antecedent is the ruling to which something is attached, and the consequent is the attached ruling. The attachment itself can be affirmative or negative, making the conditional affirmative or negative regardless of the internal quality of the parts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent (muqaddam)
The antecedent is the first part of the conditional, the ruling to which something is attached. In 'If the sun is shining, then the day exists', the antecedent is 'the sun is shining'.
The antecedent is a categorical proposition placed in brackets.
2
Identify the consequent (tālī)
The consequent is the second part, the attached ruling. In the same example, 'the day exists' is the consequent.
Like the antecedent, it is a categorical proposition.
3
Determine the quality of the conditional link
Look at the tool: if only a conditional tool (like 'if', 'whenever') appears, the link is affirmative (positive). If a negation word like 'laysa al-battata' precedes the tool, the link is negative. Example: 'Not if a person has his hands cut off, then he is a writer' is a universal negative conditional.
The negation applies to the connection, not to the parts.
4
Practice with Islamic examples
From the passage: 'If there were in them gods besides Allah, they would have corrupted' – antecedent: 'there were in them gods besides Allah', consequent: 'they would have corrupted', link affirmative. 'Not whenever an animal has wings does it fly in the air' – link is negative.
These examples illustrate that the conditional's truth value depends solely on the link.

The author explains that in an affirmative conditional, the mere conditional tool (like 'if' or 'whenever') suffices; in a negative conditional, a negation word (like 'laysa al-battata') is placed before the tool. Importantly, the antecedent and consequent themselves are categorical propositions placed in brackets regardless of their internal quality; the affirmation or negation is solely on the conditional link between them. Finally, any linguistic expression agreed upon by grammar rules can be used for this purpose. An Islamic example of a universal affirmative conditional: 'Whenever prayer is established, it restrains from immorality' (inna al-salata tanhā ‘an al-fahshā’ – but that's categorical; better: 'If the prayer is established, it prevents immorality' – though not a perfect conditional. A better Islamic example from the table: 'Whenever they kindle a fire for war, Allah extinguishes it' (Qur'an 5:64) illustrates a universal affirmative conditional. For universal negative: 'Not whenever an animal has wings does it fly in the air' – this is a logical example but can be replaced with an Islamic one: 'Not if a person is poor is he necessarily ungrateful' – but the text's own examples are fine. The commentary should stick to the text's own examples.

A conjunctive conditional is composed of an antecedent, a consequent, and a link; the truth lies in the link, not the parts.

The Conjunctive Conditional: Structure and Types
Author defines the conditional proposition, explains positive and negative types, and provides Quranic examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the antecedent, consequent, and linking type (positive/negative) of a conjunctive conditional proposition, and construct a valid example from Islamic sources.

Core Idea
The conjunctive conditional links two judgments — its truth depends on the linking, not on the truth of its parts.

This lesson introduces the conjunctive conditional proposition (shartiyya muttasila), a fundamental tool in Islamic logic for expressing conditional relationships. You will learn its components (antecedent, consequent, conditional tool) and how to distinguish between positive and negative conditionals. The passage also clarifies that the judgment applies to the linking itself, not to the individual judgments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three components
In any conjunctive conditional, locate the conditional tool (e.g., 'if', 'whenever'), the antecedent (the part before the tool), and the consequent (the part after). In the example 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists', the tool is 'if', antecedent is 'the sun is shining', consequent is 'daytime exists'.
The antecedent and consequent are originally categorical propositions and are placed in parentheses in logical notation.
2
Determine the type of linking (positive or negative)
Check whether the proposition affirms or denies the connection between antecedent and consequent. If it affirms (e.g., 'if A then B'), it is positive. If it negates (e.g., 'not (if A then B)' or 'it is not the case that whenever A then B'), it is negative. In the passage, 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists' is positive; 'Not at all: whenever an animal has wings, it flies in the air' is negative.
The negation applies to the linking, not to the antecedent or consequent.
3
Verify the judgment scope
Understand that the judgment in a conditional is about the existence or non-existence of the conditional link, not about the truth of the antecedent or consequent individually. For example, 'If the number is odd, then it is divisible into two equal parts' is false because the linking does not hold, even though both parts may be true or false in other contexts.
This nuance distinguishes conditionals from categorical propositions.
4
Construct your own conditional
Choose a conditional relationship from Islamic knowledge (e.g., from fiqh or kalam). Write a positive conditional by selecting an appropriate tool (in, law, kullama, idha), and ensure the antecedent and consequent are complete categorical propositions. Then test whether the linking holds logically.
Example: 'If a person performs wudu, then he is purified.' This affirms the link.

بين المقدم والتالي. ويستخدم لذلك أي تعبير كلامي توافق عليه قواعد اللغة. وظاهر أن سبب تسمية هذه القضية (شرطية متصلة) وجود التعليق الشرطي فيها على وجه الاتصال كما رأينا، لا على وجه الانفصال والتعاند كما عَلَيْهِمُ الْقُرْءَانُ لَا يَسْجُدُونَ ۩ ﴾. هذه قضية شرطية متصلة، لأنها اشتملت على حكم إيجابي بتعليق استنكاف الكافرين عن السجود لله على وجود المقتضي له، وهو قراءة القرآن عليهم المشتمل على دلائل ربوبية الله وألوهيته والأمر بالسجود له وعبادته وحده. ولما كان الارتباط الشرطي فيها ارتباطاً إيجابياً كانت القضية موجبة. وظاهر في هذا المثال أنه لا يفهم منه أنهم يسجدون لله إذا لم يقرأ عليهم القرآن، إذ هو أمر مسكوت عنه، ولكن يفهم عقلاً أنهم لا يسجدون من باب أولى. لأنهم إذا لم يسجدوا مع وجود الدلائل القرآنية فكيف يسجدون إذا لم توجد. ب ـ وقول الله تعالى في سورة (المائدة ٥): ﴿ كُلَّمَا أَوْقَدُوا نَارًا لِّلْحَرْبِ أَطْفَأَهَا اللَّهُ ﴾. قضية شرطية متصلة، لأنها اشتملت على حكم بتعليق إطفاء الله لنار الحرب على إيقاد الكافرين لها. والارتباط الشرطي فيها ارتباط إيجابي بأداة من أدوات السور الكلي، فالقضية إذن كلية موجبة. جـ ـ وقول الله تعالى في سورة (الأنبياء ٢١): ﴿ لَوْ كَانَ فِيهِمَا ءَالِهَةٌ إِلَّا اللَّهُ لَفَسَدَتَا ﴾. قضية شرطية متصلة، لأنها اشتملت على حكم بوجود اللزوم بين تعدد
Tool of negation, tool of connection, conditional, antecedent, follower, tool of connection, consequent. If (the sun is shining) then (daytime exists). If (there were gods besides Allah) then (they would have corrupted). Whenever (they kindled a fire for war) (Allah extinguished it). And when (the Quran is recited to them) (they do not prostrate). Positives. Not at all (whenever (the animal has wings) then (it flies in the air)). Not at all (if (the number is odd) then (it is divisible into two equal parts without fraction)). Not at all (whenever (a person has his hands cut off) then (he is not a writer)). Negatives. From the examples we note that the condition tool is what established the connection between the antecedent and consequent, and that the positive judgment contained in the conditional proposition is the affirmation of linking a judgment to another judgment, and the negative judgment contained in it is the negation of this linking, whether the linked judgment is positive or negative, and whether the judgment linked to it is also positive or negative. So affirmation and negation in the conditional proposition are observed in the conditional linking. As for the antecedent, which originally is a categorical proposition, we place it in the conditional proposition within parentheses, whatever its previous status was positive or negative. Similarly, the consequent, which originally is a categorical proposition, we also place it within parentheses in the conditional proposition, whatever its previous status was positive or negative. Then the conditional linking comes between them. If it is an affirmative linking, it suffices with just the condition tool; if the intent is to negate the linking, something is added before the condition tool that indicates the negation of the existence of the connection between the antecedent and consequent. For this, any verbal expression agreed upon by the rules of language is used. It is apparent that the reason for naming this proposition 'conditional conjunctive' is the existence of the conditional linking in it in a conjunctive manner, as we saw, not in a disjunctive and contradictory manner as in the disjunctive conditional which will be explained later. With this previous explanation, the features of the conjunctive conditional proposition have become completely clear to us. General examples of the conjunctive conditional: A - The saying of Allah, the Exalted, in Surah Al-Inshiqaq (84): 'And when the Quran is recited to them, they do not prostrate.' This is a conjunctive conditional proposition, because it includes an affirmative judgment linking the disbelievers' abstention from prostration to Allah upon the existence of its motivating factor, which is the recitation of the Quran to them containing proofs of Allah's lordship and divinity and the command to prostrate to Him and worship Him alone. Since the conditional linking in it is affirmative, the proposition is positive. It is apparent in this example that it is not understood that they prostrate to Allah if the Quran is not recited to them, for that is a matter not mentioned, but it is rationally understood that they do not prostrate a fortiori, because if they do not prostrate with the existence of Quranic proofs, how would they prostrate if they do not exist? B - The saying of Allah, the Exalted, in Surah Al-Ma'idah (5): 'Whenever they kindled a fire for war, Allah extinguished it.' A conjunctive conditional proposition, because it includes a judgment linking Allah's extinguishing of the fire of war upon the disbelievers' kindling it. The conditional linking in it is an affirmative linking with a tool of universal quantifier, so the proposition is thus universal affirmative. C - The saying of Allah, the Exalted, in Surah Al-Anbiya' (21): 'If there had been in them gods besides Allah, they would have corrupted.' A conjunctive conditional proposition, because it includes a judgment of the existence of entailment between multiplicity
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage defines the conjunctive conditional proposition (al-qadiyya al-shartiyya al-muttasila) and explains its structure and types. The author begins by listing the components: the tool of negation (adatu al-salb), the tool of connection (adatu al-rabṭ), the conditional (sharṭi), the antecedent (muqaddam), the follower (tabi‘), etc. Then several examples are given in both positive and negative forms, using natural language and a standard logical notation with parentheses. From the examples, the author draws the key insight: the conditional tool establishes the connection between antecedent and consequent. The judgment in a conditional is about the link, not about the truth of the components. A positive conditional affirms the link (e.g., 'If the sun is shining, daytime exists'), while a negative conditional denies the link (e.g., 'Not at all: if the number is odd, it is divisible into two equal parts'). Importantly, the antecedent and consequent are themselves categorical propositions that are placed in parentheses regardless of their original polarity. The linking is then expressed by the conditional tool; for affirmative linking, only the tool is used; for negative linking, a negation is prefixed to the tool. Any linguistic expression conforming to grammar may be used. The author clarifies the naming: it is called 'conjunctive' because the linking is in a conjunctive manner (ittisal), as opposed to the disjunctive conditional which involves separation (infisal) and opposition. Then three Quranic examples are analyzed. Example A (84:21) shows a positive conditional where the antecedent is the recitation of the Quran and the consequent is the disbelievers' failure to prostrate. The author notes that it does not imply that they would prostrate if the Quran were not recited; it is logically a fortiori. Example B (5:64) is a universal affirmative conditional due to the universal quantifier 'kullama'. Example C (21:22) asserts the necessary entailment (luzum) between multiplicity of gods and corruption, which is a key theological argument for tawhid.

Key Terms
شرطية متصلة (shartiyya muttasila): A proposition that links two judgments via a condition in a conjunctive manner, where the antecedent and consequent are connected by a conditional tool. [Note: No significant shift; this is the standard definition across classical and modern Mantiq.]
المقدم (al-muqaddam): The antecedent in a conditional proposition; it is the part that comes before the conditional tool, originally a categorical proposition placed in parentheses. [Note: Stable term; in earlier texts it is also called al-muqaddam.]
التالي (al-tali): The consequent in a conditional proposition; it is the part that follows the conditional tool, originally a categorical proposition placed in parentheses. [Note: Stable term.]
أداة الشرط (adatu al-shart): The conditional tool (e.g., 'if', 'whenever') that expresses the linking between antecedent and consequent. [Note: No significant shift.]
الإيجاب والسلب في القضية الشرطية (al-ijab wa al-salb fi al-qadiyya al-shartiyya): In a conditional, affirmation or negation applies to the linking (taliq) between antecedent and consequent, not to the truth of the parts themselves. [Note: This nuance is sometimes missed by beginners who think it applies to the components.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) A negative conjunctive conditional (salba muttasila).
B) A positive conjunctive conditional (mujaba muttasila).

Key Takeaway
The conjunctive conditional proposition asserts or denies a linking between two categorical judgments, not the truth of the judgments themselves.
